Kafalah — Surety

12 min
Invalidity of Conditional Release from Suretyship
Commentary on a ruling about conditional release from suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a conditional release from suretyship is invalid in the Hanafi school and identify the exceptions for customary conditions.

Core Idea
Conditional release from suretyship is invalid; the suretyship remains valid and the condition is null.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks a nuanced ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: the conditional release (ta‘līq al-barā'a) from suretyship is invalid. The commentary navigates an intra-Hanafi dispute: some hold that because the creditor only has a right to claim, the release is a pure waiver valid conditionally, while the preferred view treats the release as a transfer (tamlik) that cannot be conditional. The author clarifies that it is the conditional release itself that is void, not the condition, so the suretyship endures and the creditor may still claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views on conditional release
Recognize that one view (from al-Hidayah) treats release as a pure waiver (isqat) like divorce, allowing conditional validity; the other view (preferred) treats it as tamlik and thus invalid if conditional.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
Analyze the reasoning behind the two views
Examine why the first view considers the release a waiver: the creditor does not own the debt, only the right to claim. The second view counters that even the right to claim is like a debt because it is a means, and its conditional transfer is invalid.
The key difference hinges on whether the release is tamlik or isqat.
3
Understand the exception for customary conditions
Note that pure conditions (unconventional, no benefit to creditor) are invalid, but customary conditions (e.g., meeting tomorrow) are valid as per an example from al-Idah.
This exception balances the general rule.
4
Clarify the author's resolution: the conditional release is invalid, not the condition
The author emphasizes that it is the release that is suspended (the barā'a mu'allaqa) that is void; the act of conditioning itself is not invalid. Thus the suretyship remains effective.
This resolves the apparent contradiction: the condition is null, the suretyship continues.
5
Confirm with the margin note of Sharh al-Majma'
The margin note confirms: 'the suretyship is valid and the condition is invalid.' This is the relied-upon position.
The author considers this the correct understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبطل تعليق البراءة من الكفالة بالشرط) لما فيه من معنى التمليك كما في سائر البراآت، ويروى لأنه عليه المطالبة دون الدين في الصحيح فكان إسقاطاً محضاً يصح كالطلاق، ولهذا لا يرتد إبراء الكفيل بالرد بخلاف إبراء الأصيل؛ كذا في الهداية. وظاهره ترجيح عدم بطلانه بناء على الصحيح وذكر الزيلعي الشارح أنه لا يصح التعليق أيضاً وإن لم يكن عليه إلا المطالبة لما فيه من تمليك المطالبة وهي كالدين لأنها وسيلة إليه والتمليك لا يقبله. وفي الخانية : لو قال للكفيل أخرجتك عن الكفالة فقال الكفيل لا أخرج لم يصر خارجاً ا هـ. فثبت أن إبراء الكفيل أيضاً يرتد بالرد. وفي المعراج : قيل المراد بالشرط الشرط المحض الدي لا منفعة للطالب فيه أصلاً كدخول الدار ومجيء الغد لأنه غير متعارف، أما إذا كان متعارفاً فإنه يجوز كما في تعليق الكفالة لما في الإيضاح : لو كفل بالمال والنفس وقال إن وافيتك غداً فأنا بريء من المال فوافاه غداً يبرأ . من المال، فقد جوز تعليق البراءة عن الكفالة بالشرط فتأمل قول المصنف : (وبطل تعليق البراءة من الكفالة بالشرط أقول : الظاهر أن إضافة تعليق إلى البراءة من إضافة الصفة إلى موصوفها والمعنى: وبطلت البراءة المعلقة بالشرط وإذا بطلت البراءة المذكورة تبقى الكفالة على أصلها فللطالب المطالبة بدليل التعليل فإن البراءة لما كان فيها معنى التمليك لم تصح بالتعليق كما أن التمليك المعلق لا يصح. وليس المراد أن تعليق البراءة باطل لتكون البراءة صحيحة منجزة إذ لو كان كذلك لبطلت الكفالة ولما التعليل فإن البراءة من الكفالة فيها معنى التمليك والتمليك المعلق بالشرط غير صحيح، وأما نفس التعليق فليس فيه معنى التمليك فتعين أن الذي بطل هو البراءة المعلقة لا نفس تعليقها، وحينئذ فتبقى الكفالة صحيحة على أصلها تأمل. ثم رأيت في هامش نسختي شرح المجمع وهي نسخة قديمة مكتوبة على نسخة شارحه بخط بعض العلماء مكتوباً على الهامش عند قول المتن (ولا : البراءة منها بالشرط ما نصه : معناه أن الكفالة جائزة والشرط باطل .اهـ. وهذا عين ما فهمته والله الحمد. قوله : (فثبت أن إبراء الكفيل أيضاً يرتد بالرد أقول : هذا رد على قول الهداية السابق ولهذا لا يرتد بالرد لكن يمكن أن يقال إن ما في الخانية مبني على خلاف الصحيح تأمل. وقدمنا قبل صح تعليق
His statement: (And the conditional release from suretyship is invalid) because it contains an element of transfer (tamlik) as in all other releases. And it is related that the correct view is that he only has the right to claim, not the debt itself, so it is a pure waiver valid like divorce; hence the surety's release is not retracted by rejection, unlike the release of the principal debtor. Thus in al-Hidayah. Its apparent meaning is a preference for its non-invalidity based on the sound view. And al-Zayla'i the commentator mentioned that conditional release is also invalid, even if he only has the right to claim, because it involves transferring the right to claim, which is like a debt — a means to it — and transfer does not accept it. And in al-Khaniyya: If he said to the surety, 'I have removed you from the suretyship,' and the surety said, 'I do not accept the removal,' he is not removed. It is established that the surety's release is also retracted by rejection. And in al-Mi'raj: It was said that the intended meaning of 'condition' is a pure condition that holds no benefit whatsoever for the creditor, such as entering a house or the coming of tomorrow, because it is unconventional. But if it is conventional, it is permissible, as in conditional suretyship. For it is stated in al-Idah: If he guaranteed for both property and person and said, 'If I meet you tomorrow, I am released from the property,' and he meets him tomorrow, he becomes released from the property. So he permitted conditional release from suretyship. So reflect on the author's statement: (And the conditional release from suretyship is invalid). I say: The apparent meaning is that the addition of 'conditional' to 'release' is an adjective, so the meaning is: the release that is conditional is invalid. If that release is invalid, the suretyship remains as it was, so the creditor can claim, as per the reasoning. Because the release contains an element of transfer, it is not valid when conditional, just as a conditional transfer is not valid. It is not intended that the conditional release itself is invalid so that the release becomes valid and immediate, because then the suretyship would be invalid, and there would be no reasoning. Rather, the release that is conditional is what is invalid, not the condition itself. Thus the suretyship remains valid. Reflect. Then I saw in the margin of my copy of Sharh al-Majma' — an old copy written on the copy of its commentator by hand of some scholar — at the text's statement 'and not: the release from it by condition,' the following text: 'Its meaning is that the suretyship is valid and the condition is invalid.' This is exactly what I understood, praise be to Allah. His statement: (It is established that the surety's release is also retracted by rejection). I say: This is a response to the previous statement of al-Hidayah that it is not retracted by rejection, but it can be said that what is in al-Khaniyya is based on a view contrary to the sound one. Reflect. And we have presented before the validity of conditional... [incomplete]
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn discussing the invalidity of a conditional release from suretyship. The author begins by explaining the matn's statement: 'And the conditional release from suretyship is invalid.' He provides the reasoning: release contains an element of transfer (tamlik), like transferring the right to claim, and such transfers are not valid when suspended on a condition. He notes that according to another narration, the correct view is that the creditor only has the right to claim (mutalaba) and not the debt itself, so the release is a pure waiver (isqat) and thus valid even conditionally, like divorce. This would imply that the conditional release is valid, but the author clarifies that the apparent meaning of al-Hidayah seems to prefer non-invalidity (i.e., validity) based on that narration. However, al-Zayla'i rejects that and says conditional release is invalid even if the creditor only has the right to claim, because that right is like a debt, and its transfer cannot be conditional.

The author then cites al-Khaniyya: if the creditor says 'I have removed you from suretyship' and the surety rejects it, the removal is not effective, proving that the surety's release can be retracted by rejection, contrary to what al-Hidayah suggested. Next, he references al-Mi'raj, which distinguishes between pure conditions (e.g., entering a house, coming of tomorrow) that hold no benefit for the creditor — these are unconventional and invalid — and customary conditions, which are permissible. An example from al-Idah shows that if a surety says 'If I meet you tomorrow, I am released from the property guarantee,' and he meets him, the release is valid. This indicates that customary conditional releases are allowed.

Finally, the author clarifies the correct understanding: it is the conditional release (the suspended release) that is invalid, not the act of conditioning itself. So the suretyship remains valid and the creditor can still claim. The margin note from Sharh al-Majma' confirms this: the suretyship is valid and the condition is invalid. The passage ends with a note about the retraction by rejection, observing that the ruling in al-Khaniyya may be based on a weaker view, but students should reflect on it.

Key Terms
تعليق البراءة (ta‘līq al-barā'a): Suspending the release from suretyship on the occurrence of a future condition.
تمليك (tamlīk): Transfer of ownership or of a right. The release from suretyship is considered to involve tamlik of the right to claim, which cannot be conditional.
إسقاط (isqāt): Dropping or waiving a right. A pure waiver (isqat) like divorce can be conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, what happens to the suretyship if the creditor says to the surety, 'I release you from the guarantee if tomorrow comes'?
A) The suretyship is void and the surety is free.
B) The conditional release is valid, so the surety is freed upon the coming of tomorrow.
C) The conditional release is invalid, so the suretyship remains, and the creditor can claim.
D) The suretyship becomes suspended until the condition is fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
The conditional release from suretyship is invalid because it involves tamlik; only the suretyship remains valid, and the condition is disregarded except for customary conditions.

No Document Written Upon Discharge in Suretyship
Opening ruling of the chapter on suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a document is not written upon discharge (ibra') in suretyship and explain the customary basis for this rule.

Core Idea
Writing a document upon discharge would wrongly imply receipt; custom only attaches that meaning to documents in other contexts.

In the chapter on suretyship, the Hanafi jurists establish a key principle regarding documentation: when a creditor discharges a guarantor or debtor, no written document (sikk) is drawn up. This ruling is not arbitrary—it is rooted in customary practice ('urf). Understanding this distinction helps avoid legal confusion between a discharge and an acknowledgment of payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the discharge (ibra') from a suretyship obligation. The ruling is negative: no written document (sikk) is produced for this discharge.
The ruling is specific to the discharge, not to the original suretyship contract.
2
Understand the customary basis
In Hanafi law, writing a document in financial transactions is customarily treated as an acknowledgment of receipt (iqrar bil-qabd). This custom gives legal effect to the document as proof of payment.
Custom ('urf) is a recognized source of law in Hanafi fiqh when not conflicting with primary texts.
3
Apply the ruling to discharge
Since discharge is a gratuitous release of a debt, not a receipt of payment, the customary link between writing and acknowledgment does not apply. Hence, no document is written.
If a document were written, it could be misinterpreted as an admission that the debt was actually paid, which contradicts the nature of ibra'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة. بالإبراء لا يكتب عليه الصك فجعلت الكتابة إقراراً بالقبض عرفاً ولا عرف عند الإبراء؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
Book of Suretyship. Upon discharge, a document is not written for it; for writing is made an acknowledgment of receipt by custom, and there is no custom at the time of discharge. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This is a ruling statement opening the chapter on suretyship. The author states that when a creditor discharges a guarantor (or a debtor), no written document (صك) is drawn up for the discharge. The reason is that in customary practice, writing a document is considered an acknowledgment that the amount was received (إقرار بالقبض). However, for discharge itself, there is no such custom (عرف) that would make the writing an admission of receipt. So the absence of a document does not harm the discharge. This is supported by a reference to Fath al-Qadir. This ruling is a basic principle regarding the documentation of discharges in suretyship transactions.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Discharge or release from a debt or obligation voluntarily granted by the creditor
الصك (al-sikk): A written document or deed that serves as evidence of a transaction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a document not written upon discharge (ibra') in suretyship according to this passage?
A) Because writing a document is considered an acknowledgment of receipt by custom, which is not the intended meaning in discharge.
B) Because discharge is not a valid legal act.
C) Because the creditor is not permitted to write anything.
D) Because the book of suretyship does not discuss documents.

Key Takeaway
A discharge in suretyship requires no written document because custom treats writing as acknowledgment of receipt, which is inapplicable to a mere release.

Discharge from Claim vs. Discharge from Wealth: Admission Status
Intra-madhhab dispute on whether a defendant's statement of discharge from a lawsuit is an admission of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a defendant's claim of discharge from the lawsuit and discharge from the debt, and state the Hanafi ruling on each as admission.

Core Idea
Discharge from a claim is not admission; discharge from wealth is.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of a defendant's statement that the plaintiff has discharged him from a claim. The core issue is whether such a statement amounts to an admission (iqrar) of the underlying debt. Early Hanafi jurists (متقدمون) held it is not, because a claim may be false. Some later jurists (متأخرون) analogized it to discharge from wealth, which is an admission. Understanding this distinction is crucial for judges and muftis in evaluating evidence and avoiding unjust liability based on ambiguous wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement in dispute
The defendant says: 'The plaintiff discharged me from this claim.' Determine what exactly is being claimed – the claim itself (دعوى) or the underlying wealth (مال).
The wording matters: 'discharge from the claim' vs. 'discharge from the wealth.'
2
State the two positions
Early jurists: Not an admission. Some later jurists: It is an admission, by analogy to discharge from wealth.
Both views are within the Hanafi school; the author selects the early view as more correct.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each position
Early view: A claim may be baseless (باطل), so discharge from a claim does not affirm the debt. Later view: Discharge from wealth necessarily implies wealth existed; discharge from a claim implies the claim had a valid basis yielding a debt.
The key distinction: certainty of wealth vs. uncertainty of a claim.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Example: In court, defendant says 'He discharged me from this claim.' Under the early view, the plaintiff must prove the debt. Under the later view, the statement alone may establish liability.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is the burden of proof.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
The author (and al-Bazzaziyya) declare the early view as 'more correct' (أصح). For fatwa, discharge from a claim is not an admission.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف المتأخرون فيما إذا قال المدعى عليه أبرأني المدعي من الدعوى التي يدعي علي؛ منهم من قال هو إقرار بالمال كما لو قال أبرأني من المال الذي ادعاه، ومنهم من قال لا يكون إقراراً لأن الدعوى تكون بحق وباطل؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي البزازية من الدعوى: دعوى البراءة عن الدعوى لا يكون إقراراً بالدعوى عند المتقدمين وخالفهم المتأخرون، ودعوى البراءة عن المال إقرار وقول المتقدمين أصح اهـ.
And the later jurists differed regarding when the defendant says, 'The plaintiff discharged me from the claim which he makes against me.' Some said it is an admission of the wealth (debt), just as if he said 'He discharged me from the wealth which he claimed.' Others said it is not an admission because a claim may be based on right or falsehood. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Bazzaziyya from 'al-da'wa': claiming discharge from the claim is not an admission of the claim according to the early jurists, and the later jurists opposed them. And claiming discharge from wealth is an admission, and the statement of the early jurists is more correct. End.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the early jurists (متقدمون) and the later jurists (متأخرون) concerning a specific scenario: when the defendant in a lawsuit says, 'The plaintiff discharged me from this claim.' The later jurists differed among themselves: some considered this statement to be an admission of the debt (مال) underlying the claim, analogizing it to saying 'He discharged me from the wealth claimed.' Others said it is not an admission because a claim (دعوى) can be based on right (حق) or falsehood (باطل); thus claiming discharge from the claim does not necessarily imply the existence of a debt. The passage then cites al-Bazzaziyya, which records that the earlier jurists (متقدمون) held that claiming discharge from the claim (دعوى البراءة عن الدعوى) is not an admission of the claim, while the later jurists disagreed. However, claiming discharge from the wealth itself (مال) is an admission. The author sides with the early jurists, deeming their view 'more correct' (أصح). This is significant because the distinction affects how a judge treats such a statement in court: if it is considered an admission, the defendant may be held liable accordingly; if not, the case continues normally.

Key Terms
دعوى البراءة (da'wa al-bara'a): A claim by the defendant that the plaintiff discharged him from the lawsuit or from the debt
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession that establishes a right against the person making it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the earlier Hanafi jurists (متقدمون), if a defendant says 'The plaintiff discharged me from this claim,' does this statement constitute an admission of the debt?
A) Yes, it is an admission of the debt just like discharge from wealth.
B) No, it is not an admission because a claim can be baseless.
C) It depends on whether the claim is valid.
D) The early jurists did not address this issue.

Key Takeaway
Discharge from a claim is not an admission of the debt; discharge from the wealth is – this distinction is decisive in Hanafi procedural law.

Conditional Discharge and Customary Conditions
Expansion and reconciliation of a ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional discharge from suretyship and distinguish between customary and non-customary conditions, providing the legal reasoning and the reconciled position.

Core Idea
Conditional discharge is invalid for non-customary conditions but valid for customary conditions due to customary practice.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator expands on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding conditional discharge from suretyship. The ruling initially appears absolute, but the commentary reveals a nuanced reconciliation: the prohibition applies only to non-customary conditions, while customary conditions are permissible. This lesson will walk you through the legal reasoning and the intra-Hanafi resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is conditional discharge from suretyship (تعليق البراءة من الكفالة). The base ruling states it is invalid (باطل). Identify the hukm level.
The ruling applies to the discharge, not the underlying suretyship.
2
Trace the proof (dalil)
The primary proof is qiyas on other discharges (ibra') which involve tamlik (transfer of ownership). Because tamlik cannot be conditional, conditional discharge is invalid. An alternative view likens it to isqat (forfeiture) which can be conditional like divorce.
The nature of the guarantor's obligation: he is only subject to claim (mutalaba), not the debt itself.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf
There is a conflict between narrations: one says it is invalid, another says it is valid. The commentator uses the distinction between customary (متعارف) and non-customary (غير متعارف) conditions to reconcile.
The narration of invalidity applies to non-customary conditions; the narration of validity applies to customary conditions.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The author resolves by restricting the base text's ruling to non-customary conditions, following al-Mi'raj and al-Idah. This becomes the mufta bihi position.
Customary conditions are valid because they provide benefit to the claimant and are accepted by custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبطل تعليق البراءة من الكفالة بالشرط) لما فيه من معنى التمليك كما في سائر البراآت، ويروى لأنه عليه المطالبة دون الدين في الصحيح فكان إسقاطاً محضاً يصح كالطلاق، ولهذا لا يرتد إبراء الكفيل بالرد بخلاف إبراء الأصيل؛ كذا في الهداية. وظاهره ترجيح عدم بطلانه بناء على الصحيح وذكر الزيلعي الشارح أنه لا يصح التعليق أيضاً وإن لم يكن عليه إلا المطالبة لما فيه من تمليك المطالبة وهي كالدين لأنها وسيلة إليه والتمليك لا يقبله. وفي الخانية : لو قال للكفيل أخرجتك عن الكفالة فقال الكفيل لا أخرج لم يصر خارجاً ا هـ. فثبت أن إبراء الكفيل أيضاً يرتد بالرد. وفي المعراج : قيل المراد بالشرط الشرط المحض الذي لا منفعة للطالب فيه أصلاً كدخول الدار ومجيء الغد لأنه غير متعارف، أما إذا كان متعارفاً فإنه يجوز كما في تعليق الكفالة لما في الإيضاح : لو كفل بالمال والنفس وقال إن وافيتك غداً فأنا بريء من المال فوافاه غداً يبرأ من المال، فقد جوز تعليق البراءة عن الكفالة بالمال. وكذا إذا علق البراءة باستيفاء البعض يجوز أو علق البراءة عن البعض بتعجيل البعض يجوز؛ ذكره في مبسوط شيخ الإسلام. فعلم أن المراد بالشرط الشرط الغير المتعارف، واختلاف الروايتين في صحة التعليق محمول على هذا فرواية عدم الجواز فيما إذا كان غير متعارف ورواية الجواز فيما إذا كان متعارفاً ا هـ . فعلى هذا فكلام المؤلف محمول على شرط غير متعارف.
His saying: (And the conditional discharge from suretyship is invalid) because there is in it the meaning of tamlik (transfer of ownership) as in other discharges. And it is narrated that because the guarantor is subject to claim (mutalaba) but not the debt itself, according to the correct view, so it is a pure forfeiture (isqat) valid like divorce, and therefore the discharge of the guarantor is not rejected by refusal, unlike the discharge of the principal debtor. Thus in al-Hidaya. And its apparent meaning is a preference for its non-invalidity based on the correct view. And al-Zayla'i the commentator mentioned that the conditional form is also invalid, even if the guarantor is only subject to claim, due to the transfer of the right of claim (tamlik al-mutalaba), which is like a debt because it is a means to it, and tamlik does not accept being conditional. And in al-Khaniyya: 'If one said to the guarantor, "I have removed you from the suretyship," and the guarantor said, "I do not leave," he does not become removed.' It is thus established that the discharge of the guarantor is also rejected by refusal. And in al-Mi'raj: 'It is said that what is meant by condition is a bare condition (shart mahd) in which there is no benefit for the claimant at all, such as entering the house or the coming of tomorrow, because it is not customary. As for a customary condition, it is permissible, as in conditional suretyship.' Because in al-Idah: 'If one guaranteed for money and person and said, "If I meet you tomorrow, I am discharged from the money," and he met him tomorrow, he is discharged from the money.' So he allowed conditional discharge from suretyship for money. Similarly, if he conditioned the discharge upon partial collection, it is permissible, or if he conditioned discharge from part upon accelerating part, it is permissible; mentioned in the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. So it is known that what is meant by condition is the non-customary condition, and the difference between the two narrations about the validity of conditional discharge is carried upon this: the narration of impermissibility applies to non-customary conditions, and the narration of permissibility applies to customary conditions. End. So on this basis, the author's statement is applied to a non-customary condition.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This is an expansion probe on the ruling that conditional discharge (تعليق البراءة) from suretyship is invalid (باطل). The author of the sharh (likely Ibn Nujaym) explains the reasoning: there is an element of tamlik (transfer of ownership) in discharge, similar to other discharges (e.g., ibra' from debt), and tamlik cannot be made conditional. However, there is a conflicting narration that the guarantor is only subject to claim (مطالبة) and not the debt itself, so discharge is pure forfeiture (إسقاط محض) similar to divorce, which can be conditional. The commentary notes that the text of al-Hidaya apparently favors the former (invalidity) based on the correct view. Then al-Zayla'i argues that even if pure forfeiture, conditional discharge is invalid because it involves tamlik of the right of claim (تمليك المطالبة), which is like debt and does not accept condition. The passage then cites al-Khaniyya to show that the guarantor's discharge can be rejected (يرتد بالرد), contrary to what al-Hidaya may suggest. This establishes that the discharge of the guarantor is also rejected by refusal. Finally, the commentary reconciles conflicting reports by introducing the distinction: the prohibition applies only to non-customary conditions (شرط غير متعارف) that provide no benefit to the claimant, while customary conditions like meeting tomorrow or partial payment are valid. Thus, the author's ruling is interpreted as applying to non-customary conditions. This is a nuanced discussion showing how later Hanafi jurists harmonized contradictory texts.

Key Terms
تعليق البراءة (ta'liq al-bara'a): Making the discharge from suretyship conditional upon a future event or condition
الشرط الغير متعارف (al-shart ghayr al-muta'arif): A condition that is not customary in such transactions, providing no benefit to the claimant (e.g., entering a house, coming of tomorrow)
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership of a right or property; in this context, the right of claim (mutalaba) is being transferred

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciled position in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which of the following conditional discharges from suretyship for money would be valid?
A) 'If you enter this house, I am discharged.'
B) 'If I meet you tomorrow, I am discharged.'
C) 'I am discharged from the debt if you never ask me to pay.'
D) 'I am discharged if you forgive the debt.'

Key Takeaway
The validity of conditional discharge from suretyship depends on whether the condition is customary; non-customary conditions render the discharge void but the suretyship remains intact.

Three Scenarios of Conditional Discharge from Personal Suretyship
Detailed ruling on conditional discharge from personal suretyship with three scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scenarios of conditional discharge from personal suretyship and state the ruling (valid/invalid) for the discharge and the condition in each.

Core Idea
Conditional discharge from personal suretyship is valid; the condition's validity depends on whether it is tied to financial suretyship.

The previous lesson covered suretyship for money; this lesson turns to personal suretyship (kafala bi al-nafs), where conditional discharge has a detailed three-part ruling. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains three distinct scenarios based on the nature of the condition and the presence of financial suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of suretyship
First, determine whether the suretyship is for the person only (kafala bi al-nafs) or for both person and money (kafala bi al-nafs wa al-mal). The ruling on conditional discharge hinges on this distinction.
Suretyship for money (kafala bi al-mal) was covered earlier; here we focus on personal suretyship.
2
Understand Scenario 1: Discharge valid, condition invalid
When the claimant discharges the guarantor from personal suretyship on condition that the guarantor pays a sum of money (e.g., ten dirhams), the discharge is valid but the condition is invalid. The condition is treated as a forbidden sale of discharge.
The guarantor does not have to pay the money.
3
Understand Scenario 2: Both discharge and condition valid
If the guarantor is also a guarantor for money owed by the principal, and the claimant stipulates that the guarantor pay that money debt in exchange for discharge from personal suretyship, both the discharge and the condition are valid. The payment is for the financial suretyship, not for the discharge itself.
This avoids riba because the payment is for an existing debt.
4
Understand Scenario 3: Both discharge and condition invalid
If the guarantor is only for the person, and the claimant stipulates that the guarantor pay money to the claimant and then recover it from the principal debtor, the entire transaction is void. This is because it amounts to a loan with benefit (riba).
The guarantor does not pay, and the personal suretyship remains.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on sulh
The passage mentions a dispute regarding a settlement (sulh) where the guarantor pays money to be discharged. In one narration (from al-Jami'), the settlement is invalid; in another, the guarantor is discharged. This is a legitimate difference within the Hanafi school.
The mufta bihi position is that the sulh is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراد من الكفالة الكفالة بالمال احترازاً عن كفالة النفس فإنه يصح تعليق البراءة منها على تفصيل مذكور في الخانية قال : إذا علق براءة الكفيل بالنفس بشرط فهو على وجوه ثلاثة : في وجه تجوز البراءة ويبطل الشرط نحو أن يكفل رجل بنفسه رجل فأبرأه الطالب عن الكفالة على أن يعطيه الكفيل عشرة دراهم جازت البراءة ويبطل الشرط، وإن صالح الكفيل المكفول له على مال ليبرئه عن الكفالة لا يصح الصلح، ولا يجب المال على الكفيل ولا يبرأ عن الكفالة في رواية الجامع وإحدى روايتي الحوالة والكفالة وفي رواية أخرى يبرأ عن الكفالة. وفي وجه تجوز البراءة والشرط وصورة ذلك : رجل كفل بنفس رجل وبما عليه من المال فشرط الطالب على الكفيل أن يدفع المال إلى الطالب ويبرئه عن الكفالة بالنفس جازت الكفالة والشرط. وفي وجه لا يجوز كلاهما وصورة ذلك : رجل كفل بنفس رجل خاصة فشرط الطالب على الكفيل أن يدفع إليه المال ويرجع بذلك على المطلوب فإنه يكون باطلاً ا هـ.
And he intended by suretyship the suretyship for money, as a precaution against suretyship for the person, because conditional discharge from it is valid according to a detail mentioned in al-Khaniyya. He said: If he conditions the discharge of the guarantor for the person with a condition, there are three aspects: In one aspect, the discharge is valid and the condition is invalid, such as a man becoming guarantor for the person of another man, and the claimant discharges him from the suretyship on condition that the guarantor gives him ten dirhams – the discharge is valid and the condition is invalid. And if the guarantor settles with the guaranteed person (al-makful lahu) for money to discharge him from the suretyship, the settlement is not valid, the money is not obligatory on the guarantor, and he is not discharged from the suretyship in the narration of al-Jami' and one of the two narrations of al-Hawala wa al-Kafala; and in another narration he is discharged from the suretyship. In another aspect, both the discharge and the condition are valid, and its example: a man became guarantor for the person of a man and for the money owed by him; then the claimant stipulated on the guarantor that he pay the money to the claimant and he discharges him from the suretyship for the person – the suretyship and the condition are valid. In another aspect, both are invalid, and its example: a man became guarantor specifically for the person of a man, then the claimant stipulated on the guarantor that he pay the money and then have recourse for that against the guaranteed person – that is void. End.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains that the previous ruling about invalidity of conditional discharge applies specifically to suretyship for money (كفالة بالمال). For suretyship for the person (كفالة بالنفس), the ruling is detailed in al-Khaniyya with three scenarios. First scenario: if the condition is purely financial and separate from the suretyship (e.g., the claimant discharges the guarantor for a payment of ten dirhams), the discharge is valid but the condition (the payment) is void because it is effectively a forbidden sale of discharge. However, if the guarantor tries to settle (صالح) with the claimant for money to obtain discharge, the settlement is invalid in the majority narration. Second scenario: if the guarantor is also a guarantor for money, and the claimant conditions that the guarantor pay the money and in return discharge him from the personal suretyship, both are valid because the payment is for the money suretyship (which is a debt) and the discharge is for the personal suretyship. Third scenario: if the guarantor is only for the person, and the claimant requires him to pay money and then recover from the original debtor, this is void because it amounts to a loan with benefit, which is usurious. This detail shows the nuanced Hanafi treatment of conditional discharges in different types of suretyship.

Key Terms
كفالة النفس (kafala bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person, meaning the guarantor guarantees the presence of the principal debtor (e.g., in court)
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise where the guarantor pays money to the claimant in exchange for discharge from personal suretyship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does the discharge from personal suretyship and the condition both become valid?
A) When the guarantor is only for the person and agrees to pay money to the claimant.
B) When the guarantor is for both person and money, and the claimant conditions payment of the money debt in exchange for discharging the personal suretyship.
C) When the guarantor settles with the claimant for money to be discharged from personal suretyship.
D) When the condition is customary such as meeting tomorrow.

Key Takeaway
Conditional discharge from personal suretyship is valid, but the condition itself is only valid when tied to an existing financial debt under the same guarantee.

Suretyship for Prescribed Punishment or Retaliation is Invalid
Ruling with proof: suretyship for hadd or qisas is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suretyship for prescribed punishment (hadd) and retaliation (qisas), explain the reason based on the non-applicability of agency in penalties, and distinguish between suretyship for the punishment itself and suretyship for the person liable.

Core Idea
A guarantor cannot bear another's punishment because deterrence requires personal execution.

We often think of suretyship as a financial guarantee, but can you guarantee to undergo punishment on behalf of a criminal? The Hanafi school answers with a clear ruling: such a guarantee is void. In this lesson, we examine the logical and legal basis for this invalidity, grounded in the principle that agency (niyaba) does not apply to penalties. The deterrence objective of punishment necessitates that the punishment be inflicted on the actual offender, not a substitute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What contract is being discussed? What is the hukm (legal classification)? The act is suretyship (kafalah) for a hadd punishment or qisas. The ruling is that it is batil (void).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
What proof does the text provide? The proof is a rational argument (qiyas) based on the principle that agency (niyaba) is not valid in penalties because the intended purpose (deterrence/zajr) cannot be achieved through a substitute.
The dalil is 'aqli (rational), not naqli (textual).
3
Understand the rationale
Why does the objective of deterrence prevent suretyship? If a guarantor undergoes punishment, the actual offender is not deterred. The personal experience of punishment is essential for individual deterrence. Therefore, the obligation cannot be transferred.
This is a teleological argument (maqasid-based).
4
Note the crucial distinction
The ruling distinguishes between suretyship for the punishment itself (batil) and suretyship for the person liable (ja'iz). The text explicitly states that suretyship for the person (i.e., guaranteeing to produce the offender) is permissible, citing al-Binaya and al-Hidaya.
This distinction is vital for correct application.
5
Consider the cross-madhhab context
Is this ruling agreed upon? The Hanafi position is opposed by some other schools that may allow suretyship for punishment. The Hanafi stance is based on the non-validity of agency in hudud, which is a fundamental principle.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (difference of opinion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والكفالة بحد وقود أي بطل التكفيل بحد وقود لأنه يتعذر إيجابه عليه لعدم جريان النيابة في العقوبة لعدم حصول المقصود منها وهو الزجر. قيد الكفالة بنفس الحد والقود لأن الكفالة بنفس من عليه يجوز؛ وصرح به في البناية وأشار إليه في الهداية. وقدمنا أنه لا يجوز بنفس من عليه في الحدود الخالصة فليراجع في شرح قوله «ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حد وقود
His saying: (And suretyship for a prescribed punishment or retaliation) i.e., the guarantee of a prescribed punishment or retaliation is void because it is impossible to make it obligatory upon the guarantor due to the non-applicability of agency (niyaba) in punishment, as the intended purpose (deterrence) cannot be achieved. He restricted the suretyship to the very punishment and retaliation because suretyship for the person upon whom it is due is permissible; and this was explicitly stated in al-Binaya and alluded to in al-Hidaya. And we have previously stated that it is not permissible for the person upon whom it is due in the case of pure penalties, so refer to the commentary on his statement: 'And he is not compelled to accept suretyship for the person in prescribed punishment or retaliation.'
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof that suretyship for a prescribed punishment (حد) or retaliation (قود) is invalid (باطل). The reason given is that agency (نيابة) does not apply in penal matters (عقوبات) because the objective of punishment is personal deterrence (زجر), which cannot be achieved through a guarantor. However, the author notes a fine point: the invalidity is for suretyship for the punishment itself (i.e., guaranteeing to undergo the punishment on behalf of another). But suretyship for the person of the one who is liable for punishment (e.g., guaranteeing to produce his body) is permissible, as stated in al-Binaya and al-Hidaya. This means you can guarantee that the criminal will appear for punishment, but you cannot guarantee to bear the punishment yourself. The text also references an earlier discussion about the distinction between pure penalties (حدود خالصة) and other rights, where the same rule applies. This is an important distinction in Hanafi law between financial suretyship and penal suretyship.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed prescribed punishment for offenses like theft, adultery, etc.
قود (qawad (or qisas)): Retaliation, i.e., the right of the victim or his heirs to exact equivalent punishment (e.g., life for life)
نيابة (niyaba): Agency; representation of another in legal acts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is suretyship for a prescribed punishment (حد) or retaliation (قود) invalid according to the passage?
A) Because the punishment is too severe to be guaranteed.
B) Because agency (niyaba) does not apply in penalties; the deterrent purpose requires personal execution.
C) Because the guarantor may refuse to undergo punishment.
D) Because the law forbids any type of suretyship for crimes.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for the punishment itself (hadd or qisas) is void; only suretyship for the person liable is permitted in Hanafi law.

Suretyship for Sold Item, Pawn, and Trust is Invalid
Ruling and proof for invalidity of suretyship for three items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition for valid suretyship of specific chattels (that the chattel must be guaranteed by itself) and distinguish between chattels guaranteed by themselves vs. by something else.

Core Idea
For suretyship to be valid, the chattel must be a direct liability on the principal debtor — not covered by another obligation.

In Hanafi law, suretyship (kafalah) for specific chattels is valid only if the chattel is a direct liability on the principal debtor, meaning he cannot be freed except by delivering that specific item or its equivalent. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why suretyship is invalid for three common examples: a sold item before delivery (mabee'), a pawned item (marhun), and a trust item (amanah). The reasoning turns on how the chattel's value is secured — by itself or by something else. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly applying the rules of suretyship in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalid suretyships
The text states that suretyship for a sold item (before delivery to buyer), a pawned item, and a trust item is invalid (batil). List these three examples.
These are the specific items discussed in the passage.
2
Understand the condition for valid suretyship
The passage gives the condition: the chattel must be 'guaranteed by itself' (madmin bi-nafsihi) on the principal debtor, meaning he is personally liable for that specific chattel and cannot be freed except by delivering it or its equivalent.
This condition excludes items whose value is covered by another obligation (e.g., price, debt).
3
Classify the three items
For each of the three items, determine why they fail the condition: sold item before delivery is guaranteed by the price (not by itself), pawned item is guaranteed by the debt, trust item is not guaranteed at all (trustee not liable without negligence).
Use the Hanafi categories: madmin bi-ghayrihi (guaranteed by something else) vs. madmin bi-nafsihi (guaranteed by itself).
4
Apply the condition to valid examples
The passage lists valid examples: a sold item in a defective sale (mabee' bi bay' fasid), property taken on inspection (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'), and usurped property (maghsoub). Confirm each is madmin bi-nafsihi.
These items are direct liabilities on the possessor.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction from al-Khaniyya
The author addresses a potential contrary opinion from al-Khaniyya, explaining that it refers to rescission (iqala) of the suretyship contract, not a valid discharge. This preserves the ruling's consistency.
This shows how later scholars reconcile apparent conflicts within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومبيع ومرهون (وأمانة أي وبطلت الكفالة بالمبيع والمرهون. أما الكفالة بالمبيع للمشتري فلأن المبيع مضمون بغيره وهو الثمن، والكفالة بالأعيان المضمونة وإن كانت تصح عندنا خلافاً للشافعي لكن إنما تصح بالأعيان المضمونة بنفسها كالمبيع بيعاً فاسداً والمقبوض على سوم الشراء أو المغصوب لا بما كان مضموناً بغيره كالمبيع والمرهون لأن من شرطها أن يكون المكفول مضموناً على الأصيل بحيث لا يمكنه أن يخرج عنه إلا بدفعه أو دفع مثله، والمبيع قبل القبض ليس بمضمون على البائع حتى لو هلك لا يجب عليه شيء. ورقتين الجواب بأن ما في الخانية إقالة لعقد الكفالة لا إبراء.
His saying: And a sold item, pawned item, and trust, i.e., the suretyship for the sold item and pawned item is invalid. As for suretyship for the sold item for the buyer, it is because the sold item is guaranteed by something else, namely the price. And suretyship for guaranteed chattels, although it is valid according to us (Hanafis) contrary to al-Shafi'i, is only valid for chattels that are guaranteed by themselves, such as a sold item in a defective sale, property taken on inspection (sawm al-shira'), or usurped property; not for property that is guaranteed by something else, like the sold item (before delivery) or pawned item. Because one of its conditions is that the guaranteed thing (makful) be held as a liability on the principal debtor such that he cannot be freed from it except by delivering it or its equivalent. And the sold item before delivery is not guaranteed on the seller; even if it perishes, nothing is incumbent upon him. And the answer in two papers: that what is in al-Khaniyya is a rescission (iqala) of the suretyship contract, not a discharge (ibra').
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling that suretyship for a sold item (before delivery to the buyer), a pawned item, and a trust item (amanah) is invalid (باطل). The reasoning focuses on the nature of the 'guaranteedness' (damān) of the object. In Hanafi law, suretyship is valid for objects that are guaranteed by themselves (أعيان مضمونة بنفسها), meaning the principal debtor is personally liable for that specific item and cannot be released except by delivering it or its equivalent. Examples are a sold item in a defective sale (بيع فاسد), property taken on inspection (مقبوض على سوم الشراء), or usurped property (مغصوب). In contrast, a sold item before delivery (مبيع قبل القبض) is not guaranteed on the seller; if it perishes, the seller owes nothing to the buyer. Similarly, a pawned item (مرهون) is guaranteed by the debt (the loan), not by itself. A trust item (أمانة) is not guaranteed at all because the trustee is not liable for loss without negligence. So suretyship for such items is invalid because the condition of the item being a liability on the principal is not met. The passage also references a fatwa from al-Khaniyya that might seem contradictory, and the author clarifies that what al-Khaniyya means is a rescission (إقالة) of the suretyship contract, not a valid discharge. This shows the precise conditions required for a valid suretyship for specific chattels.

Key Terms
مضمون بغيره (madmun bi-ghayrihi): Guaranteed by something else; the object's value is covered by another obligation (e.g., price for sold item, debt for pawn)
أعيان مضمونة بنفسها (a'yan madmuna bi-nafsiha): Chattels that are guaranteed by themselves, meaning the principal debtor is personally liable for that specific item (e.g., usurped property)
إقالة (iqala): Rescission or cancellation of a contract; here, rescission of the suretyship contract, not a discharge of the obligation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following chattels can be the subject of a valid suretyship in the Hanafi school?
A) A sold item before delivery.
B) A pawned item.
C) Property taken on inspection (مقبوض على سوم الشراء).
D) An item held as a trust (أمانة).

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for a specific chattel is invalid unless the chattel is a direct liability (madmin bi-nafsihi) on the principal debtor.

The Validity of Kafalah for Trust Property: Refuting Sarakhsi
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah for trust property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafalah for trust property and explain why Sarakhsi's objection is refuted using textual and rational evidence.

Core Idea
Kafalah to deliver a trust object is valid; the surety's obligation is to bring and hand over the object.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a surety can guarantee delivery of an object that is held in trust, such as a deposit (wadi'ah) or loan (ariyah). The author refutes Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi's view that such kafalah is invalid, citing authoritative texts from Zahir al-Riwayah and providing a logical argument about the nature of the obligation to return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Focus on trust property (amana) like deposit, loan, lease, etc. The question is whether kafalah to deliver such objects is valid.
The apparent problem is that trust property is not guaranteed (mamun) on the principal, so how can a surety be liable?
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The kafalah here is not for liability (daman) but for delivery (taslim). The surety must bring and hand over the object.
The benefit is to compel the surety to produce the object, not to compensate for loss.
3
Examine Sarakhsi's objection
Sarakhsi considered kafalah for delivering a loan (ariyah) invalid, possibly because he viewed the obligation to return as merely vacating (takhliya) rather than delivery.
The passage refutes this by citing explicit texts from al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Mabsut affirming validity.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author argues that the principal is obligated to return upon demand, and this obligation can be transferred to the surety. Even if the obligation is defined as vacating, the surety can be required to bring and vacate.
The passage concludes that the distinction between different trusts is weak.
5
Confirm the relied-upon position
The majority view, supported by Zahir al-Riwayah, is that kafalah to deliver any trust property is valid. This is the mufta bihi.
Sarakhsi's position is not accepted in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحد وقود ومبيع ومرهون وأمانة وصح لو ثمناً ومغصوباً ومقبوضاً على سوم الشراء ومبيعاً وإنما ينفسخ به البيع والمرهون غير مضمون على المرتهن بنفسه وإنما يسقط دينه إذا هلك فلا يمكن إيجاب الضمان على الكفيل وهو ليس بواجب على الأصيل أطلقه فشمل ما إذا ضمن الرهن عن المرتهن للراهن أو عكسه ؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. وأما الأمانة كالوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة والعارية والمستأجر في يد المستأجر فلا يمكن جعلها مضمونة على الكفيل وهي غير مضمونة على الأصيل وقالوا رد الوديعة ليس بواجب على المودع بل الواجب عدم المنع عند طلب المودع فلا يجب على الكفيل تسليمها قيد بالكفالة بالعين لأن الكفالة بتسليمها أمانة أو مضمونة صحيحة وفائدته حينئذ إلزام إحضار العين وتسليمها، ولو عجز بأن مات العبد المبيع أو المستأجر أو الرهن انفسخت الكفالة وزان الكفالة بالنفس سواء. وما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي أن الكفالة بتسليم العارية باطلة باطل فقد نص في الجامع الصغير أن الكفالة بتسليم العارية صحيحة، وكذا في المبسوط. ونص القدوري أنها بتسليم المبيع جائزة، ونص في التحفة على جميع ما أوردناه أن الكفالة بالتسليم صحيحة. والوجه عندي أن لا فرق الثلاث الأول من الوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة وبين العارية بین وما معها من الآمانات إذ لا شك في وجوب الرد عند الطلب. فإن قال الواجب التخلية بينه وبينها لا ردها إليه فنقول : فليكن مثل هذا الواجب على الكفيل وهو أن يحصلها ويخلي بينه وبينها بعد إحضاره إليها ونحن نعني بوجوب الرد ما هو أعم هذا ومن حمل المردود من إليه. قال في الذخيرة : الكفالة بتمكين المودع من الأخذ صحيحة ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. ورده على شمس الأئمة السرخسي مأخوذ من معراج الدراية ويساعده قول الشارح ويجوز في الكل أن يتكفل بتسليم العين مضمونة أو أمانة وقيل : إن كان تسليمه واجباً على الأصيل كالعارية والإجارة جاز وإلا فلا فأفاد أن التفصيل بين أمانة وأمانة ضعيف .
With a defined number, fuel, sold item, mortgaged, trust, and valid if price, usurped, possession on purchase approval, and sold defectively. The contract is rescinded by sale; the mortgaged item is not guaranteed upon the mortgagee by itself; his debt only cancels if it perishes. So it is not possible to impose liability on the surety, and it is not obligatory upon the principal. He made it general, so it includes if he guaranteed the mortgage from the mortgagee to the mortgagor or vice versa; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. As for the trust, like deposit, mudaraba property, partnership, loan, and the leased object in the lessee's hand, it cannot be made guaranteed upon the surety, and it is not guaranteed upon the principal. They said: Returning the deposit is not obligatory upon the depositary; rather the obligation is not to prevent upon demand. So the surety is not obligated to deliver it. This is restricted by kafalah bi al-'ayn, because kafalah to deliver it as trust or guaranteed is valid. Its benefit then is to impose bringing and delivering the object. If unable, e.g., the sold, leased, or mortgaged slave dies, the kafalah is rescinded; its rule is the same as kafalah for the person. What Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned that kafalah to deliver a loan is invalid is false; indeed it is stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that it is valid, and likewise in al-Mabsut. Al-Quduri stated that kafalah to deliver a sold item is valid, and al-Tuhfa states regarding all we mentioned that kafalah for delivery is valid. The opinion according to me is that there is no difference between the first three (deposit, mudaraba property, partnership) and the loan and other trusts, since there is no doubt about the obligation of return upon demand. If it is said: The obligation is to vacate between him and it, not to return it, we say: Let such obligation be upon the surety—to bring it and vacate between him and it after bringing it. By 'obligation of return' we mean what is more general: this and the one who bears the thing returned to whom it belongs. It is stated in al-Dhakhira: Kafalah to enable the depositor to take is valid; as in Fath al-Qadir. His refutation of Sarakhsi is taken from Mi'raj al-Diraya, supported by the commentator's statement: It is permissible in all to guarantee delivery of the object, whether trust or guaranteed. It was said: If its delivery is obligatory upon the principal (like loan and lease) it is permissible; otherwise no. So it shows the distinction between one trust and another is weak.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding kafalah bi al-'ayn. The author first explains that the matn includes various types of property. He then focuses on trust property (amana) like deposit, mudaraba capital, partnership property, loan, and lease. The apparent problem is that these are not guaranteed upon the principal, so how can a surety be liable? However, the author clarifies that kafalah bi al-'ayn to deliver the object is valid, and its benefit is to compel the surety to bring and hand over the object. He then refutes Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi's view that kafalah for delivering a loan is invalid, citing explicit texts from al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Mabsut, al-Quduri, and al-Tuhfa that affirm its validity. The author provides his own reasoning: there is no doubt that the principal is obligated to return the object upon demand (even if the obligation is defined as vacating), so the surety can be obligated similarly. He concludes that the distinction between different types of trusts is weak, and the correct view is the validity of kafalah for delivering any trust property. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the majority and refutes Sarakhsi.

Key Terms
كفالة بالعين (kafālah bi al-ʿayn): A contract of suretyship where the surety guarantees the delivery of a specific object (not a debt).
أمانة (amānah): Property held in trust, such as deposit (wadi'ah), loan (ariyah), or lease (ijarah), where the holder is not liable for loss unless negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is kafalah to deliver a loaned item (ariyah) valid?
A) No, because the borrower is not obligated to return it.
B) Yes, it is valid as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Mabsut.
C) Only if the loan is for a fixed term.
D) Only if the lender requests it.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah to deliver any trust property is valid in the Hanafi school; the surety is obligated to bring and hand over the object.

Kafalah — Surety

Valid Subjects for Kafalah bi al-Ayn: Core Principles
Expansion on matn's list of valid subjects for kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four main subjects for which kafalah bi al-ayn is valid according to the Hanafi school and explain the rationale for each.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid for anything that is a guaranteed debt or a guaranteed object upon the principal.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that kafalah bi al-ayn is valid for price, usurped property, property held on purchase approval, and a defectively sold item. We will explore why each qualifies, along with important branches and exceptions that clarify the scope of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four valid subjects
Locate the phrase 'Wa ṣaḥḥa law thamanan wa maghṣūban wa maqbūḍan ʿalā sawm al-shirāʾ wa mabīʿan fāsidā'. List these four items.
The text also mentions additional valid subjects later, but focus on these four first.
2
Explain the rationale for each
For price: because it is a valid debt (dayn ṣaḥīḥ). For the others: because they are guaranteed upon the principal (maḍmūn ʿalā al-aṣīl) by themselves, i.e., if they perish, their value is owed.
The value (qīmah) is treated like the object itself.
3
Note the exception for a minor's sale
If a restricted minor sells and a person guarantees the price after the minor receives it, the kafalah is invalid because the price is not owed by the minor. If before receipt, it is valid.
This exception is from al-Khaniyyah.
4
Identify additional valid subjects
The passage extends validity to the substitute for blood money (if a slave), dowry, and khulʿ compensation. These are not invalidated by destruction of the object.

قوله : (وصح لو ثمناً ومغصوباً ومقبوضاً علي سوم الشراء ومبيعاً فاسداً) أي صح الضمان لو كان المضمون إلى آخره. أما الثمن فلكونه ديناً صحيحاً مضموناً على المشتري، وأما ما عداه فلكونه مضموناً بنفسه على الأصيل لأنه إذا هلك وجبت قيمته وهي كهو.

بالكفالة بالعين الخ) فرع ذكر في نور العين يرمز الجامع ما نصه رب المتاع لو أخذ من مستعيره أو غاصبه برده كفيلاً صح ولو رد رجع عليه بأجر مثل عمله إذ الكفيل بأمر يرجع بما ضمن وشمل عمله أجر عمله، ولو أخذ به وكيلاً لا كفيلاً لا يجبر على رده لتبرعه بخلاف الكفيل اهـ. قوله: (وما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي إلى قوله باطل أخذه صاحب الفتح من الدراية ولم يلتفت إليه في العناية قال في النهر وفيه نظر لأن شمس الأئمة ليس ممن لم يطلع على الجامع بل لعله اطلع على رواية أقوى من ذلك فاختارها لأن هذا أمر موهوم ومن حفظ حجة على من لم يحفظ . قوله : (والوجه عندي أن لا فرق الخ) رد على التفصيل الآتي المنقول عن الشارح الزيلعي.

وإن كان المضمون مستهلكاً فالمضمون قيمته لما في السراج الوهاج ولو ادعى على رجل أنه غصبه ألف درهم وهو في يده أو في منزله أو ادعى شيئاً يكون ديناً من مكيل أو موزون فضمن له رجل ما ادعى كان على الضامن أن يأتي بذلك الشيء بعينه، فإن لم يأت بذلك الشيء لم يضمن حتى يستحقه المدعي على المدعى عليه، وإن ادعى ألفاً مستهلكة أو كراً مستهلكاً فضمنه رجل فهو ضامن من وإن لم يقم المدعي بينة لأن العين ما دامت باقية فالضمان ينصرف إلى إحضارها ولا ينصرف إلى تسليمها إلا بعد الاستحقاق وإن كانت هالكة فالضمان ينصرف إلى القيمة فصار ضمانه دلالة على الاعتراف بالضمان .اهـ والمقبوض على سوم الشراء إنما يكون من هذا النوع إذا له في الحمل على دابة الغير لأنه لو أعطى دابة من عنده لا يستحق الأجرة لأنه أتى بغير المعقود عليه . قيد بكونها معينة لأنها لو كانت بغير عينها
سمي
ساعته
قوله : (ولو) كفل المشتري بالثمن لغريم ثم استحق المبيع برئ الكفيل الخ) قال في النهر : والفرق بينهما فيما يظهر أنه الاستحقاق تبين أن الثمن غير واجب على المشتري وفي الرد بالعيب مع ونحوه وجب المسقط بعد ما تعلق حق الغريم به فلا يسري عليه قوله (وإن فسد بعد صحته الخ)
His statement: (And valid if price, usurped, possession on purchase approval, and sold defectively.) i.e., the guarantee is valid if the guaranteed item is etc. As for price, because it is a valid debt guaranteed upon the buyer; as for others, because they are guaranteed upon the principal by themselves, for if they perish, their value becomes due and it is like them. (Depending on kafalah bi al-ʿayn, etc.) A branch mentioned in Nur al-ʿAyn summarizing al-Jamiʿ: The owner of goods, if he takes from the borrower or usurper a guarantor for its return, it is valid. If he returns, he recovers from him the wage equivalent of his labor, because the guarantor by order recovers what he guaranteed, and his labor is included. If he takes an agent (wakeel) not a guarantor, he is not compelled to return because of his gratuitous act, unlike the guarantor. His statement: (What Shams al-Aʾimmah al-Sarakhsi mentioned up to his saying 'invalid' was taken by the author of al-Fath from al-Dirayah, and he did not pay attention to al-ʿInayah. He said in al-Nahr: There is consideration because Shams al-Aʾimmah is not one who has not seen al-Jamiʿ; perhaps he saw a stronger transmission and chose it, because this is a weak matter, and one who remembers is proof against one who does not. His statement: (And the opinion according to me that there is no difference etc.) is a refutation of the upcoming distinction transmitted from al-Zaylaʿi. (Defective, and transport of a specific rented animal, and service of a slave hired for service, and without acceptance of the claimant in the session.) Excluded from price is what a restricted minor sold, and a man became guarantor for it, or he guaranteed the inhiram (risk) after the minor received the price—the kafalah is not valid, because he guaranteed what is not guaranteed upon the principal. If he guaranteed the inhiram before the minor received it, it is valid; as in al-Khaniyyah. Among objects for which kafalah is valid are the substitute for blood money if it is a slave, and a man becomes its guarantor—it is valid; if it perishes before delivery, he owes its value. Also the dowry and the substitute for khulʿ, because these are not invalidated by the destruction of the object; as in al-Khaniyyah. If he guarantees the price and the sold item is repossessed (mustahaqq), the guarantor is discharged; likewise if the buyer returns it due to a defect, whether by court order or without, or due to option of inspection or condition. If the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged; if he returns it due to a defect with or without court order, he is not discharged. If he guarantees the dowry for the husband and it subsequently falls entirely before consummation or half before it, the guarantor is discharged from the whole in the first case and from half in the second, as a consequence of the husband's discharge. If he guarantees the price and then the sale is found to be corrupt, the guarantor—if he has paid—may recover from either the seller or the buyer as he wishes; if it became corrupt after validity (e.g., they added a corrupt condition), the recourse is from the buyer against the seller. Its completion is in al-Tatarkhaniyyah here. It is mentioned in the chapter of option of condition that if there is a guarantor for the price and the buyer cancels, but does not return the sold item to the seller, then the seller may demand the price from the guarantor until the buyer returns the sold item. This contradicts his statement here that the guarantor is discharged by the cancellation of the sale due to option of condition and the like; so let it be considered. As for guarantee of the usurped: if the guaranteed item is an existing object, the guarantor is bound to bring it and deliver it, not its value if it perishes; if the guaranteed item is consumed, then what is guaranteed is its value, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. If a man claims that someone usurped a thousand dirhams from him and they are in his hand or house, or claims something that is a debt of measured or weighed items, and another man guarantees for him what he claimed, then the guarantor must produce that very thing. If he does not produce it, he does not become liable until the claimant proves in court that he is entitled to it from the defendant. If he claims a consumed thousand or a consumed kur, and a man guarantees it, he is a guarantor from the moment, even if the claimant does not produce evidence, because as long as the object remains, the guarantee is directed to bringing it, and it is not directed to delivery except after entitlement; if it is destroyed, the guarantee is directed to value. So his guarantee is an indication of admission of liability. And the object held on purchase approval is of this type only if it has a price; otherwise it is a trust, as we have presented in the book of sales. His statement: (And transport of a specific rented animal and service of a slave hired for service) i.e., the kafalah becomes void for transporting a specific animal etc., because if it is specific, the guarantor is unable to deliver it since he has no authority to transport someone else's animal; if he provides another animal, he does not deserve wage because he does what was not contracted. It is restricted to being specific because if it is not specific, it is as in the text. His statement: (And if the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent difference is that repossession shows the price was never owed to the buyer, whereas in the case of return due to defect, the thing that has been nullified was established after the creditor's right attached to it, so it does not extend to him. His statement: (And if it becomes corrupt after its validity, etc.)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn's statement that kafalah bi al-ʿayn is valid for price, usurped property, property held on purchase approval (maqbud ʿala sawm al-shiraʾ), and sold property even if defective (mabiʿ fasid). The commentator explains each: price is a valid debt; the others are guaranteed upon the principal because they are of value. He then discusses branches and exceptions. He notes that if a minor sells, kafalah for the price after he receives it is invalid, but before receipt it is valid. Kafalah is also valid for the substitute of blood money (e.g., a slave) and for dowry and khulʿ compensation. He then distinguishes between cases where the guarantor is discharged: if the sold item is repossessed (mustahaqq), the guarantor is discharged from the price; if the buyer returns the item due to a defect, the guarantor is discharged only if the return is by court order or without (but in a special case where the buyer himself is the guarantor for the price to his creditor, he is not discharged). For usurped property, if the object exists, the guarantor must bring it; if consumed, he is liable for the value. If the guarantee is for an object held on purchase approval, it is considered guaranteed only if the item has a price; otherwise it is a trust. Finally, he notes an apparent inconsistency in the text regarding the effect of the buyer's exercise of an option to cancel, advising scrutiny. The commentary draws from various sources like al-Khaniyyah, al-Tatarkhaniyyah, al-Nahr, and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale; a valid debt guaranteed upon the buyer.
مغصوب (maghsūb): Usurped property; it is guaranteed upon the usurper; kafalah for it is valid.
مقبوض على سوم الشراء (maqbūḍ ʿalā sawm al-shirāʾ): Property taken on approval to purchase; it is treated as a guarantee if it has a price, else as a trust.
كفيل بالدرك (kafīl bi al-darak): Guarantor of indemnity against eviction (risk of repossession).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid subject for kafalah bi al-ayn according to the passage?
A) Price
B) Usurped property
C) A minor's debt after he receives payment
D) Substitute for blood money (slave)

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi al-ayn is valid for any item that is a debt or a guaranteed object upon the principal, with specific exceptions for minors and conditions on discharge.

Discharge of the Guarantor: Repossession, Defects, and Options
Detailed rules on discharge of the guarantor for price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where a guarantor for the price is discharged (repossession vs. defect return) and apply the rule to the buyer-as-guarantor scenario.

Core Idea
A guarantor for the price is discharged upon repossession of the sold item, but not upon return due to defect if the guarantor is the buyer himself.

The passage presents critical distinctions for when a guarantor for the price is released from liability. The key factor is whether the event nullifies the sale retroactively (repossession) or merely terminates it (defect return). We will unpack this difference and address an apparent contradiction in the texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the statement: 'If he guarantees the price and the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged; likewise if he returns it due to a defect, by court order or without, or due to option of inspection or condition.'
This seems to treat repossession and defect return similarly.
2
Examine the exception for the buyer as guarantor
Note the case: 'If the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged; if he returns it due to a defect with or without court order, he is not discharged.'
This is a key exception.
3
Understand the rationale
The passage from al-Nahr explains: repossession shows the price was never owed, while defect return nullifies a right that had already attached to the creditor.
Thus, the guarantor's liability is affected differently.
4
Note the inconsistency on option of condition
The passage mentions a contradiction: earlier it says the guarantor is discharged by cancellation due to option of condition, but another source says he remains liable until the buyer returns the item.
This is left for further research (tata'ammal).
