قوله : (وصح لو ثمناً ومغصوباً ومقبوضاً علي سوم الشراء ومبيعاً فاسداً) أي صح الضمان لو كان المضمون إلى آخره. أما الثمن فلكونه ديناً صحيحاً مضموناً على المشتري، وأما ما عداه فلكونه مضموناً بنفسه على الأصيل لأنه إذا هلك وجبت قيمته وهي كهو.

بالكفالة بالعين الخ) فرع ذكر في نور العين يرمز الجامع ما نصه رب المتاع لو أخذ من مستعيره أو غاصبه برده كفيلاً صح ولو رد رجع عليه بأجر مثل عمله إذ الكفيل بأمر يرجع بما ضمن وشمل عمله أجر عمله، ولو أخذ به وكيلاً لا كفيلاً لا يجبر على رده لتبرعه بخلاف الكفيل اهـ. قوله: (وما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي إلى قوله باطل أخذه صاحب الفتح من الدراية ولم يلتفت إليه في العناية قال في النهر وفيه نظر لأن شمس الأئمة ليس ممن لم يطلع على الجامع بل لعله اطلع على رواية أقوى من ذلك فاختارها لأن هذا أمر موهوم ومن حفظ حجة على من لم يحفظ . قوله : (والوجه عندي أن لا فرق الخ) رد على التفصيل الآتي المنقول عن الشارح الزيلعي.

كتاب الكفالة
۳۸۷
فاسداً وحمل دابة معينة مستأجرة وخدمة عبد استؤجر للخدمة وبلا قبول الطالب في مجلس
ويستثنى من الثمن ما باع به صبي محجور عليه فكفل به رجل أو كفل بالدرك بعدما قبض الصبي الثمن لم تصح الكفالة لكونه كفل بما ليس بمضمون على الأصيل، وإن كفل بالدرك قبل قبض الصبي صحت ؛ كذا في الخانية. ومما تصح به الكفالة من الأعيان بدل الصلح عن الدم لو كان عبداً فكفل به إنسان ،صحت، فإن هلك قبل القبض فعليه قيمته، ومنها المهر وبدل الخلع لأن هذه الأشياء لا تبطل بهلاك العين ؛ كذا في الخانية. لو كفل بالثمن فاستحق المبيع برئ الكفيل، وكذا لو رده بعيب بقضاء أو بغير قضاء أو بخيار رؤية أو شرط، ولو كفل المشتري بالثمن لغريمه ثم استحق المبيع برئ الكفيل، ولو رده بعيب بقضاء أو بغير قضاء لا . ولو كفل بالمهر عنه ثم سقط عنه كله قبل الدخول أو نصفه قبله برئ الكفيل عن الكل في الأول وعن النصف في الثاني حكماً لبراءة الزوج. ولو كفل بالثمن ثم ظهر فساد البيع رجع الكفيل بما دفعه إن شاء على البائع وإن شاء على المشتري وإن فسد بعد صحته بأن ألحقا به شرطاً فاسداً فالرجوع للمشتري على البائع وتمامه في التتارخانية هنا. وذكر في باب خيار الشرط لو كان بالثمن كفيل ففسخ المشتري فلم يرد المبيع إلى البائع فله مطالبة الكفيل بالثمن حتى يرد المشتري المبيع .اهـ. وهو مخالف لقوله هنا إن الكفيل يبرأ بفسخ البيع بخيار الشرط ونحوه فليتأمل. وأما ضمان المغصوب فإن كان المضمون عيناً قائماً فيلزم الضامن إحضارها وتسليمها لا قيمتها إن هلكت، وإن كان المضمون مستهلكاً فالمضمون قيمته لما في السراج الوهاج ولو ادعى على رجل أنه غصبه ألف درهم وهو في يده أو في منزله أو ادعى شيئاً يكون ديناً من مكيل أو موزون فضمن له رجل ما ادعى كان على الضامن أن يأتي بذلك الشيء بعينه، فإن لم يأت بذلك الشيء لم يضمن حتى يستحقه المدعي على المدعى عليه، وإن ادعى ألفاً مستهلكة أو كراً مستهلكاً فضمنه رجل فهو ضامن من وإن لم يقم المدعي بينة لأن العين ما دامت باقية فالضمان ينصرف إلى إحضارها ولا ينصرف إلى تسليمها إلا بعد الاستحقاق وإن كانت هالكة فالضمان ينصرف إلى القيمة فصار ضمانه دلالة على الاعتراف بالضمان .اهـ والمقبوض على سوم الشراء إنما يكون من هذا النوع إذا له ثمن وإلا فهو أمانة كما قدمناه في البيوع قوله : ( وحمل دابة معينة مستأجرة وخدمة عبد استؤجر للخدمة) أي وبطلت الكفالة بحمل دابة إلى آخره لأنها إذا كانت معينة كان الكفيل عاجزاً عن تسليمها لأنه لا ولاية له في الحمل على دابة الغير لأنه لو أعطى دابة من عنده لا يستحق الأجرة لأنه أتى بغير المعقود عليه . قيد بكونها معينة لأنها لو كانت بغير عينها
سمي
ساعته
قوله : (ولو) كفل المشتري بالثمن لغريم ثم استحق المبيع برئ الكفيل الخ) قال في النهر : والفرق بينهما فيما يظهر أنه الاستحقاق تبين أن الثمن غير واجب على المشتري وفي الرد بالعيب مع ونحوه وجب المسقط بعد ما تعلق حق الغريم به فلا يسري عليه قوله (وإن فسد بعد صحته الخ)
His statement: (And valid if price, usurped, possession on purchase approval, and sold defectively.) i.e., the guarantee is valid if the guaranteed item is etc. As for price, because it is a valid debt guaranteed upon the buyer; as for others, because they are guaranteed upon the principal by themselves, for if they perish, their value becomes due and it is like them. (Depending on kafalah bi al-ʿayn, etc.) A branch mentioned in Nur al-ʿAyn summarizing al-Jamiʿ: The owner of goods, if he takes from the borrower or usurper a guarantor for its return, it is valid. If he returns, he recovers from him the wage equivalent of his labor, because the guarantor by order recovers what he guaranteed, and his labor is included. If he takes an agent (wakeel) not a guarantor, he is not compelled to return because of his gratuitous act, unlike the guarantor. His statement: (What Shams al-Aʾimmah al-Sarakhsi mentioned up to his saying 'invalid' was taken by the author of al-Fath from al-Dirayah, and he did not pay attention to al-ʿInayah. He said in al-Nahr: There is consideration because Shams al-Aʾimmah is not one who has not seen al-Jamiʿ; perhaps he saw a stronger transmission and chose it, because this is a weak matter, and one who remembers is proof against one who does not. His statement: (And the opinion according to me that there is no difference etc.) is a refutation of the upcoming distinction transmitted from al-Zaylaʿi. (Defective, and transport of a specific rented animal, and service of a slave hired for service, and without acceptance of the claimant in the session.) Excluded from price is what a restricted minor sold, and a man became guarantor for it, or he guaranteed the inhiram (risk) after the minor received the price—the kafalah is not valid, because he guaranteed what is not guaranteed upon the principal. If he guaranteed the inhiram before the minor received it, it is valid; as in al-Khaniyyah. Among objects for which kafalah is valid are the substitute for blood money if it is a slave, and a man becomes its guarantor—it is valid; if it perishes before delivery, he owes its value. Also the dowry and the substitute for khulʿ, because these are not invalidated by the destruction of the object; as in al-Khaniyyah. If he guarantees the price and the sold item is repossessed (mustahaqq), the guarantor is discharged; likewise if the buyer returns it due to a defect, whether by court order or without, or due to option of inspection or condition. If the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged; if he returns it due to a defect with or without court order, he is not discharged. If he guarantees the dowry for the husband and it subsequently falls entirely before consummation or half before it, the guarantor is discharged from the whole in the first case and from half in the second, as a consequence of the husband's discharge. If he guarantees the price and then the sale is found to be corrupt, the guarantor—if he has paid—may recover from either the seller or the buyer as he wishes; if it became corrupt after validity (e.g., they added a corrupt condition), the recourse is from the buyer against the seller. Its completion is in al-Tatarkhaniyyah here. It is mentioned in the chapter of option of condition that if there is a guarantor for the price and the buyer cancels, but does not return the sold item to the seller, then the seller may demand the price from the guarantor until the buyer returns the sold item. This contradicts his statement here that the guarantor is discharged by the cancellation of the sale due to option of condition and the like; so let it be considered. As for guarantee of the usurped: if the guaranteed item is an existing object, the guarantor is bound to bring it and deliver it, not its value if it perishes; if the guaranteed item is consumed, then what is guaranteed is its value, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. If a man claims that someone usurped a thousand dirhams from him and they are in his hand or house, or claims something that is a debt of measured or weighed items, and another man guarantees for him what he claimed, then the guarantor must produce that very thing. If he does not produce it, he does not become liable until the claimant proves in court that he is entitled to it from the defendant. If he claims a consumed thousand or a consumed kur, and a man guarantees it, he is a guarantor from the moment, even if the claimant does not produce evidence, because as long as the object remains, the guarantee is directed to bringing it, and it is not directed to delivery except after entitlement; if it is destroyed, the guarantee is directed to value. So his guarantee is an indication of admission of liability. And the object held on purchase approval is of this type only if it has a price; otherwise it is a trust, as we have presented in the book of sales. His statement: (And transport of a specific rented animal and service of a slave hired for service) i.e., the kafalah becomes void for transporting a specific animal etc., because if it is specific, the guarantor is unable to deliver it since he has no authority to transport someone else's animal; if he provides another animal, he does not deserve wage because he does what was not contracted. It is restricted to being specific because if it is not specific, it is as in the text. His statement: (And if the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent difference is that repossession shows the price was never owed to the buyer, whereas in the case of return due to defect, the thing that has been nullified was established after the creditor's right attached to it, so it does not extend to him. His statement: (And if it becomes corrupt after its validity, etc.)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn's statement that kafalah bi al-ʿayn is valid for price, usurped property, property held on purchase approval (maqbud ʿala sawm al-shiraʾ), and sold property even if defective (mabiʿ fasid). The commentator explains each: price is a valid debt; the others are guaranteed upon the principal because they are of value. He then discusses branches and exceptions. He notes that if a minor sells, kafalah for the price after he receives it is invalid, but before receipt it is valid. Kafalah is also valid for the substitute of blood money (e.g., a slave) and for dowry and khulʿ compensation. He then distinguishes between cases where the guarantor is discharged: if the sold item is repossessed (mustahaqq), the guarantor is discharged from the price; if the buyer returns the item due to a defect, the guarantor is discharged only if the return is by court order or without (but in a special case where the buyer himself is the guarantor for the price to his creditor, he is not discharged). For usurped property, if the object exists, the guarantor must bring it; if consumed, he is liable for the value. If the guarantee is for an object held on purchase approval, it is considered guaranteed only if the item has a price; otherwise it is a trust. Finally, he notes an apparent inconsistency in the text regarding the effect of the buyer's exercise of an option to cancel, advising scrutiny. The commentary draws from various sources like al-Khaniyyah, al-Tatarkhaniyyah, al-Nahr, and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): Repossession of a sold item by a third party due to superior title.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): Option of condition; a stipulated right to cancel a sale within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the guarantor of the price if the buyer returns the sold item due to a defect, when the guarantor is a third party (not the buyer)?
A) The guarantor is discharged.
B) The guarantor is not discharged.
C) The guarantor is discharged only if the return is by court order.
D) The guarantor remains liable until the seller resells the item.

Key Takeaway
The discharge of a guarantor for the price depends on the cause of sale termination: repossession always discharges, but defect return only discharges a third-party guarantor, not the buyer himself.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Guarantee of Specific Items: Usurped Property, Purchase Approval, and Services
Detailed rules for guarantee of usurped property, purchase approval, and services

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for guaranteeing usurped property (existing vs. consumed), property on purchase approval, and why kafalah for specific services (rented animal, hired slave) is invalid.

Core Idea
For usurped property and similar, the guarantor must bring the object if it exists, pay value if consumed; for specific services, kafalah is invalid due to impossibility of delivery.

This lesson covers the detailed rules for guaranteeing specific physical objects and services. The key is understanding what the guarantor must do when the object exists versus when it is consumed, and why certain services cannot be guaranteed because the guarantor lacks control over the specific item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for usurped (maghsūb) property
Find the passage: if the guaranteed object is an existing ʿayn, the guarantor must bring it; if it is consumed, he must pay the value. This applies regardless of whether the claimant proves the claim.
The text adds details about claims of specific sums.
2
Understand the nature of property on purchase approval (maqbūḍ ʿalā sawm al-shirāʾ)
This item is treated as a guarantee only if it has a price (thaman); otherwise it is a trust (amānah). So the guarantee is valid only if the price is set.
This was discussed earlier in the book of sales.
3
Explain why kafalah for specific services is invalid
The text gives two examples: transport of a specific rented animal, and service of a specific hired slave. The reason: the guarantor cannot force the animal or slave to perform; he lacks authority. If he provides a substitute, he does not fulfill the contract.
It is invalid (bāṭilah) because delivery is impossible.
4
Note the distinction between specific and non-specific services
The invalidity is restricted to when the animal or slave is specified (muʿayyan). If not specified, the guarantor can provide any equivalent, so the guarantee would be valid.
The text explicitly says 'qayyada bi kawnihā muʿayyanah'.

قوله : (وصح لو ثمناً ومغصوباً ومقبوضاً علي سوم الشراء ومبيعاً فاسداً) أي صح الضمان لو كان المضمون إلى آخره. أما الثمن فلكونه ديناً صحيحاً مضموناً على المشتري، وأما ما عداه فلكونه مضموناً بنفسه على الأصيل لأنه إذا هلك وجبت قيمته وهي كهو.

قوله : (ولو) كفل المشتري بالثمن لغريم ثم استحق المبيع برئ الكفيل الخ) قال في النهر : والفرق بينهما فيما يظهر أنه الاستحقاق تبين أن الثمن غير واجب على المشتري وفي الرد بالعيب مع ونحوه وجب المسقط بعد ما تعلق حق الغريم به فلا يسري عليه قوله (وإن فسد بعد صحته الخ)
His statement: (And valid if price, usurped, possession on purchase approval, and sold defectively.) i.e., the guarantee is valid if the guaranteed item is etc. As for price, because it is a valid debt guaranteed upon the buyer; as for others, because they are guaranteed upon the principal by themselves, for if they perish, their value becomes due and it is like them. (Depending on kafalah bi al-ʿayn, etc.) A branch mentioned in Nur al-ʿAyn summarizing al-Jamiʿ: The owner of goods, if he takes from the borrower or usurper a guarantor for its return, it is valid. If he returns, he recovers from him the wage equivalent of his labor, because the guarantor by order recovers what he guaranteed, and his labor is included. If he takes an agent (wakeel) not a guarantor, he is not compelled to return because of his gratuitous act, unlike the guarantor. His statement: (What Shams al-Aʾimmah al-Sarakhsi mentioned up to his saying 'invalid' was taken by the author of al-Fath from al-Dirayah, and he did not pay attention to al-ʿInayah. He said in al-Nahr: There is consideration because Shams al-Aʾimmah is not one who has not seen al-Jamiʿ; perhaps he saw a stronger transmission and chose it, because this is a weak matter, and one who remembers is proof against one who does not. His statement: (And the opinion according to me that there is no difference etc.) is a refutation of the upcoming distinction transmitted from al-Zaylaʿi. (Defective, and transport of a specific rented animal, and service of a slave hired for service, and without acceptance of the claimant in the session.) Excluded from price is what a restricted minor sold, and a man became guarantor for it, or he guaranteed the inhiram (risk) after the minor received the price—the kafalah is not valid, because he guaranteed what is not guaranteed upon the principal. If he guaranteed the inhiram before the minor received it, it is valid; as in al-Khaniyyah. Among objects for which kafalah is valid are the substitute for blood money if it is a slave, and a man becomes its guarantor—it is valid; if it perishes before delivery, he owes its value. Also the dowry and the substitute for khulʿ, because these are not invalidated by the destruction of the object; as in al-Khaniyyah. If he guarantees the price and the sold item is repossessed (mustahaqq), the guarantor is discharged; likewise if the buyer returns it due to a defect, whether by court order or without, or due to option of inspection or condition. If the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged; if he returns it due to a defect with or without court order, he is not discharged. If he guarantees the dowry for the husband and it subsequently falls entirely before consummation or half before it, the guarantor is discharged from the whole in the first case and from half in the second, as a consequence of the husband's discharge. If he guarantees the price and then the sale is found to be corrupt, the guarantor—if he has paid—may recover from either the seller or the buyer as he wishes; if it became corrupt after validity (e.g., they added a corrupt condition), the recourse is from the buyer against the seller. Its completion is in al-Tatarkhaniyyah here. It is mentioned in the chapter of option of condition that if there is a guarantor for the price and the buyer cancels, but does not return the sold item to the seller, then the seller may demand the price from the guarantor until the buyer returns the sold item. This contradicts his statement here that the guarantor is discharged by the cancellation of the sale due to option of condition and the like; so let it be considered. As for guarantee of the usurped: if the guaranteed item is an existing object, the guarantor is bound to bring it and deliver it, not its value if it perishes; if the guaranteed item is consumed, then what is guaranteed is its value, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. If a man claims that someone usurped a thousand dirhams from him and they are in his hand or house, or claims something that is a debt of measured or weighed items, and another man guarantees for him what he claimed, then the guarantor must produce that very thing. If he does not produce it, he does not become liable until the claimant proves in court that he is entitled to it from the defendant. If he claims a consumed thousand or a consumed kur, and a man guarantees it, he is a guarantor from the moment, even if the claimant does not produce evidence, because as long as the object remains, the guarantee is directed to bringing it, and it is not directed to delivery except after entitlement; if it is destroyed, the guarantee is directed to value. So his guarantee is an indication of admission of liability. And the object held on purchase approval is of this type only if it has a price; otherwise it is a trust, as we have presented in the book of sales. His statement: (And transport of a specific rented animal and service of a slave hired for service) i.e., the kafalah becomes void for transporting a specific animal etc., because if it is specific, the guarantor is unable to deliver it since he has no authority to transport someone else's animal; if he provides another animal, he does not deserve wage because he does what was not contracted. It is restricted to being specific because if it is not specific, it is as in the text. His statement: (And if the buyer guarantees the price to his creditor and then the sold item is repossessed, the guarantor is discharged, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent difference is that repossession shows the price was never owed to the buyer, whereas in the case of return due to defect, the thing that has been nullified was established after the creditor's right attached to it, so it does not extend to him. His statement: (And if it becomes corrupt after its validity, etc.)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn's statement that kafalah bi al-ʿayn is valid for price, usurped property, property held on purchase approval (maqbud ʿala sawm al-shiraʾ), and sold property even if defective (mabiʿ fasid). The commentator explains each: price is a valid debt; the others are guaranteed upon the principal because they are of value. He then discusses branches and exceptions. He notes that if a minor sells, kafalah for the price after he receives it is invalid, but before receipt it is valid. Kafalah is also valid for the substitute of blood money (e.g., a slave) and for dowry and khulʿ compensation. He then distinguishes between cases where the guarantor is discharged: if the sold item is repossessed (mustahaqq), the guarantor is discharged from the price; if the buyer returns the item due to a defect, the guarantor is discharged only if the return is by court order or without (but in a special case where the buyer himself is the guarantor for the price to his creditor, he is not discharged). For usurped property, if the object exists, the guarantor must bring it; if consumed, he is liable for the value. If the guarantee is for an object held on purchase approval, it is considered guaranteed only if the item has a price; otherwise it is a trust. Finally, he notes an apparent inconsistency in the text regarding the effect of the buyer's exercise of an option to cancel, advising scrutiny. The commentary draws from various sources like al-Khaniyyah, al-Tatarkhaniyyah, al-Nahr, and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.

Key Terms
عين قائمة (ʿayn qāʾimah): An existing physical object that has not been consumed.
مستهلك (mustahlik): Property that has been consumed (e.g., food eaten, money spent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following guarantees is INVALID according to this passage?
A) Guaranteeing the value of a consumed usurped property.
B) Guaranteeing the return of a specific rented car.
C) Guaranteeing property held on purchase approval when the price is set.
D) Guaranteeing the price of a sold item.

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafalah for physical items depends on whether the subject is an existing object (must be delivered) or consumed (value due), and whether the service is specified (invalid) or generic.

Valid and Invalid Subjects of Kafalah: The 'Ayn Exception and the Minor's Sale
Rulings on valid and invalid subjects for kafalah with an exception for the minor's sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid subjects of kafalah in Hanafi fiqh and apply the rule that kafalah is valid for obligations that become debts if the specific object perishes.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid only for financial liabilities; it is invalid for mere uses or non-liabilities.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules on what subjects are valid for kafalah (guarantee). The text distinguishes invalid cases—like guaranteeing a hired animal's transport—from valid ones—like guaranteeing dower or blood money substitute. The key principle is that kafalah is only valid when the subject is a financial obligation or something that transforms into a debt upon destruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The passage lists specific cases of kafalah and declares them either invalid (fāsid/bāṭil) or valid (ṣaḥīḥ). Determine the ruling for each case.
2
List the invalid subjects
From the text: kafalah for transporting a specific hired animal, for the service of a specific hired slave, and for the price in a sale by a minor after the minor has taken the price (with an exception). Note the common feature: none of these are pre-existing financial obligations.
Kafalah bi al-darak (guarantee against eviction) before the minor takes the price is valid.
3
List the valid 'ayn subjects
The text states that kafalah is valid for: (1) the substitute in a blood money settlement (badal al-ṣulḥ ʿan al-dam), (2) the dower (mahr), and (3) the compensation for khulʿ. These are all financial obligations that become debts if the specific object perishes.
4
Understand the rationale
Why are these valid? Because the obligation is not voided by the destruction of the specific object. For example, if a slave given as mahr dies before delivery, the guarantor must pay its value. Thus, kafalah for such 'ayn is valid since the liability remains as a debt.
5
Apply the rule to new cases
Using the principle, test additional scenarios: e.g., guaranteeing a specific house as a gift (hiba) versus guaranteeing a specific debt. Determine validity based on whether the obligation is financial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة ۳۸۷ فاسداً وحمل دابة معينة مستأجرة وخدمة عبد استؤجر للخدمة وبلا قبول الطالب في مجلس ويستثنى من الثمن ما باع به صبي محجور عليه فكفل به رجل أو كفل بالدرك بعدما قبض الصبي الثمن لم تصح الكفالة لكونه كفل بما ليس بمضمون على الأصيل، وإن كفل بالدرك قبل قبض الصبي صحت ؛ كذا في الخانية. ومما تصح به الكفالة من الأعيان بدل الصلح عن الدم لو كان عبداً فكفل به إنسان ،صحت، فإن هلك قبل القبض فعليه قيمته، ومنها المهر وبدل الخلع لأن هذه الأشياء لا تبطل بهلاك العين ؛ كذا في الخانية.
The chapter of kafalah. It is invalid: the carriage of a specific hired animal and the service of a slave hired for service, and without the acceptance of the claimant in the session. And excepted from the price is what a boy under legal interdiction sold and a man guaranteed for it or he guaranteed for the guarantee of indemnity after the boy had taken the price, the guarantee is not valid because he guaranteed something that is not a liability on the principal. If he guaranteed for the guarantee of indemnity before the boy took the price, it is valid; as in al-Khaniyah. And from among the specific objects for which guarantee is valid are the substitute in blood money settlement; if it was a slave and a person guaranteed for him, it is valid. If it perishes before taking possession, he must pay its value. And from them are the dower and the compensation for divorce by khul', because these things are not invalidated by the destruction of the specific object; as in al-Khaniyah.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniyah presents rulings on the valid and invalid subjects of kafalah. The matn first lists invalid cases: kafalah for transporting a specific hired animal or for the service of a specific hired slave is invalid, and kafalah without the creditor's acceptance in the contract session is also invalid. The commentary then provides an exception: kafalah for the price (thaman) in a sale by a minor under interdiction is invalid if done after the minor has taken the price, because the price was never a liability on the minor. However, kafalah bi al-darak (guarantee against eviction) before the minor takes the price is valid. The passage then lists valid 'ayn (specific objects) for kafalah: the substitute in a blood money settlement (badal al-sulh 'an al-dam), the dower (mahr), and the compensation in khul' (badal al-khul'). For these, if the specific object perishes before delivery, the guarantor must pay its value because the obligation becomes a debt. This is because these obligations are not voided by the destruction of the object.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee or suretyship, where the guarantor (kafil) undertakes to fulfill an obligation if the principal (asil) fails.
بدل الصلح عن الدم (badal al-sulh 'an al-dam): A substitute payment made in a settlement for homicide or injury, avoiding qisas or diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person guarantees for the substitute in a blood money settlement, and the substitute (a slave) perishes before delivery, what is the guarantor's liability?
A) He is not liable because the slave perished.
B) He must pay the value of the slave.
C) He must pay the blood money instead.
D) He is released from all liability.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for a specific object is valid only if the obligation becomes a debt upon the object's destruction; otherwise, it is invalid.

Kafalah Release upon Istihqaq and Defect Return
Ruling on kafalah release due to istihqaq and defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state whether a kafil for the price is released when the sold item is claimed by a third party or returned due to a defect, and distinguish the case where the buyer is also the kafil.

Core Idea
Istihqaq extinguishes the price debt; defect return also releases the kafil unless the buyer is the kafil for his own debt.

The kafalah for the price (thaman) is a guarantee of a debt. When the underlying obligation disappears, the kafil should be released. This lesson examines two common causes: the sold item being claimed by its true owner (istihqaq) or being returned due to a defect. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between a straightforward kafalah and a more complex arrangement where the buyer guarantees the price to his own creditor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents separate rulings for (1) istihqaq and (2) return due to defect, whether by court order or voluntarily, or due to khiyar al-ru'yah or khiyar al-shart.
Both scenarios involve the sale being undone.
2
Note the general rule
In both cases, the kafil is released because the debt of the price is no longer owed.
The kafil's liability is accessory to the principal debt.
3
Examine the exception
When the buyer himself guaranteed the price to his creditor, then if the sold item is claimed, the kafil is still released; but if the buyer returns it due to a defect, the kafil is not released.
The difference lies in the origin of the debt: istihqaq proves the debt never existed; defect return creates a new debt by virtue of the buyer's right of return.
4
Apply the rule
State the ruling for a hypothetical: A guarantees for B's purchase from C. If a third party D proves ownership of the item, A is released. If B returns a defective item voluntarily, A is also released. But if B is A's creditor and A guarantees B's debt to C, and B returns due to defect, A remains liable.
Check the specifics: who is the kafil and who is the principal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو كفل بالثمن فاستحق المبيع برئ الكفيل، وكذا لو رده بعيب بقضاء أو بغير قضاء أو بخيار رؤية أو شرط، ولو كفل المشتري بالثمن لغريمه ثم استحق المبيع برئ الكفيل، ولو رده بعيب بقضاء أو بغير قضاء لا . ولو كفل بالمهر عنه ثم سقط عنه كله قبل الدخول أو نصفه قبله برئ الكفيل عن الكل في الأول وعن النصف في الثاني حكماً لبراءة الزوج. ولو كفل بالثمن ثم ظهر فساد البيع رجع الكفيل بما دفعه إن شاء على البائع وإن شاء على المشتري وإن فسد بعد صحته بأن ألحقا به شرطاً فاسداً فالرجوع للمشتري على البائع وتمامه في التتارخانية هنا. وذكر في باب خيار الشرط لو كان بالثمن كفيل ففسخ المشتري فلم يرد المبيع إلى البائع فله مطالبة الكفيل بالثمن حتى يرد المشتري المبيع .اهـ. وهو مخالف لقوله هنا إن الكفيل يبرأ بفسخ البيع بخيار الشرط ونحوه فليتأمل. قال في النهر : وكان الفرق بينهما أن بظهور الفساد تبين أن البائع أخذ شيئاً لا يستحقه فيرجع الكفيل عليه وإن ألحقا به شرطاً فاسداً لم يتبين أن البائع حين قبضه قبض شيئاً لا يستحقه.
If he guaranteed for the price and the object of sale is claimed (istihaqq), the guarantor is released. Similarly, if he returns it due to a defect, whether by judgment or without judgment, or due to option of inspection or option of condition. And if the buyer guaranteed for the price to his creditor, then the object of sale is claimed, the guarantor is released. But if he returns it due to a defect, whether by judgment or without judgment, he is not released. And if he guaranteed for the dower, then the entire dower falls before consummation or half of it before consummation, the guarantor is released from all in the first and from half in the second, by operation of the husband's discharge. And if he guaranteed for the price, then the sale turns out to be void (fasid, from its inception), the guarantor may recover what he paid, if he wishes from the seller and if he wishes from the buyer. But if it becomes void after it was valid, by their adding a void condition, then the recourse is for the buyer against the seller. The rest is in al-Tatar Khaniyah. And it is mentioned in the chapter of option of condition: if there is a guarantor for the price, and the buyer rescinds and does not return the sold item to the seller, the seller may demand the guarantor for the price until the buyer returns the sold item. End quote. And this contradicts his statement here that the guarantor is released by rescission of the sale due to option of condition and similar; so let one reflect. Al-Nahr said: The difference between them is that with the appearance of voidness (fasid), it becomes clear that the seller took that which he did not deserve, so the guarantor has recourse against him. But if they added a void condition, it does not become clear that the seller, when he took, took that which he did not deserve.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on kafalah for the price (thaman) and its release. The general rule: if the sold item is claimed by a rightful owner (istihqq), the kafil for the price is released because the debt of the price disappears. Similarly, if the buyer returns the item due to a defect (whether by court order or mutual agreement) or due to khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) or khiyar al-shart (option of condition), the kafil is released. However, if the buyer himself guaranteed the price to his own creditor (e.g., he is also a debtor), and the sold item is claimed, the kafil is released, but if the buyer returns the item due to a defect (even by court order), the kafil is NOT released. The reason is that in the case of istihqq, the price never was an obligation, but in return due to defect, the price was an obligation that only fell due to a new cause (the defect), so the kafil remains liable. The passage then treats kafalah for mahr: if the husband is released from the mahr completely before consummation (e.g., by valid annulment), the kafil is released from the entire mahr; if half falls before consummation (as in a divorce before consummation), the kafil is released from half. This is because the principal's liability ends. Next, kafalah for the price when the sale is void from the start (fasid asl) vs. void after being valid (due to a condition). In the first case, the kafil can recover from either seller or buyer; in the second, only from the seller. A contradiction is noted: in the Khiyar al-Shart section, it says the kafil remains liable until the buyer returns the item, while here it says the kafil is released upon rescission of the sale. Al-Nahr resolves this: with initial voidness, the seller's taking the price was unjustified from the start, so the kafil can recover from the seller. But with a later void condition, the seller's initial taking was justified, so the kafil cannot recover from the seller directly; rather, the buyer must return the item first.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A claim of ownership by a third party that invalidates the sale.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The guarantor who assumes liability for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer who guaranteed the price to his own creditor returns the sold item due to a defect, is the kafil released?
A) Yes, because the sale is rescinded.
B) No, because the debt to the creditor remains.
C) Yes, but only with court order.
D) No, because the kafil is the buyer's creditor.

Key Takeaway
The kafil for the price is released when the sale is undone by istihqaq or defect return, except when the buyer is the kafil for his own debt to the creditor.

Voidness of Sale and Kafalah: Fasid Asl vs Later Fasid with Contradiction Resolution
Rulings on void sales and contradiction resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of initial voidness (fasid asl) from later voidness due to a condition on the kafil's recourse, and explain the apparent contradiction regarding khiyar al-shart.

Core Idea
Fasid asl gives the kafil recourse against both seller and buyer; later fasid limits recourse to seller. The contradiction in khiyar al-shart is resolved by the timing of obligation.

This lesson unpacks the rulings when a sale is void (fasid) and its impact on kafalah for the price. The critical distinction is whether the voidness was from the beginning or arose later due to a condition. Additionally, an apparent contradiction between different parts of the book on kafalah and khiyar al-shart is resolved by al-Nahr's commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of voidness
Fasid asl: the sale is void from inception (e.g., missing essential condition). Fasid after sahih: the sale was valid but later made void by inserting a void condition.
The passage distinguishes these.
2
State the recourse for the kafil
In fasid asl, the kafil can recover from either the seller or the buyer. In later fasid, recourse is only against the seller.
Why? In fasid asl, the seller took money he never deserved. In later fasid, the seller initially deserved the money, so the buyer must recover from seller, and kafil can only claim from seller.
3
Analyze the contradiction
In the chapter on khiyar al-shart, it says the kafil remains liable until the buyer returns the item. But here it says the kafil is released upon rescission by any khiyar. This seems contradictory.
Al-Nahr provides the resolution.
4
Al-Nahr's resolution
The difference is that with fasid asl, the seller's taking of the price was unjust from the start, so the kafil has direct recourse. With later fasid (or khiyar rescission), the seller initially took the price justly, so the kafil cannot recover from seller unless the buyer first returns the item.
Thus the ruling in khiyar al-shart is consistent: if the buyer does not return, the kafil remains liable because the rescission hasn't fully undone the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): Voidness of a contract. Hanafi distinguishes fasid asl (void from inception) and fasid after sahih (void due to later condition).
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of condition: right to rescind within a stipulated period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of fasid asl (void from inception), from whom can the kafil recover?
A) Only from the seller.
B) Only from the buyer.
C) From either seller or buyer.
D) He cannot recover.

Key Takeaway
The kafil's recourse depends on whether the voidness was initial or later; initial voidness gives recourse against both parties, later voidness only against the seller.

Guarantee for Usurped Property: Existing vs. Consumed
Rulings on guarantee for usurped property depending on its existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation of a guarantor for usurped property depending on whether the property still exists or is consumed.

Core Idea
For a guarantor of usurped property, the obligation is to produce the specific object if it exists; only if consumed does the value become due.

This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi law of guarantee (kafalah) for usurped property. The guarantor's duty shifts based on whether the usurped item remains in existence or has been consumed. Understanding this difference prevents confusion about when the guarantor must deliver the actual item versus pay its value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of guaranteed property
The passage distinguishes between an existing specific object (ʿayn qāʾimah) and a consumed object (mustahlik). This is the foundational distinction that determines the guarantor's obligation.
A dirham is consumed when spent; a car remains an ʿayn.
2
Determine the guarantor's duty for an existing ʿayn
If the guaranteed object still exists, the guarantor must produce that exact item and hand it over. The guarantor is not liable for its value even if it later perishes.
The guarantor is not a seller; he is returning the very item.
3
Determine the guarantor's duty for a consumed object
If the object is consumed (e.g., money spent), the guarantee applies to the value. The guarantor must pay the value, and his acceptance of the guarantee implies admission of liability.
No need for the claimant to prove the consumed state separately.
4
Apply to the scenario of a claim
If a claimant alleges a specific existing item (e.g., 'a thousand dirhams in the defendant's hand'), the guarantor must bring that exact item. If he cannot, he is not liable until the claimant proves the claim against the defendant. However, if the claim is for a consumed item, the guarantor is liable immediately even without proof.
The difference hinges on whether the object is still in existence.
5
Note the exception for items taken on approval
The passage ends by connecting this rule to items taken for inspection with intent to purchase: if such an item has a price, it is treated like a guarantee (daman); otherwise it remains a trust (amānah).
This shows the principle extends beyond pure usurpation cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ضمان المغصوب فإن كان المضمون عيناً قائماً فيلزم الضامن إحضارها وتسليمها لا قيمتها إن هلكت، وإن كان المضمون مستهلكاً فالمضمون قيمته لما في السراج الوهاج ولو ادعى على رجل أنه غصبه ألف درهم وهو في يده أو في منزله أو ادعى شيئاً يكون ديناً من مكيل أو موزون فضمن له رجل ما ادعى كان على الضامن أن يأتي بذلك الشيء بعينه، فإن لم يأت بذلك الشيء لم يضمن حتى يستحقه المدعي على المدعى عليه، وإن ادعى ألفاً مستهلكة أو كراً مستهلكاً فضمنه رجل فهو ضامن من وإن لم يقم المدعي بينة لأن العين ما دامت باقية فالضمان ينصرف إلى إحضارها ولا ينصرف إلى تسليمها إلا بعد الاستحقاق وإن كانت هالكة فالضمان ينصرف إلى القيمة فصار ضمانه دلالة على الاعتراف بالضمان .اهـ والمقبوض على سوم الشراء إنما يكون من هذا النوع إذا له ثمن وإلا فهو أمانة كما قدمناه في البيوع
As for the guarantee of usurped property: if the guaranteed object is a specific existing 'ayn, the guarantor is obliged to produce it and hand it over, not its value if it perishes. But if the guaranteed object is consumed (mustahlik), then what is guaranteed is its value. As in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if a person claims against a man that he usurped a thousand dirhams while it is in his hand or in his house, or claims something that is a debt such as a measured or weighed item, and a man guarantees for him what he claimed, then the guarantor must bring that specific thing. If he does not bring that thing, he is not liable until the claimant proves his right against the defendant. But if he claims a consumed thousand dirhams or a consumed kurr, and a man guarantees for him, then he is a guarantor, even if the claimant does not establish proof. This is because as long as the 'ayn remains, the guarantee is directed to producing it, and it is not directed to handing it over until after entitlement. But if it is destroyed, the guarantee is directed to the value. Thus his guarantee becomes an indication of admission of liability. End quote. And what is seized on the basis of a purchase negotiation is of this type if it has a price; otherwise it is a trust, as we have earlier in the chapter of sales.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj explains the rules of guarantee for usurped property (al-maghsab). If the object of the guarantee is a specific existing 'ayn (like a particular car), the guarantor must bring and deliver that exact object. If the object is consumed (mustahlik, as with money that has been spent), then the guarantee applies to its value. The passage discusses a scenario: a claimant alleges that a defendant has usurped a thousand dirhams that still exist in the defendant's possession (e.g., in his hand or house). If a third party guarantees that claim (i.e., promises to deliver the dirhams), the guarantor must bring the specific coins. If the guarantor fails to bring them, he is not liable until the claimant proves the validity of his claim against the defendant. However, if the claim is for a consumed thousand dirhams (i.e., the money has been used up), a guarantee for that is valid, and the guarantor is liable from the start, even without proof from the claimant. The reason is that when the 'ayn exists, the guarantee is about producing the 'ayn, and the guarantor is not liable until the claimant's right is established. But when the 'ayn is consumed, the guarantee is about the value, and the guarantor's acceptance of guarantee implies admission of liability. The passage ends by noting that an item taken for inspection with intent to purchase is similar: if it has a price, it is considered a guarantee; otherwise it is a trust.

Key Terms
ضمان المغصوب (daman al-maghsab): Guarantee for the return of or value of usurped property.
عين قائمة ('ayn qa'imah): A specific, existing object that is not consumed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone claims that a defendant has usurped a specific existing item, and a person guarantees that claim, what must the guarantor do?
A) Pay the value of the item immediately.
B) Bring the specific item to the claimant.
C) Prove his own innocence.
D) Wait until the defendant admits.

Key Takeaway
A guarantor of a usurped existing 'ayn must produce the exact object; a guarantor of consumed property must pay the value, and his guarantee implies admission of liability.

Kafalah for Service of a Specific Hired Animal or Slave
Explanation of invalidity of kafalah for service on specific hired object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid forms of kafalah for hired services involving specific vs. non-specific objects and explain the ruling with its rationale.

Core Idea
Kafalah for service (haml) on a specific animal is invalid because the guarantor lacks authority to grant permission to use that animal.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a line from the matn about kafalah for the carriage of a specific hired animal and the service of a specific hired slave. The key ruling is that such kafalah is invalid because the guarantor cannot fulfill the obligation—he has no authority over the specific object to grant the necessary permission (tahmil). However, if the guarantee is merely for delivery of the animal itself, it is valid. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the crucial distinctions that determine validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its application
Read the source passage and note that kafalah for 'carriage of a specific animal' (haml dabbah mu'ayyanah) and 'service of a specific slave' (khidmat abd mu'ayyan) is declared invalid. Determine the hukm level: batil (void).
2
Understand the basis: inability due to lack of authority
The commentator argues that the guarantor is unable to deliver the service because he has no authority (wilayah) over the specific animal to grant permission for loading (tahmil). If he provides his own animal, he does not fulfill the contract because the contracted service is tied to that specific animal.
Contrast with kafalah for delivery (taslim) of the specific animal, which is valid because the guarantor can physically hand over the animal.
3
Distinguish specific vs. non-specific
The passage restricts the invalidity to cases where the animal is specified ('mu'ayyan'). If the contract is for carriage generally (non-specific), kafalah is valid because the guarantor can provide his own animal. Note the condition that the service is defined by the object.
4
Distinguish delivery vs. loading (tahmil)
The commentator clarifies that what is owed is a combination of delivery and permission to load. For a specific animal, the guarantor cannot grant permission; for a non-specific animal, he can by supplying his own animal. This distinction determines validity.
Reference to al-Nihayah's formulation: 'tarkib' (combination) of delivery and permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وحمل دابة معينة مستأجرة وخدمة عبد استؤجر للخدمة) أي وبطلت الكفالة بحمل دابة إلى آخره لأنها إذا كانت معينة كان الكفيل عاجزاً عن تسليمها لأنه لا ولاية له في الحمل على دابة الغير لأنه لو أعطى دابة من عنده لا يستحق الأجرة لأنه أتى بغير المعقود عليه . قيد بكونها معينة لأنها لو كانت بغير عينها جازت الكفالة لأنه يمكنه الحمل على دابة نفسه والحمل هو المستحق. وقيد بالحمل لأنه لو كفل بتسليم الدابة المعينة يجوز كما قدمناه وفي فتح القدير والحاصل أنه إن كان الحمل على الدابة بتسليمها فينبغي أن تصح الكفالة لأن الكفالة بتسليم المستأجر صحيحة ولم يمنع منه كون المستأجر ملكاً لغير الكفيل، وإن كان التحميل ينبغي أن لا يصح فيهما لأن التحميل غير واجب على الأصيل. والحق أن الواجب في الحمل على دابة معينة أو غير معينة ليس مجرد تسليمها بل المجموع من تسليمها والإذن في تحميلها وهو ما ذكر في النهاية من التركيب، وما ذكرنا من الحمل عليها ففي المعينة لا يقدر على الإذن في تحميلها إذ ليس له ولاية عليها ليصح إذنه الذي هو معنى الحمل، وفي غير المعينة يمكنه ذلك عند تسليم دابة نفسه أو دابة استأجرها اهـ .
His saying: (And the carriage of a specific hired animal and the service of a slave hired for service) meaning: the kafalah is invalid for the carriage of an animal, etc., because if it is specific, the guarantor is unable to deliver it since he has no authority over the carriage on another's animal. For if he gives an animal from his own possession, he is not entitled to the wage because he has brought other than what was contracted. It is restricted to being specific, because if it was not specific, the kafalah would be valid because he can transport on his own animal, and the transport is what is due. And it is restricted to carriage, because if he guaranteed the delivery of the specific animal, it is valid as we have mentioned. In Fath al-Qadir: the summary is that if the carriage on the animal means delivery of it, then kafalah should be valid because kafalah for delivery of the leased object is valid, and the fact that the leased object is owned by another does not prevent it. But if it means loading (tahmil), then kafalah should be invalid in both cases because loading is not obligatory on the principal. The correct view is that what is obligatory in carriage of an animal, whether specific or not, is not merely delivery, but a combination of delivery and permission to load it, as mentioned in al-Nihayah with the term 'tarkib'. As for our mention of 'carriage', for a specific animal he cannot give permission to load it since he has no authority over it to make his permission valid, which is the meaning of carriage. For a non-specific animal, he can do that by delivering his own animal or an animal he hires.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a line from the matn: 'And the carriage of a specific hired animal and the service of a slave hired for service.' The commentator explains why kafalah for these is invalid. The key reason: if the animal is specific, the guarantor cannot deliver the service because he has no authority to force the owner to allow use of the animal. If the guarantor were to provide his own animal, he would not fulfill the contract because the contracted animal is specific. However, if the animal is not specific (i.e., the contract is for carriage generally, not a particular animal), the kafalah is valid because the guarantor can provide his own animal. The commentary also distinguishes between kafalah for 'delivery of the animal' (tashim al-dabbah) and kafalah for 'loading on it' (haml). For delivery of a specific animal, kafalah is valid because the guarantor can deliver the animal itself. For loading on a specific animal, kafalah is invalid because the guarantor cannot grant permission to use the animal. The correct view from al-Nihayah is that what is owed under the hire contract is a combination of delivery and permission to load. For a specific animal, the guarantor cannot grant permission; for a non-specific animal, he can by providing an animal of his own or hiring one.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): In this context, 'loading' or 'carriage' as a service; specifically, the act of transporting goods on an animal.
التحميل (al-tahmil): The permission to load; an authority granted by the owner to use the animal for loading.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafalah for transporting a specific hired animal invalid?
A) Because the guarantor does not own the animal.
B) Because the guarantor cannot grant permission to load a specific animal he does not own.
C) Because hire contracts are invalid.
D) Because the animal might die.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for a service that requires permission to use a specific object is invalid because the guarantor lacks authority over that object; delivery of the object itself, however, can be guaranteed even if specific.

Creditor's Acceptance in Kafalah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on session acceptance in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the requirement of the creditor's acceptance in the contract session for kafalah and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In kafalah, two Imams require session acceptance; Abu Yusuf allows later ratification—and the fatwa follows him.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi disagreement. While Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani insist that the creditor's acceptance must occur in the same session as the offer, Abu Yusuf allows the contract to be valid if the creditor ratifies later. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the legal reasoning behind each view and concludes with the operative fatwa. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying kafalah contracts in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the views of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (acceptance must be in the session or the contract is invalid) and of Abu Yusuf (acceptance can be later or even unnecessary).
Note that Abu Yusuf's view is further split: some riwayas require ratification, others do not.
2
Examine the proofs (ta'lil)
Understand the underlying reasoning: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad view kafalah as tamlik (ownership transfer of the right to demand), requiring both parties' presence. Abu Yusuf views it as iltizam (commitment), which the guarantor can undertake alone.
The term 'tamlik' explains why session acceptance is needed; 'iltizam' explains why it is not.
3
Analyze the fuduli exception
See that even Abu Hanifa and Muhammad allow a fuduli (unauthorized agent) to accept on behalf of an absent creditor, making the contract suspended (mawquf) pending ratification.
This shows the condition relates to the creditor's own acceptance, not acceptance in general.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Recognize that according to al-Bazzaziyyah, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view (the second opinion). Thus, in practical fatwa, later ratification validates the contract.
Some later sources (e.g., al-Islah, al-Idah) even omit the creditor from the condition, emphasizing effectiveness over validity.
5
Apply the distinction: sihha vs nafadh
Distinguish between a condition of validity (sihha) and a condition of effectiveness (nafadh). Acceptance in the session is a condition of validity for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while for the fatwa it is a condition of effectiveness.
This distinction clarifies why later ratification can cure the defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبلا) قبول الطالب في مجلس العقد أي وبطلت الكفالة بلا قبول الطالب في مجلس الإيجاب أي لم تنعقد أصلاً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز إذا بلغه فأجاز. ولم يشترط في بعض النسخ الإجازة وهو الأظهر عنه والخلاف في في النفس والمال جميعاً له أنه تصرف التزام فيستبد به الملتزم وهذا وجه الظاهر عنه، ووجه التوقف ما قدمناه في الفضولي في النكاح ولهما أن فيهما معنى التمليك وهو تمليك المطالبة منه فيقوم بهما جميعاً والموجود شطره فلا يتوقف على ما وراء المجلس إلا أن يقبل عن الطالب فضولي فإنه يصح ويتوقف على إجازته وللكفيل أن يخرج نفسه عنها قبل إجازته ؛ كذا شرح المجمع والحقائق. وبه علم أن قبول الطالب بخصوصه إنما هو شرط النفاذ، وأما أصل القبول في مجلس الإيجاب فشرط الصحة، فلو حذف الطالب في الكتاب لكان أولى كما فعل في الإصلاح ونبه عليه في الإيضاح وفي البزازية الفضولي لو فسخ الموقوف لا في البزازية. وفي البزازية : الفتوى على قول الثاني قيد بالإنشاء لأنه لو أخبر عن قال في النهر : وكان الفرق بينهما أن بظهور الفساد تبين أن البائع أخذ شيئاً لا يستحقه فيرجع الكفيل عليه وإن ألحقا به شرطاً فاسداً لم يتبين أن البائع حين قبضه قبض شيئاً لا يستحقه. قوله: (ولم يشترط في بعض النسخ الإجازة هذه عبارة .الهداية. قال في الفتح أي نسخ كفالة الأصل عن أبي يوس بل إنه نافد إن كان المكفول عنه غائباً . قوله : (ووجه التوقف قال الرملي : أي التوقف على الإجازة اهـ. وقوله «ما قدمناه الخ قال في الفتح وهو أن شطر العقد يتوقف حتى إذا عقد فضولي لامرأة على آخر توقف على الإجازة كما إذا كان عقداً تاماً بأن خاطب عنه فضولي آخر، وعندهما لا يتوقف إلا أن خاطب عنه فضولي آخر فلا يتوقف عندهما إلا العقد التام قوله : (وبه علم الخ) قال في الفتح قالوا : إذ قبل عنه قابل توقف بالإجماع وحينئذ فقوله لا يصح إلا بقبول المكفول له غير صحيح بل الشرط أن يقبل في المجلس إن كان حاضراً فينفذ أو يقبل عنه فضولي إن كان غائباً فيتوقف إلى
His saying: (And without) the acceptance of the claimant in the session of the contract, meaning: the kafalah is invalid without the acceptance of the claimant in the session of the offer; i.e., it is not concluded at all. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is valid if he learns of it and approves. In some copies, approval is not stipulated, and that is the more apparent from him. The dispute applies to both guarantee of person and money. For him (Abu Yusuf), it is a transaction of commitment, so the one who commits is sole master. This is the apparent view from him. The reason for suspension (tawaquf) is what we have mentioned regarding the fuduli in marriage. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), these transactions contain an element of ownership, i.e., ownership of the right to demand from him, so both parties must be present. Since only one side is present, it does not suspend beyond the session unless a fuduli accepts on behalf of the claimant, in which case it is valid and suspended on his ratification. The guarantor may withdraw before ratification. As in Sharh al-Majma' and al-Haqa'iq. From this it is understood that the acceptance of the claimant specifically is a condition of effectiveness (nafadh), while the original acceptance in the session of the offer is a condition of validity (sihha). So if the claimant is omitted from the book, it would be better, as done in al-Islah and pointed out in al-Idah. In al-Bazzaziyyah: the fuduli may annul the suspended contract; not in al-Bazzaziyyah. In al-Bazzaziyyah: the fatwa is according to the second opinion, restricted to initiation, because if he reports of... Al-Nahr said: The difference between them is that by the appearance of voidness, it becomes clear that the seller took what he did not deserve, so the guarantor has recourse against him. But if they added a void condition, it does not become clear that the seller, when he took, took what he did not deserve. His saying: (And in some copies, approval is not stipulated) this is the wording of al-Hidayah. Al-Fath said: i.e., in the copies of Kafalat al-Asl from Abu Yusuf, it is even effective if the principal is absent. His saying: (And the reason for suspension) al-Ramli said: i.e., suspension on ratification. End quote. And his saying: 'what we have mentioned' etc. Al-Fath said: that half of the contract suspends, such that if a fuduli contracts a marriage for a woman with another man, it suspends on ratification, just as if it were a complete contract by addressing another fuduli. According to them, it does not suspend unless a fuduli addresses another fuduli; they only suspend a complete contract. His saying: (And from this it is understood) Al-Fath said: They said: if a proxy accepts on his behalf, it suspends by consensus. Hence, his statement 'it is not valid except with the acceptance of the principal' is incorrect; rather, the condition is that the acceptance be in the session if he is present, in which case it takes effect, or a fuduli accepts on his behalf if he is absent, in which case it suspends until...
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq discusses the requirement of the creditor's acceptance in the contract session for kafalah. The matn states that kafalah is invalid without acceptance in the session. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that acceptance must occur in the same session; otherwise, the contract is not concluded. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that if the creditor learns of the kafalah later and ratifies it, it becomes valid. Some transmissions from Abu Yusuf do not even require ratification, making the kafalah immediately effective. The dispute applies to both kafalah bi al-nafs and kafalah bi al-mal. The basis for Abu Yusuf's view: kafalah is a commitment (iltizam) that the guarantor can make independently, like a promise. The basis for the other two imams: kafalah involves an element of ownership (tamlik) on the part of the creditor (the right to demand payment), so both offer and acceptance are required, and acceptance must be in the session if the creditor is present. However, if a fuduli (unauthorized person) accepts on behalf of an absent creditor, it is valid but suspended on the creditor's ratification; the guarantor may withdraw before ratification. The fatwa is according to the second opinion (of Abu Yusuf), as stated in al-Bazzaziyyah. Further commentaries refine that acceptance in the session is a condition of validity, while the creditor's own acceptance is a condition of effectiveness. The text also includes a digression on a different issue (the difference between initial voidness and subsequent voidness in sales), which seems misplaced. The passage ends with a discussion on fuduli acting on behalf of an absent creditor, stating that if a proxy (qabil) accepts, it suspends by consensus, and the correct view is that acceptance must be in the session if the creditor is present, or by a fuduli if absent, in which case it suspends.

Key Terms
قبول الطالب (qabul al-talib): The acceptance of the creditor (the person in whose favor the guarantee is made) in the contract session.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized third party who acts on behalf of another without prior agency.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; here, the transfer of the right to demand payment from the guarantor.
التزام (iltizam): A binding commitment undertaken unilaterally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a person makes a kafalah offer and the creditor is not present, what is the ruling?
A) The kafalah is invalid.
B) It is valid if the creditor later approves.
C) It is valid immediately without approval.
D) It is valid only if the guarantor is a relative.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in Hanafi kafalah is that the creditor's acceptance may occur after the session via ratification, not necessarily in the same session.

Kafalah for Delivering a Specific Animal: The Obligation of Loading
Ruling with proof on kafalah for animal delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah for delivering a specific animal and explain why it hinges on the ability to grant permission to load.

Core Idea
A kafalah to deliver a specific animal is invalid because the guarantor cannot grant permission to load—the obligation is composite.

In this passage, the Hanafi jurists discuss the validity of a guarantee (kafalah) to deliver an animal. The key insight is that the obligation is not merely delivery but includes giving permission to load. This distinction determines whether the guarantee is valid for a specific or non-specific animal. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a kafalah (guarantee) to deliver an animal. The text states that the guarantee is valid if it is for a non-specific animal, but invalid if for a specific animal. What is the basis for this distinction?
2
Understand the composite obligation
The text explains that the true obligation is not just delivering the animal (taslim), but also granting permission to load it (tahmil). The guarantor must be able to fulfill both parts.
The phrase 'al-majmū‘ min taslīmihā wa al-idhn fī taḥmīlihā' encapsulates this.
3
Distinguish specific vs non-specific
For a specific animal (mu‘ayyanah) owned by another, the guarantor has no authority to grant permission to load, so the guarantee fails. For a non-specific animal (ghayr mu‘ayyanah), he can deliver his own animal or a hired one, thus he can grant permission.
4
Analyze the proof
The proof is rational (qiyās): the obligation is composite; if the guarantor cannot fulfill one part (permission), the guarantee is invalid. The text cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Nihayah as authorities for this analysis.
This is an example of ta‘līl (reasoning based on the effective cause).
5
Note the ikhtilaf
The text implies there is a difference of opinion: some earlier scholars considered kafalah for a specific animal valid if delivery alone is required. However, the correct view (al-ḥaqq) is that the composite obligation invalidates it.
This ikhtilaf is resolved in favor of the composite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جازت الكفالة لأنه يمكنه الحمل على دابة نفسه والحمل هو المستحق. وقيد بالحمل لأنه لو كفل بتسليم الدابة المعينة يجوز كما قدمناه وفي فتح القدير والحاصل أنه إن كان الحمل على الدابة بتسليمها فينبغي أن تصح الكفالة لأن الكفالة بتسليم المستأجر صحيحة ولم يمنع منه كون المستأجر ملكاً لغير الكفيل، وإن كان التحميل ينبغي أن لا يصح فيهما لأن التحميل غير واجب على الأصيل. والحق أن الواجب في الحمل على الدابة معينة أو غير معينة ليس مجرد تسليمها بل المجموع من تسليمها والإذن في تحميلها وهو ما ذكر في النهاية من التركيب، وما ذكرنا من الحمل عليها ففي المعينة لا يقدر على الإذن في تحميلها إذ ليس له ولاية عليها ليصح إذنه الذي هو معنى الحمل، وفي غير المعينة يمكنه ذلك عند تسليم دابة نفسه أو دابة استأجرها اهـ.
The guarantee is valid because he can load it onto his own animal, and the loading is what is owed. It is restricted to loading because if he guaranteed delivering a specific animal, it is valid as we have previously mentioned. In Fath al-Qadir, the conclusion: if the loading on the animal is by delivering it, then the guarantee should be valid because guaranteeing the delivery of the hired animal is valid, and the fact that the hired animal is owned by someone other than the guarantor does not prevent it. If it is the act of loading, then it should not be valid in either case because loading is not obligatory upon the original (debtor). The correct view is that what is obligatory in loading on a specific or non-specific animal is not mere delivery but the combination of delivery and permission to load it, which is the composite mentioned in al-Nihayah. Regarding what we mentioned about loading on it: for a specific animal, he cannot give permission to load it because he has no authority over it to make his permission valid, which is the meaning of loading. For a non-specific animal, he can do so when delivering his own animal or one he has hired.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of kafalah (guarantee) for delivering an animal. It begins by stating that the guarantee is valid because the guarantor can fulfill the obligation by using his own animal. The key distinction is between taslim (delivery) and tahmil (loading). If the obligation is merely delivery, guarantee is valid; if it is loading, there is a problem because loading is not an obligation on the principal debtor. The correct view, according to this text, is that the obligation is a composite of delivery and permission to load. For a specific animal (mu'ayyana), the guarantor cannot grant permission because he has no ownership; for a non-specific animal, he can, by delivering his own or a hired animal. This demonstrates Hanafi reasoning based on the nature of the obligation.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A guarantee or surety contract where the guarantor undertakes to fulfill an obligation of the principal debtor.
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery of the animal.
تحميل (taḥmīl): Loading, i.e., granting permission to load.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is kafalah for a specific animal (mu‘ayyanah) not valid?
A) Because the guarantor cannot deliver it.
B) Because the guarantor cannot give permission to load it since he has no authority over it.
C) Because the animal is owned by someone else.
D) Because loading is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kafalah for delivering an animal is valid only if the guarantor can both deliver and grant permission to load; for a specific animal he cannot, so the guarantee is invalid.

Requirement of Acceptance by Creditor in Kafalah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance timing in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the necessity of acceptance in the council of contract for kafalah and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
In kafalah, acceptance by the creditor must occur in the council of contract according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf allows later ratification.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does the creditor's acceptance have to be in the same session as the offer for kafalah to be valid? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that it must, otherwise the contract is void. Abu Yusuf, however, argues that kafalah is a commitment that can be ratified later. We will explore the reasoning behind each view and the practical consequence in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core disagreement is whether the creditor's acceptance must occur in the council of contract (majlis al-'aqd) for kafalah to be valid.
This is a condition of validity, not just enforceability.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They hold that without acceptance in the council, the kafalah is void (lam tan'aqid). Both offer and acceptance must happen in the same session.
They see kafalah as a transfer of the right to demand (tamlik), requiring mutual participation.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf allows the contract to be valid if the creditor ratifies it later, even after the council ends.
He views kafalah as a binding commitment (iltizam) that can be perfected later.
4
Analyse the proof for each position
The Sahibayn analogise to tamlik (e.g., sale), where both parties must be present. Abu Yusuf compares to a unilateral commitment that can be accepted later.
The text also distinguishes between a condition of validity (sihha) and enforceability (nafadh).
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative view)
According to Al-Bazzaziyya, the fatwa is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf (the second opinion), but some sources say fatwa is on the Sahibayn.
The dispute remains within the school; both positions are recognised.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبلا) قبول الطالب في مجلس العقد أي وبطلت الكفالة بلا قبول الطالب في مجلس الإيجاب أي لم تنعقد أصلاً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف: يجوز إذا بلغه فأجاز. ولم يشترط في بعض النسخ الإجازة وهو الأظهر عنه والخلاف في في النفس والمال جميعاً له أنه تصرف التزام فيستبد به الملتزم وهذا وجه الظاهر عنه، ووجه التوقف ما قدمناه في الفضولي في النكاح ولهما أن فيهما معنى التمليك وهو تمليك المطالبة منه فيقوم بهما جميعاً والموجود شطره فلا يتوقف على ما وراء المجلس إلا أن يقبل عن الطالب فضولي فإنه يصح ويتوقف على إجازته وللكفيل أن يخرج نفسه عنها قبل إجازته؛ كذا شرح المجمع والحقائق. وبه علم أن قبول الطالب بخصوصه إنما هو شرط النفاذ، وأما أصل القبول في مجلس الإيجاب فشرط الصحة، فلو حذف الطالب في الكتاب لكان أولى كما فعل في الإصلاح ونبه عليه في الإيضاح وفي البزازية الفضولي لو فسخ الموقوف لا في البزازية. وفي البزازية : الفتوى على قول الثاني
His saying: (Without) acceptance of the creditor in the council of contract – meaning the guarantee is invalid without the acceptance of the creditor in the council of offer, i.e., it is not concluded at all. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is valid if he is informed and then ratifies. In some copies, ratification is not stipulated, and this is the more apparent of his views. The dispute applies to both persons and property. For him (Abu Yusuf) it is a binding act (tasarruf iltizam), so the person undertaking it acts independently. This is the apparent basis for his view. The basis for the view that it depends (waqf) is what we have mentioned earlier about the proxy marriage (al-fuduli fi al-nikah). For the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) it has the meaning of transfer of ownership (tamlik), which is the transfer of the right to demand from him, so both parties must participate. Since only half (the offer) is present, it does not depend on what is beyond the council unless a proxy (fuduli) accepts on behalf of the creditor, in which case it is valid but depends on his ratification. The guarantor may withdraw before ratification. This is as explained in Sharh al-Majma‘ and al-Haqa’iq. From this it is known that the acceptance of the creditor specifically is a condition of enforceability (nafadh), while the basic acceptance in the council of offer is a condition of validity (sihha). So if the creditor were omitted from the document, it would be better, as done in al-Islah and pointed out in al-Idah. In al-Bazzaziyya: The proxy (fuduli) can rescind the suspended contract? No. In al-Bazzaziyya: The fatwa is according to the second opinion.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the necessity of acceptance by the creditor in the council of contract for a valid kafalah. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) require that the acceptance occur in the same session; otherwise, the contract is not concluded. Abu Yusuf allows the contract to be valid if the creditor ratifies it later. The reasoning: Abu Yusuf sees kafalah as a commitment (iltizam) that can stand alone, while the Sahibayn see it as a transfer of a right of demand (tamlik), requiring both offer and acceptance in the same council. The text further distinguishes between a condition of enforceability (nafadh) and a condition of validity (sihha), and discusses the role of a proxy (fuduli). The fatwa is reported to be on the second opinion (Abu Yusuf's), but some sources say fatwa is on the opinion of the two (Sahibayn).

Key Terms
مجلس العقد (majlis al-'aqd): The council or session where the contract offer is made; acceptance must occur in this session according to some scholars.
فضولي (fuduli): A proxy who acts without prior authorization; the contract depends on ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the creditor does not accept in the council of contract, what is the ruling?
A) The kafalah is void (lam tan'aqid).
B) The kafalah is valid if ratified later.
C) The kafalah is valid but requires compensation.
D) The kafalah is valid only for property debts.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on acceptance timing in kafalah hinges on whether kafalah is a transfer of rights (tamlik) or a commitment (iltizam).
