Kafalah by Heir of a Sick Person – Ruling and Conditions
Ruling on kafalah by heir of a sick person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kafalah by the heir of a terminally ill person and identify the condition for its validity.

Core Idea
The heir's guarantee on behalf of a sick person is valid like a will, but only if the sick person has assets.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with a special guarantee (kafalah) where a terminally ill person instructs his heir to guarantee his debts. The scholars treat this as a will (wasiyyah) in effect, allowing it even without naming creditors, but it is valid only when the sick person has sufficient assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the heir guaranteeing the sick person's debts. The ruling is validity (sahih), as the passage states: 'innamā tasiḥḥu idhā kāna lahu māl'.
2
Locate the condition
The key condition is that the sick person must have assets (māl). Without assets, the kafalah is invalid.
3
Understand the legal basis
It functions like a will (wasiyyah) because it takes effect after death and clears liability. The heir stands in the place of the creditor.
The text likens it to a command for marriage.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Scholars differ on whether a stranger can do this. The stronger view (Fath al-Qadir) is that it is valid only for the heir.
Relied-upon position: valid for heir, not stranger.
5
Evaluate the benefit
Though the heir is already liable, this guarantee emphasizes the obligation and helps when assets are absent or disputed.
Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj notes it only adds emphasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إلا أن يكفل وارث المريض عنه) بأن يقول المريض لوارثه تكفل عني بما علي من الدين فكفل به مع غيبة الغرماء لأن ذلك وصية في الحقيقة ولذا تصح وإن لم يسم المكفول لهم ولهذا قالوا : إنما تصح إذا كان له مال أو يقال إنه قائم مقام الطالب لحاجته إليه تفريغاً لذمته وفيه نفع الطالب فصار كما إذا حضر بنفسه. وإنما يصح بهذا اللفظ ولا يشترط القبول لأنه يراد به التحقيق دون المساومة ظاهراً في هذه الحالة فصار كما إذا كفل بنفسه كالأمر بالنكاح قيد بالوارث لأن المريض لو قال ذلك لأجنبي اختلف المشايخ فيه فمنهم من قال بالجواز تنزيلاً للمريض منزلة الطالب، ومنهم من قال بعدمه لأن الأجنبي غير مطالب بقضاء دينه بلا التزام فكان المريض والصحيح سواء والأول أوجه؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وحقق أنها كفالة لكن يرد عليه توقفها على المال كما قدمناه. وقيد بالمريض لأن الصحيح لو قال ذلك لوارثه أو غيره لم يصح ومن هنا يقال إنها ليست كفالة من كل وجه لأنها لا تصح إلا إذا كان للمريض مال، فلو كانت كفالة مطلقاً لصحت مطلقاً وليست وصية من كل وجه لأنها لو كانت وصية مطلقاً لصح الأمر من الصحيح ولذا قال في معراج الدراية في تعليل الكتاب بأن ذلك وصية في الحقيقة نظر إذ لو كانت وصية حقيقة لما اختلف الحكم بين حالة الصحة وحالة المرض إلا أن يؤول بأنه في معنى الوصية في الحقيقة وفيه بعد. اهـ. وقد يقال : لا فائدة في هذه الكفالة لأن الوارث مطالب بقضاء دين الميت من مال الميت سواء قال له المريض تكفل عني أو لا، وإذا لم يكن له تركة لا مطالبة عليه، سواء قال له ذلك أم لا، فأي فائدة فيها؟ وقد وقع الاشتباه لعدم الإطلاع على نقل فيما إذا تكفل بعض الورثة بأمر المريض وكان له مال غائب، هل يطالب الكفيل بقضاء دين الميت من ماله ثم يرجع في التركة أو لا؟ ولهذا قال في السراج الوهاج : إن الورثة يطالبون بدين مورثهم بلا ضمان والضمان ما زاده إلا تأكيداً. وقيد في الهداية المسألة بأمر المريض لورثته لأن الورثة لو قالوا ضمناً للناس كل دين لهم عليك ولم يطلب إجازته أو رده اهـ.
His saying: (Except that the heir of a sick person guarantees on his behalf) – meaning that the sick person says to his heir: 'Guarantee for me what I owe of debt,' so he guarantees it in the absence of the creditors, because that is a will (wasiyyah) in reality. Therefore it is valid even if the creditors are not named. For this reason they said: It is only valid if he has assets. Or it is said that he (the heir) stands in the place of the creditor due to his need to clear his (the sick person's) liability, and there is benefit for the creditor, so it is as if the creditor himself were present. It is only valid with this wording, and acceptance is not stipulated because it is intended for fulfillment (tahqeeq) rather than bargaining in this situation, so it is as if he guaranteed himself, like the command for marriage. The restriction to the heir is because if the sick person said that to a stranger, the scholars differed: some said it is valid, treating the sick person as the creditor; others said it is not, because the stranger is not obligated to pay his debt without a commitment, so the sick person and the healthy are the same. The first is stronger. So in Fath al-Qadir. It is verified that it is a kafalah, but it is subject to the condition of having assets as we have mentioned. It is restricted to the sick person because if a healthy person said that to his heir or another, it is not valid. Hence it is said that it is not a full kafalah because it is only valid if the sick person has assets; if it were an absolute kafalah it would be valid absolutely. And it is not a full will because if it were an absolute will, the command from a healthy person would be valid. Therefore, in Mi'raj al-Dirayah regarding the explanation of the text, he said that it is a will in reality – there is consideration, for if it were truly a will, the ruling would not differ between health and sickness, unless it is interpreted as in the meaning of a will in reality, but that is far-fetched. End. It may be said: There is no benefit in this kafalah because the heir is obligated to pay the debt of the deceased from his assets whether or not the sick person told him to guarantee, and if there is no estate, there is no claim; so what benefit is there? This confusion arises from lack of knowledge of a report regarding when some heirs guarantee on the order of the sick person and he has absent assets: is the guarantor sued to pay the debt from his own wealth and then reimburse from the estate, or not? For this reason, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj said: The heirs are sued for the debt of their testator without guarantee, and the guarantee only emphasizes it. In al-Hidayah, the issue is restricted to the command of the sick person to his heirs, because if the heirs say 'we guarantee for the people every debt they have against you' and the creditor does not request ratification or rejection – end.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage deals with a special case: a terminally ill person (marid) instructs his heir to guarantee his debts in the absence of the creditors. The scholars consider this transaction to function like a wasiyyah (will), so it is valid even if the creditors are not named, provided the sick person has assets. The reasoning is that the heir stands in the place of the creditor to clear the deceased's liability. There is a condition that the sick person must have sufficient assets. The text distinguishes between a kafalah and a wasiyyah, noting that it partakes of both. It also addresses an objection that this kafalah seems pointless since the heir is already liable for debts from the estate. The response is that it emphasizes the obligation and is beneficial when assets are absent or disputed. The passage concludes with the effective ruling from al-Hidayah, restricting the case to the sick person's command to his heir.

Key Terms
كفالة الوارث (kafālat al-wārith): A guarantee given by the heir on behalf of a terminally ill person for his debts, treated as a will in effect.
وصية (waṣiyyah): A will or bequest; in this context, the kafalah is likened to a will because it takes effect after death and is limited to one-third of assets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the kafalah by the heir of a sick person valid?
A) The sick person must be healthy.
B) The sick person must have sufficient assets.
C) The creditors must be present.
D) The heir must be a stranger.

Key Takeaway
The heir's guarantee for a terminally ill person is valid as a will, conditional on the sick person having assets.

Guarantee by the Heir of a Patient During Illness
Explaining the validity and nature of kafalah by an heir in marad al-mawt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid guarantee (kafalah) by an heir during the patient's terminal illness and explain the Hanafi ruling on its nature (guarantee vs. bequest).

Core Idea
A guarantee by an heir during fatal illness is valid only if the patient has wealth and is treated like a bequest to relieve liability.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a unique scenario: a terminally ill patient asks his heir to guarantee his debts. The Hanafi school validates this under specific conditions, but its legal nature is debated—some consider it a guarantee, others a bequest. Understanding this case sharpens your grasp of how necessity (hajah) can transform a transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? The guarantee (kafalah) by the heir of a patient during terminal illness. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) but conditional.
2
Locate the conditions
The patient must be in terminal illness (marad al-mawt), must have wealth (mal), and the guarantor must be an heir (not a stranger).
3
Trace the legal basis
The validity is based on it being in reality a will (wasiyyah), since the patient intends to dispose of property after death to free his liability. The absence of creditors and lack of acceptance requirement also support this analogy.
4
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Later scholars differed: some consider it a guarantee, others a bequest. The passage discusses the difference and notes the fatwa position.
5
Determine the operative ruling
According to al-Bazzaziyya, the fatwa is on the opinion that it is a guarantee. However, Anfa' al-Wasa'il states the fatwa is on both opinions (the two companions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كفل بنفسه كالأمر بالنكاح قيد بالوارث لأن المريض لو قال ذلك لأجنبي اختلف المشايخ فيه فمنهم من قال بالجواز تنزيلاً للمريض منزلة الطالب، ومنهم من قال بعدمه لأن الأجنبي غير مطالب بقضاء دينه بلا التزام كفالة من كل وجه لأنها لا تصح إلا إذا كان للمريض مال، فلو كانت كفالة مطلقاً لصحت مطلقاً وليست وصية من كل وجه لأنها لو كانت وصية مطلقاً لصح الأمر من الصحيح ولذا قال في معراج الدراية في تعليل الكتاب بأن ذلك وصية في الحقيقة نظر إذ لو كانت وصية حقيقة لما اختلف الحكم بين حالة الصحة وحالة المرض إلا أن يؤول بأنه في معنى الوصية في الحقيقة وفيه بعد .اهـ. وقد يقال : لا فائدة في هذه الكفالة لأن الوارث مطالب بقضاء دين الميت من مال الميت سواء قال له المريض تكفل عني أو لا، وإذا لم يكن له تركة لا مطالبة عليه، سواء قال له ذلك أم لا، فأي فائدة فيها؟ وقد وقع الاشتباه لعدم الإطلاع على نقل فيما إذا تكفل بعض الورثة بأمر المريض وكان له مال غائب، هل يطالب الكفيل بقضاء دين الميت من ماله ثم يرجع في التركة أو لا؟ ولهذا قال في السراج الوهاج : إن الورثة يطالبون بدين مورثهم بلا ضمان والضمان ما زاده إلا تأكيدا. وقيد في الهداية المسألة بأمر المريض لورثته لأن الورثة لو قالوا ضمناً للناس كل دين لهم عليك ولم يطلب إجازته أو رده اهـ. قوله : ( وفي البزازية الفتوى على قول الثاني قال الرملي وفي أنفع الوسائل صرح بأن الفتوى على قولهما قوله : (وقد يقال لا فائدة في هذه الكفالة (الخ قال في النهر : قد يدفع بأن فائدتها تظهر في تفريغ ذمته قوله : (وقد وقع الاشتباه ابتداء كلام وقوله «لعدم الاطلاع على نقل» ثم قال: المريض ذلك منهم والغرماء غيب لم يصح، ولو قالوا ذلك بعد موته صحت الكفالة، وروي عن أبي حنيفة جواز كفالتهم في مرضه وإن لم يطلب المريض منهم ذلك؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج والخانية. وفي البدائع وأما مسألة المريض فقد قال بعض مشايخنا : إن جواز الضمان بطريق الإيصاء بالقضاء عنه بعد موته لا بطريق الكفالة، وبعضهم أجازوه على سبيل الكفالة . ووجه ما أشار إليه أبو حنيفة في الأصل وقال هو بمنزلة المعبر عن غرمائه وشرح هذه الإشارة والله أعلم أن المريض مرض الموت يتعلق الدين بماله ويصير بمنزلة الأجنبي عنه حتى لا ينفذ منه التصرف المبطل لحق الغريم، ولو قال أجنبي للورثة اضمنو الغرماء فلان عنه فقالوا ضمنا يكتفي به فكذا المريض اهـ
His statement: Except that the heir of the patient guarantees for him) such that the patient says to his heir: 'Guarantee for me what is due on me of debt,' so he guarantees it during the absence of the creditors, because that is in reality a will, and thus it is valid even if the guaranteed parties are not named. For this reason they said: It is only valid if he (the patient) has wealth, or it is said that he (the heir) stands in the place of the claimant due to his need to free his (the patient's) liability, and there is benefit for the claimant, so it becomes as if he (the claimant) had personally appeared. And it is only valid with this wording, and acceptance is not required because it is intended for verification, not negotiation, apparently in this situation, so it becomes as if he had guaranteed himself, like the command in marriage. It is restricted to the heir because if the patient said that to a stranger, the scholars differed: some said it is permissible considering the patient as standing in place of the claimant, and some said it is not because the stranger is not required to pay his debt without obligation, so the patient and the healthy person are the same, and the first is more appropriate; as in Fath al-Qadir. And it is verified that it is a guarantee, but it is objected that it depends on wealth as we have mentioned. And it is restricted to the patient because if the healthy person said that to his heir or another, it is not valid. Hence it is said that it is not a guarantee absolutely because it is only valid if the patient has wealth; if it were an absolute guarantee, it would be valid absolutely. And it is not a will absolutely because if it were an absolute will, the command from the healthy person would be valid. Therefore the author of Miraj al-Diraya said in explaining the book that it is in reality a will, but that is questionable, for if it were actually a will, the ruling would not differ between health and illness, unless it is interpreted that it is in the meaning of a will, but that is far-fetched. One might say: There is no benefit in this guarantee because the heir is required to pay the debt of the deceased from the deceased's wealth whether the patient said 'guarantee for me' or not. And if there is no estate, there is no claim against him regardless. So what benefit is there? And confusion has arisen due to lack of awareness of a transmission regarding when some heirs guarantee at the patient's command and he has absent wealth: Is the guarantor required to pay the debt of the deceased from his own wealth and then seek recourse from the estate or not? Therefore the author of Siraj al-Wahhaj said: The heirs are required to pay their testator's debt without guarantee, and the guarantee only adds emphasis. And in al-Hidayah, the issue is restricted to the patient's command to his heirs because if the heirs said: 'We guarantee to the people all the debt they have on you' and the creditors are absent, it is not valid. But if they said that after his death, the guarantee is valid. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa the permissibility of their guarantee during his illness even if the patient did not ask them that; as in Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Khaniyya. And in al-Bada'i: Regarding the issue of the patient, some of our scholars said: The permissibility of the guarantee is by way of bequest (al-isa') to pay off his debt after his death, not by way of guarantee. And some allowed it as a guarantee. And the indication of what Abu Hanifa pointed to in al-Asl is: He is like the representative of his creditors. The explanation of this indication: Allah knows best, that the patient in his fatal illness has his debt attached to his wealth and becomes like a stranger to it, such that his invalidating disposition does not take effect that harms the creditor's right. And if a stranger said to the heirs: 'Guarantee the creditors of so-and-so against him,' and they said 'we guarantee,' it suffices. So likewise the patient.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the ruling of a sick person (marid) asking his heir to guarantee his debts during his illness. The commentator explains that the validity is based on it being a kind of will (wasiyyah), since the patient's intention is to relieve his liability after death. The discussion covers conditions: it must be during the patient's illness, the patient must have wealth, and the guarantee must be by the heir (not a stranger). There is a debate among scholars about the exact nature: some consider it a guarantee proper, others a bequest. The passage also addresses a common doubt about the benefit of such a guarantee, since the heir is already liable to pay from the estate. The answer given is that the benefit lies in freeing the deceased's liability (tafriqh al-dhimmah) and that it provides security for creditors, especially when the deceased has absent assets. The text also references various authoritative sources like Fath al-Qadir, Miraj al-Diraya, Siraj al-Wahhaj, and al-Hidaya.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A guarantee or surety, a contract whereby a person assumes responsibility for a debt or obligation of another.
وارث (wārith): Heir; a person entitled to inherit the estate of the deceased.
مريض (marīḍ): A person who is ill, specifically with a terminal illness (marad al-mawt) in this context, where his debts attach to his wealth.
وصية (waṣiyyah): A will or bequest, a disposition of property to take effect after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the guarantee by the heir of a patient valid according to the majority of Hanafi scholars?
A) The patient must be healthy.
B) The patient must have wealth.
C) The heir must be a stranger.
D) The creditors must be present.

Key Takeaway
The guarantee by an heir during terminal illness is a valid transaction akin to a will, conditional on the patient having wealth, and it serves to free the deceased's liability.

Guarantee for a Deceased Insolvent: Dispute and Tarjih
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa declares the guarantee void and why the two companions consider it valid, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A guarantee for a deceased insolvent is valid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, despite Abu Hanifa's view.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of guaranteeing the debt of a person who dies insolvent. Abu Hanifa holds that the debt has fallen (sāqiṭ) and thus guaranteeing it is void, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue it remains and the guarantee is valid. The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position follows the two companions, supported by the hadith of Abu Qatada. We will explore the reasoning behind each view and understand the tarjih rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The core legal question: Is a guarantee (kafālah) valid if undertaken after the debtor's death when he leaves no estate?
The key variable is whether the debt still 'exists' in worldly legal terms.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa says the guarantee is void (bāṭil) because the debt has fallen (sāqiṭ) due to death and insolvency – the debtor is incapable of payment and no successor exists.
He considers the debt to be a 'real action' (fi'l) that cannot be performed after death.
3
State the Two Companions' Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the guarantee is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) because the debt is established (thābit) for the claimant's right and remains in the Hereafter; gratuitous payment is permissible.
They rely on the hadith of Abu Qatada and analogical reasoning (qiyās).
4
Analyze the Dalīl and Response
Both sides use the same hadith: Abu Hanifa interprets it as a prior guarantee or new undertaking, not a general rule; the companions take it as evidence of validity.
The Hanafi response uses the principle that a single incident does not establish generality (lā 'umūm lahā).
5
Determine the Muftā Bihī
The text indicates the fatwa is on the view of the two companions, as mentioned in al-Mirāj, due to protecting creditors' rights and the strength of the hadith.
Note the exception: if a debt arises after death (e.g., liability from digging a well), guarantee is valid even according to Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعن ميت مفلس أي وبطلت الكفالة عن ميت مفلس وهذا عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا . صحيحة لما روي أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتى بجنازة رجل من الأنصار فسأل هل عليه دين؟ قالوا: نعم در همان أو ديناران فامتنع من الصلاة فقال صلوا على أخيكم. فقام أو قتادة فقال : هما علي يا رسول الله فصلى عليه. ولأنه كفل بدين ثابت لأنه وجب لحق الطالب ولم يوجد المسقط ولهذا يبقى في حق أحكام الآخرة ، ولو تبرع به إنسان يصح ولذا يبقى إذا كان به كفيل. وله أنه كفل بدين ساقط لأن الدين هو الفعل حقيقة ولهذا يوصف بالوجوب لأنه في الحكم مال لأنه يؤول إليه في المآل وقد عجز بنفسه وبخلفه ففات عاقبة الاستيفاء فيسقط ضرورة والتبرع لا يعتمد قيام الدين. وإذا كان له كفيل أو له مال فخلفه إذ الإفضاء إلى الأداء باق. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الكفيل أجنبياً أو وارث الميت ولو ابنه؛ كذا في المعراج. والجواب عن الحديث أنه يحتمل الإقرار عن كفالة سابقة والإنشاء والوعد وحكاية الفعل لا عموم لها وقيد بالكفالة بعد موته لأنه لو كفل في حياته ثم مات مفلساً لم تبطل الكفالة، وكذا لو كان به رهن ثم مات مفلساً لا يبطل الرهن لأن سقوط الدين عنه في أحكام الدنيا في حقه للضرورة فتتقدر بقدرها فأبقيناه في حق الكفيل والرهن لعدم الضرورة؛ كذا في المعراج وبما قررناه علم أن الميت المفلس من مات ولا تركة له ولا كفيل عنه، ويستثنى من بطلانها مسألة في التحرير من بحث الموت من عوارض الأهلية لو تقوت الذمة بلحوق دين بعد الموت صحت الكفالة به بأن حفر بئراً على الطريق فتلف به حیوان بعد موته فإنه يثبت الدين مستنداً إلى وقت الحفر الثابت حال قيام الذمة والمستند يثبت أولاً في الحال، ويلزم اعتبار قوتها حينئذ به لكونه محل الاستيفاء اهـ
His statement: (And regarding a deceased insolvent) meaning: The guarantee for a deceased insolvent is void, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (companions) said: It is valid, because it is narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was brought a funeral of a man from the Ansar, and he asked: 'Does he have any debt?' They said: 'Yes, two dirhams or two dinars.' So he refrained from praying [over him] and said: 'Pray over your brother.' Then Abu Qatada stood up and said: 'They are upon me, O Messenger of Allah.' So he prayed over him. And because he guaranteed a debt that is established, for it was owed for the right of the claimant and no cancelling factor has occurred, therefore it remains for the purposes of the Hereafter. And if a person gratuitously pays it, it is valid, so it remains if there is a guarantor. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that he guaranteed a debt that has fallen (saqit) because the debt is in reality the action [of payment], and for this it is described as obligatory because it is considered wealth in the end, and the person has become incapable himself and by his successor, so the consequence of recovery is lost, so it falls necessarily, and gratuitous payment does not depend on the debt's existence. And if he has a guarantor or wealth, then his successor exists because the means of payment remains. He (the text) gave it absolutely, so it includes whether the guarantor is a stranger or the heir of the deceased, even his son; as in al-Miraj. And the answer to the hadith: It is possible (the report) indicates acknowledgment of a prior guarantee, or a new undertaking, or a promise, and the narration of a single action does not imply generality. And it is restricted to the guarantee after his death; if he guaranteed during his lifetime then died insolvent, the guarantee does not become void, and likewise if there was a pledge (rahn), then he died insolvent, the pledge is not void because the falling of the debt from him in the rulings of the world is out of necessity, so it is limited to its extent, so we keep it with respect to the guarantor and the pledge due to the absence of necessity; as in al-Miraj. And by what we have established, it is known that the deceased insolvent is one who dies with no estate and no guarantor for him. And there is an exception to its voidness: a case in al-Tahrir from the discussion of death from the impediments of legal capacity: if the liability becomes strong by a debt attaching after death, then the guarantee for it is valid, such as if he dug a well on the road and an animal perished by it after his death; then the debt is established based on the time of digging, which was when the liability was in existence, and it is established first in the present, and it is necessary to consider its strength then because it is the place of recovery.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a guarantee for a deceased insolvent person (mīt muflis). According to Abu Hanifa, such a guarantee is void because the debt of the deceased has fallen (saqit) by death and insolvency; the debt is considered non-existent in worldly matters, so guaranteeing it is like guaranteeing nothing. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue it is valid, relying on the hadith of Abu Qatada guaranteeing the debt of a deceased man from the Ansar, and also on analogical reasoning (qiyas) that the debt remains in the Hereafter and can be validly paid by a third party. The text also provides the Hanafi response to the hadith: it could be a prior guarantee or a new undertaking, and the single incident does not prove a general rule. The discussion also distinguishes cases where the debt was guaranteed before death or secured by a pledge, which are unaffected by the debtor's subsequent insolvency. Finally, an exception is noted: if a debt arises after death (e.g., liability for digging a well), the guarantee for such a debt is valid as the debt is based on an act when the deceased was alive.

Key Terms
ميت مفلس (mīt muflis): A deceased person who leaves no estate (tarikah) and has no guarantor (kafil) for his debts.
كفالة (kafālah): Guarantee; a contract where a person assumes responsibility for a debt or obligation of another.
ساقط (sāqiṭ): Fallen, void; refers to a debt that is no longer enforceable in worldly courts due to the debtor's death and insolvency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on guaranteeing the debt of a deceased insolvent person?
A) Void, because the debt has fallen.
B) Valid, because the debt remains established.
C) Void, unless the guarantor is an heir.
D) Valid only if the guarantee was made before death.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi position holds that a guarantee for a deceased insolvent is valid, provided the debt is considered established, and this is supported by the hadith of Abu Qatada and practical necessity.

Voidness of Agent's and Mudarib's Guarantee for the Price
Ruling that both agent and mudarib cannot guarantee the price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an agent's or mudarib's guarantee of the price is void and explain the underlying fiduciary principle.

Core Idea
An agent's guarantee of the price is void because it exceeds his fiduciary role as an amīn.

This lesson examines a concise but important ruling from Kanz al-Daqā'iq: the guarantee (kafālah) given by an agent (wakīl) to his principal (muwakkil) regarding the price (thaman) of a sale is void. The same applies to a mudarib's guarantee for the capital owner (rabb al-māl). We will explore why this is the Hanafi position and what it reveals about the legal capacity of fiduciaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved in the ruling
Recognise the agent (wakīl) and principal (muwakkil), and the mudarib and capital owner (rabb al-māl).
2
State the ruling clearly
The guarantee of the agent for the price owed to the principal is void. Similarly for the mudarib.
3
Understand the legal relationship
Both agent and mudarib are fiduciaries (amīn). They act on behalf of the principal but do not assume personal liability for debts.
4
Apply the same principle to mudarib
The mudarib is like an agent; his guarantee of the price for the capital owner is also void.
5
Note the practical effect
The principal cannot enforce such a guarantee and must look to the actual debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبالثمن للموكل ولرب المال به أي وبطلت كفالة الوكيل لموكله بالثمن وكفالة المضارب لرب المال بالثمن فيما باعه تعليل لوقوع الاشتباه وقوله «فيما إذا تكفل» متعلق بالاشتباه أو بوقع وقوله «هل يطالب الخ» قال في النهر : ينبغي على أنه وصية أن ينتظره وعلى أنها كفالة أن يلزم الكفيل بالدفع الآن.
His statement: (And regarding the price, for the principal and for the owner of the capital) meaning: The guarantee of the agent for his principal regarding the price, and the guarantee of the mudarib for the owner of the capital regarding the price in what he sold, are void. Explanation of the occurrence of confusion: And his statement 'in what if he guarantees' is related to the confusion or to occurrence. And his statement 'is he demanded?' (Answered) in al-Nahr: It should be, considering it a will, that he waits, and considering it a guarantee, that the guarantor is obliged to pay now.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This brief passage states a clear ruling: the guarantee (kafālah) given by an agent (wakīl) for his principal (muwakkil) regarding the price (thaman) of a sale is void (bāṭil). Similarly, a mudarib's guarantee for the capital owner (rabb al-mal) regarding the price is also void. The reasoning is that such a guarantee would be a form of guarantee for a debt that is not the guarantor's own and involves potential conflict of interest or lack of capacity. The commentary then connects this to the earlier discussion about the patient's heir, indicating that the confusion (ishtibāh) about benefit is similar: if the guarantee is treated as a will, payment is deferred; if as a guarantee, payment is immediate. The purpose is to clarify the distinction.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; a person authorized by another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the monetary consideration in a sale contract.
مضارب (muḍārib): The working partner in a mudarabah (profit-sharing) contract; he invests the capital of the rabb al-mal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of a proxy's (wakīl) guarantee for the price (thaman) owed to his principal?
A) Valid because the agent is acting for the principal.
B) Void because the agent cannot guarantee the price.
C) Valid only if the principal authorizes it.
D) Void only if the agent is not a relative.

Key Takeaway
An agent's or mudarib's guarantee of the price is void because they are fiduciaries and cannot assume personal liability for debts of their principals.

Validity of a Partner's Guarantee in a Joint Sale
Commentary on an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a partner's guarantee in a joint sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a partner's guarantee for a co-partner in a joint sale, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
A partner's guarantee for his co-partner in a joint sale is invalid due to division of debt before receipt, unless the sale is treated as separate transactions.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a partner's guarantee for the other partner's share of the price when they jointly sell a slave in one transaction. While Abu Hanifa strictly invalidates such a guarantee unless each sale is distinct, the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it if the price for each share is separately specified. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the concepts of joint ownership, debt division, and the structure of the sale.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that if two partners sell a jointly owned slave in one transaction (safqah), a guarantee by one partner for the other's share of the price is invalid. The guarantee is only valid if they sell in separate transactions, requiring the word 'bought' to be repeated.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the guarantee is valid if the price for each share is separately specified, even if the sale is in one contract. They do not require repeating 'bought'.
Their view is based on the fact that specification of price distinguishes the shares.
3
Analyse the reasoning for invalidity
Abu Hanifa's reason: In a joint sale, the price is a joint debt. The guarantor would be guaranteeing his own share (since the debt is not yet divided). Also, it leads to division of debt before receipt, which is prohibited.
Division of debt before receipt is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding debts.
4
Analyse the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that specifying the price for each share removes the joint nature. Each share's price becomes a separate debt, so the guarantee is for a distinct debt, not a division of a joint debt before receipt.
They consider the specification as making the sale effectively two transactions.
5
Determine the practical difference
In a joint sale, if a partner guarantees the other's share, according to Abu Hanifa the guarantee is void and the guarantor owes nothing. According to the Sahibayn, the guarantee is valid and the guarantor must pay the co-partner's share if the buyer defaults.
The mufta bihi is not specified here; later Hanafi works may give preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وللشريك إذا بيع عبد صفقة) أي وبطل كفالة الشريك لشريكه عن المشتري الثمن فيما إذا باعا شيئاً مشتركاً عقداً واحداً لأنه حصته ضامناً لنفسه لأنه ما من جزء يؤديه المشتري أو الكفيل من الثمن إلا وهو مشترك بينهما، ولأنه يؤدي إلى قسمة الدين قبل قبضه وأنه لا يجوز قيد بقوله صفقة واحدة لأنهما لو باعاه صفقتين بأن سمى كل واحد منهما لنصيبه ثمناً ضمان أحدهما نصيب الآخر لامتياز نصيب كل منهما فلا شركة بدليل صح أن له قبول نصيب أحدهما دون الآخر، ولو قبل الكل ونقد حصة أحدهما كان للناقد قبض نصيبه، ولهذا لو استوفى أحدهما نصيبه من المشتري فلا شركة للآخر بخلاف ما إذا صفقة بيع فإنه يشارك، وقد اعتبروا هنا لتعدد الصفقة تفصيل الثمن وذكروا في البيوع أن هذا قولهما، وأما قول أبي حنيفة فلا بد من تكرار لفظ بعت ولو قال المصنف وللشريك بدين مشترك وحذف قوله فيما إذا بيع عبد صفقة لكان أولى لما في الخانية : رجلان لهما على رجل دين فكفل أحدهما لصاحبه بحصته من الدين لا تصح كفالته، ولو تبرع أحدهما بأداء نصيب صاحبه من الدين كان جائزاً، وكذا الرجل إذا مات وله دين على رجل وترك ابنين فكفل أحدهما لأخيه عن المديون بحصة أخيه لا تصح الكفالة ولو تبرع أحدهما فأدى حصة صاحبه
His statement: '(And for the partner when a slave is sold in one lot)' i.e., the guarantee of a partner for his co-partner from the buyer for the price is invalid in the case where they sell something jointly in one contract because he is guaranteeing his own share, because there is no part of the price that the buyer or the guarantor pays except that it is shared between them, and because it leads to division of debt before its receipt, which is not permissible. It is qualified by his statement 'one lot' because if they sell in two lots, by each naming a price for his share, the guarantee of one for the other's share is valid due to the distinction of each share, so there is no sharing, as evidenced by the validity of accepting one share without the other. If he accepts all and pays the price of one share, the payer may take possession of his share, and if one collects his share from the buyer, there is no sharing for the other, unlike the case of one lot where he would share. They considered here that for multiple transactions, specification of the price suffices, and they mentioned in the sales that this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for Abu Hanifa's view, it is necessary to repeat the word 'bought'. It would have been better if the author said: 'And for the partner with a joint debt' and omitted 'when a slave is sold in one lot', as in al-Khaniyya: Two men have a debt on a man, and one of them guarantees his partner's share of the debt; his guarantee is not valid. If one voluntarily pays his partner's share of the debt, it is permissible. Similarly, if a man dies and has a debt on a man and leaves two sons, and one of them guarantees his brother's share from the debtor; the guarantee is not valid, but if one voluntarily pays his brother's share, it is valid.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of a partner's guarantee for his co-partner's share of the price when they sell a jointly owned item in one transaction. The ruling is that such a guarantee is invalid because the guarantor would be guaranteeing his own share of the price, leading to a division of debt before receipt. The condition for validity is that the sale be in separate transactions (two lots), each with a specified price, in which case the guarantee is valid. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that separate specification of the price suffices, while Abu Hanifa requires repeating the word 'bought' to indicate distinct sales. The author suggests an alternative phrasing to avoid confusion. The text also cites examples from al-Khaniyya: if two partners have a joint debt on a third, one cannot guarantee the other's share; but voluntary payment without prior guarantee is valid.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A single transaction (sale) involving the entire property.
قسمة الدين قبل قبضه (qismat al-dayn qabla qabdihi): Division of a debt before it is received/collected, which is prohibited in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, how can a partner's guarantee for the co-partner's share be valid in a joint sale?
A) By the word 'bought' being repeated
B) By specifying the price for each share separately
C) By the buyer consenting
D) By the debt being already divided

Key Takeaway
In a joint sale, a partner's guarantee for the other's share is invalid due to debt division concerns, but it can be valid if the sale is treated as separate transactions by specifying each share's price.

Invalidity of Master's Guarantee for Slave's Debt Due to Tuhma
Branch ruling on invalidity of guarantee due to suspicion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's guarantee for his freed slave's debt and explain the rationale based on tuhma (suspicion of self-interest).

Core Idea
A master's guarantee for his freed slave's debt is invalid because he is suspected of benefiting himself.

This lesson examines a specific branch ruling in Hanafi fiqh of suretyship (kafalah). When a master frees his indebted slave, the master becomes liable to creditors for the slave's value. If the master later guarantees the slave's debt, this seemingly helpful act is invalid. The reason is that the master's own liability creates a conflict of interest, leading to suspicion (tuhma) that he is trying to free himself from his obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
A man frees his indebted slave. The master becomes liable to creditors for the slave's value, while the slave remains liable for the full debt.
The master's liability is for the slave's value, not the debt itself.
2
Recognize the master's existing liability
The master already owes the creditors the slave's value. If he guarantees the slave's debt, he would effectively owe the same amount twice, but the guarantee could offset his own liability.
This is the key conflict.
3
Understand the principle of tuhma
Tuhma means legal suspicion. Here, the master is suspected of using the guarantee to indirectly free himself from his own liability to the creditors.
Hanafi law invalidates contracts tainted by such self-interest.
4
Apply the principle and state the ruling
Because of the tuhma, the guarantee is fasid (invalid). The master cannot guarantee a debt for which he is already liable.
The ruling protects creditors from circular arrangements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وذكر الشارح هنا فرعاً : رجل أعتق عبده المدين حتى لزمه ضمان قيمته للغرماء ولزم العبد جميع الدين ثم إن المولى ضمن الدين للغرماء فإن لا يصح لأن المولى متهم فيه بإبراء نفسه اهـ
His statement: (And the commentator mentioned here a branch:) A man freed his indebted slave such that he became liable for guaranteeing his value to the creditors, and the slave became liable for all the debt. Then the master guaranteed the debt to the creditors – it is not valid because the master is suspected of using it to free himself.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This is a branch ruling illustrating a principle in suretyship. When a master frees his slave who is in debt, the master becomes liable to creditors for the slave's value (because the slave's freedom affects his ability to pay). If the master then guarantees the slave's debt, this guarantee is invalid. The reason is that the master is accused of self-interest: by guaranteeing the debt, he would effectively be paying himself, since he is already liable for the slave's value. It is a case where the guarantor's intention is tainted by a conflict of interest.

Key Terms
المولى (al-mawla): The master who frees the slave.
المدين (al-madin): The slave who owes debt.
إبراء نفسه (ibra' nafsihi): Freeing himself (from liability) by using the guarantee to offset his own obligation to pay the slave's value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the master's guarantee of the slave's debt invalid in this scenario?
A) Because the slave is not free
B) Because the master is suspected of freeing himself from liability
C) Because the debt is not yet due
D) Because the guarantee was not in writing

Key Takeaway
A master cannot guarantee a freed slave's debt if the master already owes the creditors the slave's value, due to the principle of tuhma (suspicion).

Invalidity of Surety for 'Uhdah Due to Ambiguity
Ruling that a guarantee for 'uhdah is invalid due to ambiguity, with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a kafala bil-'uhdah is void in the Hanafi school and distinguish it from a valid guarantee of eviction (damān al-darak).

Core Idea
A guarantee with an ambiguous term is void because liability cannot be established on doubt.

In this passage, al-Haskafi explains the invalidity of a guarantee made using the term 'uhdah, which is ambiguous and can refer to different things. Because the exact obligation is uncertain, the contract cannot be executed and is ruled void (bāṭil). The author contrasts this with the valid guarantee of eviction (ضمان الدرك), which is specific.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal act is being discussed: kafala bil-'uhdah (guarantee using the term 'uhdah). The ruling is that it is invalid (bāṭil).
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
The term 'uhdah is ambiguous; it can mean the old document, the contract, its rights, the guarantee of eviction, or the option condition. This ambiguity leads to jahl (ignorance), which invalidates the contract because the obligation cannot be determined.
List the possible meanings: old document, contract, rights, eviction guarantee, option.
3
Examine the counterargument
One might argue that the term should be interpreted as the valid guarantee of eviction (daman al-darak) to validate the contract.
This is a natural objection - why not save the contract by interpretation?
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the default assumption is that the guarantor's liability is clear (free of debt). Obligation cannot be established based on doubt or probability. Therefore, the ambiguous term cannot be reinterpreted after the fact to create a valid obligation.
Key principle: al-aṣl barā'at al-dhimmah (default: liability is clear).
5
Note the practical implication
If the guarantor later clarifies that they meant something other than daman al-darak, the guarantee is still not validated because the initial contract was void from the start due to ambiguity.
Clarification after the contract does not cure the defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبالعهدة) أي وبطلت الكفالة بالعهدة لاشتباه المراد بها لإطلاقها على الصك القديم وعلى العقد وعلى حقوقه وعلى الدرك وعلى خيار الشرط فتعذر العمل بها قبل البيان فبطل للجهالة بخلاف ضمان الدرك، ولا يقال أن ينبغي يصر رف إلى ما يجوز الضمان به وهو الدرك تصحيحاً لتصرفه لأنا نقول : فراغ الذمة أصل فلا يثبت الشغل بالشك والاحتمال، وظاهر كلامهم أن الضامن إذا فسرها بغير ضمان الدرك لم يصح، ولو كان الصك القديم لقولهم إنه ملك البائع
His statement: (And by 'uhdah) i.e., the guarantee by 'uhdah is invalid because the intended meaning is ambiguous, as it is applied to the old document, the contract, its rights, the guarantee of eviction, and the option condition. So it cannot be acted upon before clarification, so it is void due to ignorance (jahl), unlike the guarantee of eviction. And it cannot be said that it should be diverted to what is valid for guarantee, namely eviction, to validate the transaction, because we say: The basis is that the liability is clear (discharge of debt is the default), so obligation cannot be established by doubt and probability. The apparent view of the scholars is that if the guarantor interprets it as other than the guarantee of eviction, it is not valid, even if it is the old document according to their statement that it is the seller's ownership.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage explains the invalidity of kafala bil-'uhdah (guarantee for warranty). The term 'uhdah is ambiguous, as it can refer to the old sale document, the contract, its rights, the guarantee against eviction (ضمان الدرك), or the option condition. Due to this ambiguity, the contract of guarantee cannot be executed without clarification, so it is void for ignorance (al-jahl). This is unlike ضمان الدرك (guarantee of eviction), which is a specific and valid form of guarantee. One might argue that the ambiguous term should be interpreted as ضمان الدرك to validate the contract, but the response is that the original state of the guarantor is free of obligation, and obligation cannot be established through doubt. If the guarantor later clarifies that he meant something other than ضمان الدرك, the guarantee is not validated.

Key Terms
العهدة (al-'uhdah): A term used for warranty in sales; ambiguous as it can refer to the document, contract, rights, eviction guarantee, or option.
الدرك (al-darak): Guarantee against eviction (if the sold item is reclaimed by a third party).
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Ignorance (uncertainty) that invalidates a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafala bil-'uhdah invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the term is too specific
B) Because it leads to uncertainty (jahl) about the obligation
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah
D) Because it requires the guarantor to be present

Key Takeaway
A guarantee contract requires a clear, unambiguous obligation; otherwise it is void due to ignorance (jahl).

The Validity of Kafala bil-Khulas: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafala bil-khulas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding kafala bil-khulas and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each interpretation.

Core Idea
The validity of a guarantee for deliverance turns on whether 'khulas' means absolute delivery or an option to deliver or refund.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the validity of kafala bil-khulas (guarantee for deliverance). The disagreement hinges on the interpretation of the term 'khulas'. You will learn how a definitional difference leads to different rulings and why the Sahibayn's view has practical significance.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa: the guarantee by khulas is invalid (batil). Note his reasoning: khulas means absolute delivery of the sold item, which is impossible if a rightful claimant exists.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the guarantee is valid (sahih). Their reasoning: khulas can be interpreted as either delivering the item if possible or returning the price if not, which is feasible and similar to daman al-darak (eviction guarantee).
3
Follow the reasoning of each
Trace the linguistic interpretation: Abu Hanifa takes the literal meaning, while the Sahibayn allow a more practical interpretation. Note that the Sahibayn's interpretation renders the contract possible and thus valid.
The dispute is purely about interpretation, not about a fundamental principle.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, if someone guarantees 'khulas' without further specification, the contract is void. According to the Sahibayn, it is valid and imposes an obligation to either deliver or refund.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
While the passage does not state which view is relied upon for fatwa, later Hanafi practice typically adopts the Sahibayn's view because it aligns with custom (urf) and avoids voiding contracts. However, the ikhtilaf remains.
When the guarantee explicitly mentions delivery or refund, it is valid by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والخلاص) أي وبطلت الكفالة بالخلاص وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : هي صحيحة بناء على تفسيرها بتخليص المبيع إن قدر عليه ورد الثمن إن لم يقدر عليه وهو ضمان الدرك في المعنى، وأبو حنيفة فسره بتخليص المبيع لا محالة ولا قدرة له عليه لأن المستحق لا يمكنه منه . ولو ضمن تخليص المبيع أو رد الثمن جاز لإمكان الوفاء به وهو تسليمه إن أجاز المستحق أو رده إن لم يجز فالخلاف راجع إلى التفسير
His statement: (And by khulas) i.e., the guarantee by khulas is invalid. This is according to Abu Hanifa, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is valid, based on interpreting it as delivering the sold item if able, and returning the price if not able, and that is essentially the guarantee of eviction. Abu Hanifa interprets it as delivering the sold item absolutely, and he has no ability to do so because the rightful claimant cannot be removed. But if he guarantees delivery of the sold item or return of the price, it is valid because fulfillment is possible, by handing it over if the claimant ratifies, or returning it if not. So the dispute goes back to interpretation.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding kafala bil-khulas (guarantee for deliverance). Abu Hanifa considers it invalid because the term 'khulas' means to deliver the sold item to the buyer, which is impossible if a third party claims ownership. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) interpret it as either delivering the item if possible or returning the price if not, which is effectively the same as ضمان الدرك (eviction guarantee), and thus valid. The practical difference is that according to Abu Hanifa, if a person guarantees 'khulas', the guarantee is void, while according to the Sahibayn, it is valid. However, if the guarantor explicitly guarantees delivery or refund, that is valid by consensus, because fulfillment is possible.

Key Terms
الخلاص (al-khulas): Guarantee for deliverance of the sold item or return of the price.
تخليص المبيع (takhlees al-mabee'): Delivering the sold item from a claim to the buyer.
المستحق (al-mustahiqq): The rightful claimant (third party with a right to the item).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is kafala bil-khulas invalid?
A) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
B) Because it requires delivering the item, which is impossible
C) Because the term is ambiguous like 'uhdah
D) Because it is only valid in writing

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafala bil-khulas depends entirely on the interpretation of 'khulas': literal (impossible) or practical (possible).

Invalidity of Kafala bi Badal al-Kitabah
Ruling with brief reason: invalidity due to tanafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kafala bi badal al-kitabah and explain why it is invalid due to contradiction (tanafi).

Core Idea
Guaranteeing the kitabah price is invalid because it creates a legal contradiction with the slave's unfree status.

In the Hanafi school, not every debt can be guaranteed. The case of a mukatab slave is unique: his obligation to pay for freedom coexists with his status as property. This creates a conflict when someone tries to guarantee that obligation. This short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the ruling, contrasting it with the valid guarantee for the substitute of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of substitute
The passage contrasts two debts: badal al-kitabah (the substitute of the contract of emancipation) and badal al-'itq (the substitute of emancipation after freedom).
2
State the ruling for each
Kafala bi badal al-kitabah is invalid; kafala bi badal al-'itq is valid. The reason lies in the timing and status of the debtor.
3
Understand the illah (reason): tanafi
The invalidity is due to 'tanafi' (internal contradiction). Guaranteeing the kitabah price would mean the guarantor is responsible for a debt owed by a slave who is still partly owned; this creates a legal contradiction. For badal al-'itq, the debt arises after the slave becomes fully free, so no contradiction exists.
Tanafi is the key legal concept: a guarantee cannot exist if it conflicts with the status of the principal debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وببدل (الكتابة لما قدمناه أول الباب قيد ببدل الكتابة لأن بدل العتق تجوز الكفالة به لأنه دين وجب عليه بعد الحرية فلا يؤدي إلى التنافي .
His statement: (And by the substitute of kitabah) – as we presented at the beginning of the chapter. It is qualified by the substitute of kitabah because for the substitute of emancipation, surety is allowed because it is a debt that becomes obligatory after freedom, so it does not lead to contradiction.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage states that kafala bi badal al-kitabah (guarantee for the substitute of a contract of emancipation) is invalid. The reason refers to an earlier explanation at the beginning of the book: guaranteeing the kitabah price would create a contradiction because while the slave is still in kitabah (earning freedom by paying installments), he is still partly property; guaranteeing that debt would lead to inconsistency (the guarantor technically guarantees something that could conflict with the slave's status). In contrast, guaranteeing the substitute of emancipation (بدل العتق) is valid because that debt is incurred after the slave becomes free, so there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The substitute price that a mukatab slave agrees to pay for his freedom.
بدل العتق (badal al-'itq): A debt incurred after emancipation, e.g., a loan taken by the freed slave.
التنافي (al-tanafi): Legal contradiction or inconsistency that invalidates the transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafala bi badal al-kitabah invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the amount is unknown
B) Because it leads to a contradiction in the slave's status
C) Because the slave is not yet free
D) Because the term is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Kafala bi badal al-kitabah is invalid due to legal contradiction; only debts arising after freedom can be guaranteed.

Kafalah — Surety

5 min
Debtor Cannot Recover Money Given to Surety Before Payment
Ruling with proof using analogy and legal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a debtor cannot recover money given to a surety before payment and explain the reasoning based on the surety's contingent right and the analogy to advance zakat.

Core Idea
A debtor cannot revoke payment to a surety while the surety may still use it to pay the debt.

In suretyship (kafalah), the relationship between debtor, surety, and creditor has specific rules. This lesson examines a key ruling: if the debtor gives money to the surety before the surety pays the creditor, the debtor cannot demand it back. The ruling rests on the surety's contingent right and the principle that once money is handed over for a purpose, it cannot be unilaterally reclaimed while the purpose remains possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states: if a debtor gives money to the surety before the surety pays the creditor, the debtor cannot recover its equivalent. The word 'مثله' (its equivalent) refers to the same amount.
The ruling is about revocability, not validity of the payment.
2
Understand the rationale
The surety's right becomes attached to the money because of the possibility that he will use it to pay the debt. As long as this possibility exists, the debtor cannot reclaim it.
The right is contingent on the surety's ability to pay.
3
Examine the analogy to zakat
The ruling is analogous to someone who pays zakat in advance to the collector. Once given, the payer cannot recover it because the collector's right is established.
The analogy shows that once a right attaches to a payment, it is irrevocable.
4
Distinguish ownership from agency
The author notes the ruling applies whether the payment is a transfer of ownership or as an agency (trust). In the agency case, the surety holds it as a trust, but still the debtor cannot recover while the possibility of payment remains.
The difference is the surety's legal capacity: owner vs trustee.
5
Recognize the broader principle
The principle is that a payment made for a specific purpose cannot be unilaterally revoked while that purpose remains possible. This protects the surety's ability to fulfill the obligation.
This aligns with Hanafi fiqh's emphasis on protecting contractual expectations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
قوله: ( ولو أعطى المطلوب الكفيل قبل أن يعطي الكفيل الطالب لا يسترد مثله) لأنه تعلق به حق القابض على احتمال قضائه الدين فلا تجوز المطالبة ما بقي هذا الاحتمال كمن عجل زكاته ودفعها إلى الساعي ولأنه ملكه بالقبض على ما نذكر أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الدفع على وجه الرسالة فلا يسترد لكنه لا يملكه بالقبض لتمحضة أمانة في يده، والفرق
قوله: (أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الدين على وجه الرسالة الخ قال في النهر : شمول كلام
Section: His statement: (And if the debtor gives to the surety before the surety gives to the creditor, he does not recover its equivalent.) Because the right of the recipient (the surety) becomes attached to it on the possibility of his paying the debt, so claiming (it back) is not permissible as long as this possibility remains, like one who pays his zakat in advance and gives it to the collector. And because he owns it by taking possession, as we mentioned. He (the author) made it general, so it includes the case where the payment is by way of agency, so he does not recover it, but he does not own it by taking possession because it is a pure trust in his hand, and the difference is... His statement: (He made it general so it includes the case where the debt is by way of agency...) said in al-Nahr: The inclusiveness of his statement...
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling: if a debtor gives money to the surety before the surety pays the creditor, the debtor cannot demand the money back. The reason is that the surety's right to hold the money is tied to the possibility that he will use it to pay the debt to the creditor. As long as that possibility exists, the debtor cannot claim a refund. This is analogous to someone who pays zakat in advance to the collector; the zakat is not recoverable after payment. The author notes that this ruling applies whether the payment is intended as a transfer of ownership or as an agency (the debtor appoints the surety as agent to pay). In the agency case, the surety holds the money as a trust (amanah), so the debtor does not own it but cannot recover it either while the possibility of payment remains. The difference between the two cases is elaborated elsewhere.

Key Terms
المطلوب (al-matloob): The debtor (the person from whom the debt is sought).
القابض (al-qabid): The recipient (here, the surety who receives the money).
الرسالة (al-risalah): Agency (the debtor appoints the surety as an agent to pay).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if the debtor gives money to the surety before the surety pays the creditor?
A) The debtor can recover it at any time
B) The debtor cannot recover it while the possibility of payment remains
C) The debtor can recover it if the surety has not paid within a month
D) The debtor can only recover it with the creditor's permission

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a debtor cannot reclaim money given to a surety before the surety pays, because the surety's right to hold the money is contingent on the possibility of paying the debt.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
15 min
Rulings on Debtor's Payment to Surety, Invalid Suretyship, and Nature of Kafala
Expanding on rulings of kafala from Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between iqtida' and risala when a debtor pays a surety, explain why suretyship by 'uhda and 'khalas' is invalid, and state the nature of kafala as creating two debts and three claims.

Core Idea
In Hanafi kafala, the surety's ownership of money depends on the debtor's intent; profit is halal only if received as compensation, not as a trust.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on critical rulings in the chapter of Kafala from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We will examine when a debtor's payment to a surety before paying the creditor is binding, which types of suretyship are invalid due to ambiguity, and the underlying legal structure of kafala as creating multiple debts and claims. Mastering these distinctions is essential for correctly advising on suretyship transactions.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the debtor's payment to the surety
Identify the two categories of payment: iqtida' (compensation) where the surety owns the money, and risala (trust) where the surety is a mere agent. Note the difference in ownership and permissibility of profit.
The author emphasizes that the surety owns the money in iqtida' and does not in risala, affecting the ruling on profit.
2
Examine invalid forms of suretyship
Study the rulings on suretyship by 'uhda (ambiguity) and 'khalas' (delivery of a sold item). Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'khalas' between Abu Hanifa and the two students.
The invalidity stems from jahala (ignorance) that makes the contract impossible to fulfill without clarification.
3
Understand the nature of kafala
Review the author's point that kafala with command creates two debts (creditor against debtor, surety against debtor) and three claims (creditor against both, surety against debtor). Recognize how this does not contradict the definition of kafala as joining liabilities.
This structure explains why the surety can take a mortgage from the debtor before paying.

ولو أعطى المطلوب الكفيل قبل أن يعطي الكفيل الطالب لا من الدين صح تبرعه وهو بمنزلة الوكيل بالبيع إذا كفل بالثمن عن المشتري لا تصح كفالته ولو تبرع بأداء الثمن عن المشتري صح تبرعه .اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: لهما دين مشترك على آخر فضمن أحدهما نصيب صاحبه لم يجز فيرجع بما أدى بخلاف ما لو أداه من غير سبق ضمان فإنه لا يرجع بما أدى، ولو توى نصيبه على المديون مر في مسائل التركة. وفي صورة يرجع بما دفع إذ قضاه على فساد فيرجع كما لو أدى بكفالة فسادة، ونظيره لو كفل ببدل الكتابة لم تصح فيرجع بما أدى إذا حسب أنه مجبر على ذلك لضمانه السابق، وبمثله لو أدى من غير سبق ضمان لا يرجع لتبرعه، وكذا وكيل البيع إذا ضمن الثمن لموكله لم يجز فيرجع، ولو أدى بغير ضمان جاز ولا يرجع اهـ قوله : (وبالعهدة) أي وبطلت الكفالة بالعهدة لاشتباه المراد بها لإطلاقها على الصك القديم وعلى العقد وعلى حقوقه وعلى الدرك وعلى خيار الشرط فتعذر العمل بها قبل البيان فبطل للجهالة بخلاف ضمان الدرك، ولا يقال أن ينبغي يصرح إلى ما يجوز الضمان به وهو الدرك تصحيحاً لتصرفه لأنا نقول : فراغ الذمة أصل فلا يثبت الشغل بالشك والاحتمال، وظاهر كلامهم أن الضامن إذا فسرها بغير ضمان الدرك لم يصح، ولو كان الصك القديم لقولهم إنه ملك البائع قوله : (والخلاص) أي وبطلت الكفالة بالخلاص وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : هي صحيحة بناء على تفسيرها بتخليص المبيع إن قدر عليه ورد الثمن إن لم يقدر عليه وهو ضمان الدرك في المعنى، وأبو حنيفة فسره بتخليص المبيع لا محالة ولا قدرة له عليه لأن المستحق لا يمكنه منه . ولو ضمن تخليص المبيع أو رد الثمن جاز لإمكان الوفاء به وهو تسليمه إن أجاز المستحق أو رده إن لم يجز فالخلاف راجع إلى التفسير قوله : ( وببدل الكتابة لما قدمناه أول الباب قيد ببدل الكتابة لأن بدل العتق تجوز الكفالة به لأنه دين وجب عليه بعد الحرية فلا يؤدي إلى التنافي .
