Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of another matn ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It distinguishes two types of suretyship: (1) the guarantor says 'I guarantee by the order of the principal' (kafāla bi-amrihī), and (2) without order. In the first case, judgment is against both the guarantor and the principal; in the second, only against the guarantor. The reasoning is that the guarantee with order is initially a donation but becomes a compensated transaction because the guarantor can seek recourse from the principal. Without order, it remains a pure donation. The commentator then discusses the objection of Imam Zufar, a Hanafi jurist, who argued that if the guarantor denies (perhaps denies being a guarantor or denies the debt?), he has wronged, so he should not be allowed to wrong others by having recourse? The Hanafi response is that the guarantor's denial makes him legally discredited (mukadhdhab shar'an), so his claim is invalid. The passage then ties back to the earlier ruling: the condition for a valid guarantee is that the debt must be decreed after the guarantee. The difference between the case of al-Fusul (where evidence was accepted) and the current case is that in al-Fusul, the claimant explicitly proved that the debt was established by a judge's ruling after the guarantee, so the guarantor becomes a litigant. Here, without that specification, the guarantor is not a litigant. The passage ends with a further explanation of the principle that when a litigant is discredited by law, his subsequent contradictory claims are not considered.

Key Terms
الكفالة بالأمر (al-kafāla bi-l-amr): Suretyship with the order of the principal; the guarantor acts at the principal's request, entitling him to recourse.
الكفالة بغير أمر (al-kafāla bi-ghayr amr): Suretyship without the principal's order; a pure donation; the guarantor cannot seek recourse.
مكذب شرعاً (mukadhdhab shar'an): Legally discredited; a person whose claim is rejected by law due to contradiction or denial in a matter where the law has already established the opposite.
زفر (Zufar): Imam Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a major Hanafi jurist and student of Abu Hanifa, known for his independent opinions within the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what is the effect of the guarantor's denial (e.g., denying the debt) on the judgment against him?
A) The judgment is invalid because of the denial.
B) The guarantor is considered legally discredited, so his denial is not considered.
C) The case is dismissed until the principal appears.
D) The guarantor must pay but cannot seek recourse.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi suretyship, the type of guarantee (with/without order) determines liability and recourse; a guarantor's denial does not alter the ruling but legally discredits him.

Kafalah — Surety

Suretyship with and without Debtor's Order: Liability and Recourse
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah and recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between kafalah bi amr and kafalah bila amr in terms of judgment against the principal, and state the majority Hanafi position on the surety's right of recourse after denial.

Core Idea
In ordered suretyship, judgment binds both; in unordered, only the surety is bound, and despite denial, he retains recourse.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi suretyship law: whether the debtor ordered the guarantee or not. The ruling determines not only who is bound by a court judgment but also whether the surety can recover from the debtor after paying. A subtle dispute within the Hanafi school explores what happens when the surety denies the debt but is proven against—does he lose his right of recourse? The majority says no, applying the principle of mukadhdhab shar'an (legally considered a liar).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the basic ruling: with the debtor's order, judgment against the surety also binds the principal; without order, judgment only binds the surety.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position from the six core books.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar argues that in unordered kafalah, if the surety denies the debt and is proven against, he should not be able to recover from the debtor because his denial was a wrong.
Zufar is a respected Hanafi scholar, but his view is a minority (nawadir).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand Zufar's reasoning: the surety wronged by denying, so he should not profit from his wrongdoing; allowing recourse would be unjust.
This is an argument based on qiyas and justice.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) respond that the surety is legally considered a liar (mukadhdhab shar'an) because his denial is contradicted by evidence. Therefore, his denial is void, and the original contract (with order) remains intact, giving him recourse.
The concept of mukadhdhab shar'an is a procedural tool to resolve contradictions between a party's claim and established evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Zufar, a surety who denies and loses cannot recover from the debtor; under the majority, he retains the right to recover. The majority view is mufta bihi.
This difference affects real-life guarantees and litigation strategies.

Source Text

As for matters that later require a claim and presentation of evidence, it is not valid. For example, if he claimed against another that he bought this slave-girl of his, then he said 'I was never your seller', and the plaintiff provided proof, then a defect was found, and the seller provided proof that he sold and is free from all defects, the evidence of innocence is not accepted due to inconsistency. The explanation is that the denial is nonexistent in one aspect and existent in another, so both aspects are given effect. Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute within the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author explains the ruling on suretyship (kafalah) distinguishing between cases where the surety acts with the debtor's order (bi amrihi) and without order (bila amr). The core ruling: If the surety is by the debtor's order, a judgment against the surety also establishes the debt against the absent principal debtor; if without order, the judgment only binds the surety. The reason is that with order, the surety is initially a donation but ultimately a recompense (the surety can recover from the debtor), so the surety's acknowledgment of the order implies acknowledgment of the debt. Without order, the surety is a pure donation and cannot recover, so the surety's denial of the debt (or claim of suretyship without order) does not affect the principal. The author then discusses a variant: if the plaintiff claims that the surety is for a debt that was already adjudged against the principal, the surety can be a litigant because the condition of suretyship is met. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagrees: he argues that since the surety denied the debt, he is unjust in his assumption, so he should not be penalized by having recourse denied. The majority (including Abu Hanifa) respond that the surety is legally considered a liar (mukadhdhab shar'an) because his denial contradicts the court's judgment or evidence, so his assumption is void. The passage also clarifies a subtle point: if the surety confesses that the principal owes a debt, that confession does not bind the surety himself because his confession does not create a new obligation against the principal but only acknowledges an existing one. The commentator elaborates on the difference between this case and a case where the plaintiff proves that a prior judgment existed against the principal. The discussion includes an explicit reference to the Hidayah and a clarification of a misunderstanding.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship or guarantee; a contract where one person (kafil) becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another (asil).
كفيل (kafil): The surety who guarantees the debt.
أمر (amr): The debtor's order or authorization for the surety to act.
مكذب شرعاً (mukadhdhab shar'an): Legally considered a liar; one whose denial is rejected by Islamic law due to contrary proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the effect on the principal if the surety (kafil) acted without the debtor's order and the plaintiff proves the debt?
A) The principal is also liable; the judgment against the surety extends to the principal.
B) The surety is solely liable; the principal is not affected by the judgment.
C) The surety is not liable at all because the kafalah is invalid without the order.
D) The principal is liable but only if the surety admits.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi suretyship, the debtor's order determines both the scope of liability and the surety's right of recourse; even if the surety denies, his recourse is preserved under the majority view due to the principle of legal falsehood (mukadhdhab shar'an).

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Four Types of Kafalah and the Stratagem for the Absent Debtor
Classifies kafalah and explains a hila for absent debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of kafalah (unrestricted/restricted, with/without order) and explain how unrestricted kafalah functions as a legal stratagem (hila) to enforce a debt against an absent debtor.

Core Idea
Unrestricted kafalah binds the absent debtor automatically, making it the perfect hila — but restricted kafalah only works if ordered.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq classifies kafalah into four types and reveals a practical legal stratagem (hila) used by Hanafi jurists to enforce debts against absent debtors. The key distinction is between unrestricted and restricted suretyship, and whether the debtor ordered the guarantee. By mastering this classification, you will understand how a present surety can be used to obtain a judgment that extends to an absent principal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification framework
The passage states kafalah is of four types: unrestricted/restricted, each with or without order. Identify these four combinations.
2
Analyze the unrestricted types
Why does unrestricted kafalah always extend judgment to the absent principal, even without order? The author says because the claimant cannot prove the debt against the surety without first proving it against the principal.
3
Examine the restricted types
Why does restricted kafalah only work if with order? Because the specific amount ties the surety's obligation to the debtor's authorization. Without order, the judgment does not extend.
4
Apply the hila scenario
Walk through the stratagem: claimant finds a willing surety, surety admits kafalah but denies debt, claimant proves debt, judgment against both, then claimant absolves surety. The debt remains against the absent principal.
5
Note the correction and nuance
The passage corrects a commentator's oversight: restricted kafalah with order can also work as hila if the claimant has witnesses. Understand when the hila is valid.

He restricted by his statement 'he has such-and-such against Zayd and that this one is a surety for him' meaning with this amount, because if the kafalah were unrestricted, like saying 'I stand surety for your wealth against so-and-so', then judgment against the surety is judgment against the principal, whether it is by his order or without his order, because the claimant cannot establish his right against the surety except after establishing it against the principal, due to our statement that the word is the surety's word that the claimant has nothing against the principal. And if that is the case, the surety becomes a litigant on his behalf even if he is absent. And our school is that judgment against an absent person is not allowed unless the claimant claims against a present person a right that cannot be established except by proving it against the absent person. Our scholars said: This is a method for one who wants to prove a debt against an absent person without there being a connection between the surety and the absent person. Similarly, if the claimant fears the death of a witness, he colludes with a man and claims such a kafalah against him; the man admits the kafalah and denies the debt; then the claimant presents evidence of the debt; then judgment is given against the surety and the principal; then the claimant absolves the surety. In summary, it is of four types: unrestricted as to amount, and restricted to it. Each is of two types: either with order or without order. So there is no distinction in the unrestricted; it is the stratagem for judgment against the absent person. And distinction exists in the restricted, but it is not suitable for the stratagem, because the condition for extension to the absent person is that it is with his order. He said in al-Khaniyyah after what the author quoted from it: 'If a man claims that he has a thousand against an absent person and that this man stood surety for me for the thousand that I have against him by his order, then this and what preceded are the same: judgment is given against the present person and that is judgment against the absent person. And if he did not say 'by his order' and the defendant denies that and provides proof, judgment is given for the thousand against the present person and it is not judgment against the absent person, unlike if he claimed general kafalah, for then there is no distinction.' His statement: 'From here it is known that what the commentator mentioned later' etc., meaning in the Book of Judgments before the chapter on arbitration. Then I saw that it agrees with what is here, and this is its text: 'If he claims against a person a debt on the basis that he is a surety for an absent person by his order, and the present person admits the kafalah but denies the debt, and the claimant presents evidence that he has a thousand dirhams against the absent person, his evidence is accepted in this case, and the right is established against the absent person and the present person, so that when the absent person arrives, it is binding on him, and he does not need to repeat the evidence.' Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that classifies kafalah into four types and explains the legal stratagem (hila) to enforce a debt against an absent debtor using unrestricted kafalah. The author first explains that the restriction to a specific amount in the matn is to clarify that the ruling differs when the kafalah is unrestricted. If the kafalah is unrestricted (like 'I guarantee whatever debt you have against so-and-so'), then judgment against the surety automatically becomes a judgment against the absent principal, regardless of whether the surety acted with the debtor's order or not. This is because the claimant cannot prove the debt against the surety without first proving it against the principal, and the surety becomes a litigant on behalf of the absent debtor. This is the method (tariq) that scholars have devised to enforce debts against absent debtors. It works even if there is no prior connection between the surety and the absent debtor. The stratagem: the claimant finds a willing person who acts as a surety; the surety admits the kafalah but denies the debt; the claimant then proves the debt against the principal; judgment is given against both; then the claimant absolves the surety, leaving the debt against the principal. The passage then summarizes the four types: 1) unrestricted kafalah with order, 2) unrestricted without order, 3) restricted with order, 4) restricted without order. In the unrestricted types, the judgment extends to the absent principal regardless of order, so the stratagem works. In the restricted types, the judgment only extends to the absent principal if the kafalah is with order, so the stratagem only works in that case. The author then corrects a mistake: the commentator (al-Sharh) apparently mentioned that a restricted kafalah to a thousand dirhams is an example of the stratagem, but the correct view is that it is the unrestricted kafalah that is the stratagem. However, the author notes that if the claimant has witnesses that the kafalah was with order, even a restricted kafalah can work. The passage ends with a quote from al-Khaniyyah confirming that a restricted kafalah with order is treated like an unrestricted one for the purpose of the stratagem.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem or device used to achieve a permissible result within the framework of sharia, here to enforce a debt against an absent debtor.
كفالة مطلقة (kafalah mutlaqah): Unrestricted suretyship; a guarantee without specifying a particular amount of debt.
كفالة مقيدة (kafalah muqayyadah): Restricted suretyship; a guarantee limited to a specific amount (e.g., one thousand dirhams).
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment against an absent person; generally not allowed except in specific circumstances like when necessary to enforce rights through a present litigant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which type of kafalah (suretyship) is always suitable as a hila (legal stratagem) to obtain a judgment against an absent debtor?
A) Unrestricted kafalah, whether with or without the debtor's order.
B) Restricted kafalah, whether with or without the debtor's order.
C) Only kafalah with the debtor's order, regardless of amount.
D) Only kafalah without the debtor's order.

Key Takeaway
The unrestricted kafalah (mutlaqah) is a powerful Hanafi hila to enforce debts against absent debtors, simplifying litigation by binding the absent principal automatically.

8 min
The Four Types of Kafala and the Principle of Representation
Classifying kafala and establishing the representation principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of kafala and explain why judgment against the surety is judgment against the principal.

Core Idea
Judgment against the surety is judgment against the principal, even if absent.

In this passage, the author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the two main categories of suretyship (kafala): absolute (mutlaqa) and limited (muqayyada). He then explains a key principle: when a judgment is issued against the surety, it is effectively against the principal (asil), because the claimant cannot prove the debt against the surety without first proving it against the principal. This principle is fundamental for understanding the Hanafi legal device for proving debts against absentees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Kafala is either absolute (without specifying the amount) or limited (specifying a particular amount).
Absolute: 'I guarantee your property against so-and-so.' Limited: 'I guarantee one thousand dirhams against so-and-so.'
2
Determine the legal effect of absolute kafala
In absolute kafala, judgment against the surety is judgment against the principal, regardless of whether it was by the principal's order.
The key phrase: 'فإن القضاء على الكفيل قضاء على الأصيل، سواء كانت بأمره أو بغير أمره'.
3
Explain the rationale
The claimant cannot prove the debt against the surety without proving it against the principal, and the surety's word is accepted regarding the absence of debt. Thus, the surety becomes a representative (khasm) for the absent principal.
The surety's denial of debt is believed, shifting the burden of proof to the claimant.
4
List the four types
Absolute kafala (with/without order) and limited kafala (with/without order). Only absolute (both) and limited with order extend to the absentee.
The limited without order does not affect the absentee.
5
Correct the common misinterpretation
Some mistakenly think limited kafala can always be used for the absentee; the author corrects that only absolute kafala is the default hila, but limited with order can also serve.
The commentator (al-Sharh) is shown to have made an oversight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
ما زعمه. قيد بقوله له على زيد كذا وأن هذا كفيل عنه يعني بهذا المقدار لأن الكفالة لو كانت مطلقة نحو أن يقول كفلت بمالك على فلان فإن القضاء على الكفيل قضاء على الأصيل، سواء كانت بأمره أو بغير أمره، لأن الطالب لا يتوصل إلى إثبات حقه على الكفيل إلا بعد إثباته على الأصيل لما ذكرنا أن القول قول الكفيل أنه ليس للطالب على الأصيل شيء، وإذا كان كذلك صار الكفيل خصماً عنه وإن كان غائباً. والمذهب عندنا أن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز إلا إذا ادعى على الحاضر حقاً لا يتوصل إليه إلا بإثباته على الغائب. قال مشايخنا وهذا طريق من أراد إثبات الدين على الغائب من غير أن يكون بين الكفيل والغائب اتصال، وكذا إذا خاف الطالب موت الشاهد يتواضع مع رجل ويدعي عليه مثل هذه الكفالة فيقر الرجل بالكفالة وينكر الدين فيقيم المدعي البينة على الدين فيقضي به على الكفيل والأصيل ثم يبرئ الكفيل. والحاصل أنها على أربعة أوجه مطلقة عن المقدار، ومقيدة به. وكل على وجهين إما بالأمر أو بعدمه فلا تفصيل في المطلقة وهي الحيلة في القضاء في الغائب والتفصيل في المقيدة ولا تصلح للحيلة لأن شرط التعدي إلى الغائب كونها بأمره والحوالة على هذه الوجوه، وفي فتاوى قاضيخان بعد أن ذكر أن الكفالة المطلقة الحيلة في الإثبات على الغائب قال : وليس هو قضاء على المسخر لأن المدعي صادق في دعواه على الكفيل ثم يبرئ المدعي الكفيل عن المال والكفالة ويبقى المال له على الغائب اهـ. ومن هنا علم ذكره الشارح فيما يأتي في شرح قوله ولا يقضى على غائب إلا أن يكون ما يدعي على الغائب سبباً لما يدعي على الحاضر» أن من الصور الكفالة المقيدة بألف درهم إلى آخره سهو ظاهر، وإنما هو في المطلقة وسيأتي التنبيه عليه في محله إن شاء الله تعالى.
درهم
هي
أن ما
الغائب
وجوده فيما يحتاج إليها فليكن هذا في ذكر منك فإنه كثير النفع ؛ كذا في الحواشي اليعقوبية . قوله : والتفصيل في المقيدة الخ) يعني أنها تصلح للحيلة لو بالأمر وإلا فلا قال في الخانية بعدما نقله المؤلف عنها ولو ادعى رجل أن له على الغائب ألف . وأن هذا الرجل كفل لي عن بالألف الذي لي عليه بأمره فهذا وما تقدم سواء يقضي على الحاضر ويكون ذلك قضاء على الغائب، ولو لم يقل بأمره وأنكر المدعى عليه ذلك فبرهن عليه يقضي بالألف على الحاضر ولا يكون قضاء على الغائب بخلاف ما لو ادعى الكفالة العامة فلا تفصيل قوله : ومن هنا علم أن ما ذكره الشارح فيما يأتي الخ) أي في كتاب القضاء قبيل باب التحكيم، ثم إن الذي رأيته فيه موافق لما هنا وهذا نصه : لو ادعى على شخص ديناً على أنه كفيل عن الغائب بأمره فأقر الحاضر بالكفالة وأنكر الدين فأقام المدعي البينة أن له على الغائب ألف درهم تقبل بينته في هذه الصورة ويثبت الحق على الغائب والحاضر حتى إذا حضر الغائب لزمه ولا يحتاج إلى إعادة البينة .اهـ. قوله : (وإنما هو في ا الحصر نظر بل في المقيدة بمقدار إذا كانت بالأمر كذلك كما علمت نعم يظهر التخصيص بالمطلقة إذا لم يكن له شهود على كون الكفالة بالأمر، أما إذا كان له شهود عليها وأثبت ذلك على الكفيل المطلقة)
في
Book of Suretyship. What he claimed. He restricted by his saying: 'He has against Zayd such-and-such and that this is a surety for him' meaning by this amount. Because if the suretyship is absolute, such as saying 'I guarantee your property against so-and-so,' then the judgment against the surety is judgment against the principal, whether it was by his order or without his order, because the claimant cannot establish his right against the surety except after establishing it against the principal, because we mentioned that the surety's word is accepted that the claimant has nothing against the principal. And if that is the case, the surety becomes a representative against him even if absent. Our school is that judgment against an absentee is not permitted unless he claims against a present person a right that cannot be established except by proving it against the absentee. Our mashayikh said: This is a method for one who wants to prove a debt against an absentee without any connection between the surety and the absentee. Similarly, if the claimant fears the death of the witness, he conspires with a man and claims against him such suretyship; the man admits the suretyship but denies the debt; then the claimant produces evidence of the debt; judgment is passed against the surety and the principal; then he discharges the surety. Summary: It has four aspects: absolute as to amount, and restricted to it. Each has two aspects: either by order or without order. So there is no difference in the absolute one; it is the legal device for judgment against an absentee. The difference is in the restricted one; it does not serve as a device because the condition for extending to the absentee is that it be by his order. The hawala follows these aspects. In Fatawa Qadikhan, after mentioning that absolute suretyship is a device for proof against an absentee, he said: It is not a judgment against the absent defendant because the claimant is truthful in his claim against the surety; then the claimant discharges the surety from the property and the suretyship, and the property remains against the absentee. End quote. From here it is known that the commentator's mention later in explaining his statement 'And judgment is not passed against an absentee unless what is claimed against the absentee is a cause for what is claimed against the present person' that among the cases is restricted suretyship of one thousand dirhams etc. is an apparent oversight; rather it is in the absolute one, and attention will be drawn to it in its place, God willing. Dirham. It. That what. The absentee. Its existence in what is needed may this be in your mention, for it is very beneficial; so in Al-Hawashi al-Ya'qubiyyah. His statement: 'And the detail in the restricted one, etc.' means that it can serve as a device if by order, otherwise not. He said in Al-Khaniyyah after the author quoted from him: If a man claims that against an absentee he has one thousand and that this man is surety for me for the one thousand that I have against him by his order, this and what preceded are equal; judgment is passed against the present person and that is judgment against the absentee. If he did not say 'by his order' and the defendant denied that and he proved against him, judgment is passed for the one thousand against the present person but it is not judgment against the absentee, unlike if he claimed general suretyship; then no detail. His statement: 'And from here it is known that what the commentator mentioned later, etc.' means in the Book of Judgments before the chapter on arbitration. Then what I saw in it agrees with what is here, and this is its text: If a person claims a debt against another on the basis that he is a surety for an absentee by his order, and the present person admits the suretyship but denies the debt, and the claimant produces evidence that he has one thousand dirhams against the absentee, his evidence is accepted in this case, and the right is established against the absentee and the present person, so that if the absentee appears, it is binding on him, and there is no need to re-establish the evidence. End quote. His statement: 'And it is only in the absolute, there is consideration in the restricted one in terms of amount if it is by order as you know; yes, specification to the absolute appears if he has no witnesses on the suretyship being by order; but if he has witnesses on it and established that against the surety' In.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the rulings of kafala (suretyship/guarantee). The author begins by clarifying a key distinction: a suretyship can be either absolute (mutlaqa) regarding the amount—e.g., 'I guarantee your property against so-and-so'—or limited (muqayyada), specifying a particular sum. The central ruling is that when a judgment is issued against the surety, it is effectively against the principal (asil) as well, even if the principal is absent. This is because the claimant cannot prove the debt against the surety without first proving it against the principal, and the surety's word is accepted regarding the absence of debt against the principal. Therefore, the surety becomes a representative (khasm) for the absent principal. However, the Hanafi school does not generally permit judgment against an absentee unless the claim against the present person is inherently linked to the claim against the absentee. 

The passage then discusses a practical legal device (hila): to prove a debt against an absentee when there is no direct connection between the surety and the absentee, or when the claimant fears witnesses may die. The device involves a collusive lawsuit: the claimant sues a present person (the surety) who admits the suretyship but denies the debt. The claimant then produces evidence of the debt (from the original transaction with the absentee), obtaining a judgment against both the surety and the absentee. The claimant then discharges the surety, leaving the debt established against the absentee. According to Fatawa Qadikhan, this is valid because the claimant is truthful in his claim against the surety; it is not a judgment against an innocent absent person.

The author summarizes that kafala has four types: absolute by order, absolute without order, limited by order, limited without order. Only the absolute ones (both with and without order) and the limited one with order can serve as a hila to reach the absentee. The limited one without order does not extend to the absentee. The commentator then corrects an apparent oversight by the original commentator (al-Sharh) who mistakenly mentioned restricted kafala as an example in the rule about judgment against absentees; the correct example is absolute kafala. The text continues with citations from Fatawa Qadikhan and Al-Khaniyyah confirming this. Finally, it notes that if there are witnesses that the suretyship was by the absentee's order, even a limited kafala can serve as a hila.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): A contract of suretyship/guarantee where the surety undertakes to fulfill an obligation if the principal fails.
مطلقة (mutlaqa): Absolute suretyship without specifying the amount; the surety guarantees all debts of the principal to the creditor.
مقيدة (muqayyada): Limited suretyship where the surety specifies a particular amount or debt.
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor in a kafala contract, the person for whom the surety guarantees.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): A legal judgment issued against a person who is not present in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of kafala does NOT serve as a legal device (hila) to prove a debt against an absentee?
A) Absolute kafala with the principal's order.
B) Absolute kafala without the principal's order.
C) Limited kafala with the principal's order.
D) Limited kafala without the principal's order.

Key Takeaway
Only absolute kafala (any order) and limited kafala by the absentee's order can extend the judgment to the absentee; limited without order does not.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Device (Hila) for Proving Debts Against Absentees
Explaining the hila procedure and correcting a previous commentator's error

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for the Hanafi hila to prove a debt against an absentee and apply the procedural steps.

Core Idea
The Hanafi hila uses a collusive lawsuit against a present surety to establish a debt against an absentee.

This section of the passage details the practical legal device (hila) that the Hanafi school permits to prove a debt against an absentee. The device involves a present surety who admits the suretyship but denies the debt; the creditor then proves the debt with evidence, obtaining a judgment against both the surety and the absentee, after which the surety is released. The author also corrects a misunderstanding about which type of kafala can be used.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Normally, Hanafis do not allow judgment against an absentee. But if the claimant has a claim against a present person that is linked to the absentee, it can be used as a hila.
The connection: the present person is a surety for the absentee.
2
Learn the procedure
The claimant finds a present person and claims that he is a surety for the absentee's debt. The present person admits the suretyship but denies the debt. The claimant then produces evidence that the absentee owes the debt.
The surety's admission of suretyship is key, but he must deny the debt to force the creditor to prove it.
3
Understand the effect
Judgment is passed against both the surety and the absentee. Then the claimant releases the surety, leaving the debt established against the absentee.
The release does not affect the debt against the absentee because it was proven independently.
4
Identify the necessary condition
The hila works only with absolute kafala or limited kafala by the absentee's order.
Without the absentee's order, the judgment does not extend to him.
5
Correct the common error
Some mistakenly think limited kafala always works. The author clarifies that only limited kafala by the absentee's order works; the default is absolute kafala.
There is a critique of the commentator (al-Sharh) who gave a misleading example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
ما زعمه. قيد بقوله له على زيد كذا وأن هذا كفيل عنه يعني بهذا المقدار لأن الكفالة لو كانت مطلقة نحو أن يقول كفلت بمالك على فلان فإن القضاء على الكفيل قضاء على الأصيل، سواء كانت بأمره أو بغير أمره، لأن الطالب لا يتوصل إلى إثبات حقه على الكفيل إلا بعد إثباته على الأصيل لما ذكرنا أن القول قول الكفيل أنه ليس للطالب على الأصيل شيء، وإذا كان كذلك صار الكفيل خصماً عنه وإن كان غائباً. والمذهب عندنا أن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز إلا إذا ادعى على الحاضر حقاً لا يتوصل إليه إلا بإثباته على الغائب. قال مشايخنا وهذا طريق من أراد إثبات الدين على الغائب من غير أن يكون بين الكفيل والغائب اتصال، وكذا إذا خاف الطالب موت الشاهد يتواضع مع رجل ويدعي عليه مثل هذه الكفالة فيقر الرجل بالكفالة وينكر الدين فيقيم المدعي البينة على الدين فيقضي به على الكفيل والأصيل ثم يبرئ الكفيل. والحاصل أنها على أربعة أوجه مطلقة عن المقدار، ومقيدة به. وكل على وجهين إما بالأمر أو بعدمه فلا تفصيل في المطلقة وهي الحيلة في القضاء في الغائب والتفصيل في المقيدة ولا تصلح للحيلة لأن شرط التعدي إلى الغائب كونها بأمره والحوالة على هذه الوجوه، وفي فتاوى قاضيخان بعد أن ذكر أن الكفالة المطلقة الحيلة في الإثبات على الغائب قال : وليس هو قضاء على المسخر لأن المدعي صادق في دعواه على الكفيل ثم يبرئ المدعي الكفيل عن المال والكفالة ويبقى المال له على الغائب اهـ. ومن هنا علم ذكره الشارح فيما يأتي في شرح قوله ولا يقضى على غائب إلا أن يكون ما يدعي على الغائب سبباً لما يدعي على الحاضر» أن من الصور الكفالة المقيدة بألف درهم إلى آخره سهو ظاهر، وإنما هو في المطلقة وسيأتي التنبيه عليه في محله إن شاء الله تعالى.

This is because the claimant cannot prove the debt against the surety without first proving it against the principal, and the surety's word is accepted regarding the absence of debt against the principal. Therefore, the surety becomes a representative (khasm) for the absent principal. However, the Hanafi school does not generally permit judgment against an absentee unless the claim against the present person is inherently linked to the claim against the absentee.

The claimant then produces evidence of the debt (from the original transaction with the absentee), obtaining a judgment against both the surety and the absentee. The claimant then discharges the surety, leaving the debt established against the absentee. According to Fatawa Qadikhan, this is valid because the claimant is truthful in his claim against the surety; it is not a judgment against an innocent absent person.

The author summarizes that kafala has four types: absolute by order, absolute without order, limited by order, limited without order. Only the absolute ones (both with and without order) and the limited one with order can serve as a hila to reach the absentee. The limited one without order does not extend to the absentee. The commentator then corrects an apparent oversight by the original commentator (al-Sharh) who mistakenly mentioned restricted kafala as an example in the rule about judgment against absentees; the correct example is absolute kafala. The text continues with citations from Fatawa Qadikhan and Al-Khaniyyah confirming this. Finally, it notes that if there are witnesses that the suretyship was by the absentee's order, even a limited kafala can serve as a hila.

Key Terms
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem or device that is permissible within the Shariah to achieve a lawful objective, here to prove a debt against an absentee.
كفالة مطلقة (kafala mutlaqa): Absolute suretyship without specifying the amount.
كفالة مقيدة (kafala muqayyada): Limited suretyship specifying a particular amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for a limited kafala to serve as a hila for proving debt against an absentee?
A) The surety must specify the amount.
B) The creditor must prove the debt.
C) The suretyship must be by the absentee's order.
D) The surety must admit the debt.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hila for proving debt against an absentee requires the present surety to admit suretyship (but deny debt) and the suretyship to be absolute or by the absentee's order.

Damān al-Darak as Acknowledgment vs. Testimony and Sealing
Explaining that guarantee against eviction constitutes acknowledgment, and that testimony and sealing do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish actions that constitute an acknowledgment (taslim) of the seller's ownership from those that do not, according to the Hanafi school, and apply this distinction to guarantee clauses, testimony, and sealing.

Core Idea
A guarantee against eviction implies acknowledgment; testimony and sealing do not.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wording and actions in a sale contract can carry legal implications beyond the transaction itself. One key issue is whether certain clauses or acts amount to an admission that the seller truly owns the property. This lesson examines a passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that clarifies that a guarantee against eviction (damān al-darak) constitutes a binding acknowledgment, whereas merely testifying or sealing the document does not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for assessing the rights and liabilities of parties and witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text rules that a guarantee against eviction (damān al-darak) is an acknowledgment (taslim) of the seller's ownership, while testimony (shahāda) and sealing (khatm) are not. First, identify these actions and their respective rulings.
The acknowledgment is a legal effect, not an independent act.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The reasoning is based on qiyas (analogy). The guarantee is compared to an explicit admission because the surety would not undertake the risk unless he believed the seller owned the property. Testimony, on the other hand, is done to preserve the event, not to admit ownership.
The proof is aqli (rational) rather than naqli (textual).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal link (wajh al-dalalah) is the assumption of financial risk by the surety. This risk implies a belief in the seller's ownership, thereby functioning as an implied acknowledgment. No such implication exists for a witness or sealer.
The Hanafis use this to argue that the guarantee is analogous to an express iqrar.
4
Note the exceptions and refinements
The passage notes that if the witness writes explicit words like 'sold while owning it' or testifies before a judge, that does constitute acknowledgment. Also, sealing alone does not change the ruling. Be aware of these nuances.
The exceptions confirm the general rule: only actions that imply risk or explicit admission count.

And whoever guarantees the kharaj of another, or mortgages by it, or guarantees his liabilities, or his division, his delivery, his testimony, and his sealing are not [acknowledgment]. His statement: 'And his suretyship for darak is an acknowledgment because if the suretyship was stipulated in the sale, its completion is by acceptance; then by lawsuit he strives to undo what was completed from his side. If it was not stipulated, then the intent is the rulings of sale and to encourage the buyer, for he would not be interested without the guarantee. So it is treated as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. The meaning of it being an acknowledgment is that it is a confirmation from the surety that the house is the seller's property. If the surety claims the house for himself against the buyer, his claim is not heard because if it were valid, the buyer would have recourse against him by virtue of the guarantee, so it would be useless. So in Al-Nihayah. It includes the case where the surety is a pre-emptor; then he has no pre-emption right, so his claim of ownership, pre-emption, or lease is not heard. We have already stated that daman al-darak is the guarantee of the price upon the eviction of the sold property. Al-darak linguistically is 'al-tabi'ah' (consequence), with vowel points. And in the eleventh chapter of sales: one who strives to undo what was completed from his side is not considered except in two cases: one, a man buys a slave, takes possession, pays the price, then an absentee claims [ownership] on such-and-such; his evidence is accepted. Second, if he gives his slave-girl as a gift to someone while the seller had sold her before, and the donee has a child from her; then the donor produces evidence that he had made her a mudabbara or had a child from her; his evidence is accepted, and the donee recovers the slave-girl and the 'aqr (compensation). End quote. The mentioned enumeration is not correct because it is refuted by what Qadikhan mentioned in sales: if the buyer claims that the sold item is free, his claim is heard; and if he sells land then claims that it was a waqf, his evidence is accepted according to the chosen opinion as mentioned by al-Walwaliji, but his claim is not heard due to contradiction while he is striving to undo what was completed from his side. His statement: 'And his testimony and his sealing are not' i.e., they are not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. The witness testifies to his claim because testimony is not stipulated in the sale and is not an acknowledgment of ownership, because sale sometimes occurs from the owner and sometimes from others. Perhaps he wrote the testimony to preserve the incident, unlike the previous case. They said: If he writes in the document 'sold while owning it' or 'a final binding sale' and writes 'witnessed that', it is an acknowledgment, unless he writes the testimony on the acknowledgment of the two contracting parties. Similarly, if he testifies before the judge about the sale and the judge gives judgment based on his testimony, even if he did not judge, it is an acknowledgment. The restriction with sealing is to clarify that mere writing without a seal is not an acknowledgment ipso facto; they mentioned it based on their custom, for they used to seal after writing their names on the document for fear of alteration and forgery. The ruling does not differ. In Fath al-Qadir: The seal was something in their time: when one wrote his name in the document, he placed his name under lead and impressed his seal ring to prevent tampering. This is not in our time. Know that their statement here that testimony does not become established against the principal, even if limited, and it seems he specified the absolute because the discussion is about the device for proof against the absentee by agreement, and that where there is no evidence. His statement: 'And know that their statement here that testimony etc.' Abu al-Su'ud said: But our shaykh narrated from Fatawa al-Shaykh al-Shalbi that his presence at the sale session and his silence without impediment prevents him from the claim, to block the door of forgery. His statement: 'And some specified it with the land tax (الموظف)' proceeded upon in Al-Nahr, then said: Therefore later in Fath al-Qadir the kafala was restricted to where it is a fixed kharaj because it is obligatory in exchange for defending the territory. He said.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling that a guarantee against eviction (damān al-darak) constitutes an acknowledgment (taslīm) of the seller's ownership, while testimony and sealing in a sale document do not. The author explains that if a suretyship against eviction is stipulated in the sale, the contract is completed by acceptance; if not stipulated, it still serves to assure the buyer. This guarantee is treated as an admission by the surety that the seller owns the property, so if the surety later claims the property for himself, his claim is rejected—otherwise, the buyer would have recourse against him, defeating the purpose. This ruling also extends to a pre-emptor (shafi‘) who cannot claim pre-emption if he is the surety.

The passage then criticizes a statement from another source that limits the cases where one who strives to undo a completed transaction is heard to only two situations. The author refutes this using examples from Qadikhan: a buyer claiming the item is free (and thus the sale void) or a seller later claiming the land was a waqf. These show exceptions. The commentary then clarifies that testimony (shahāda) and sealing (khatm) are not acknowledgments of ownership, because the reason for writing them is to preserve the record, not to admit ownership. However, if the witness writes in the document that the seller 'sold while owning it' or 'a final binding sale' and signs as witness, that does constitute acknowledgment. Similarly, testifying before a judge is acknowledgment. The seal is merely an anti-forgery measure; its absence does not affect the ruling. The passage ends with a note from Fath al-Qadir about the historical practice of sealing and a quote about specialising the kafala to fixed land tax (kharaj mawzuf).

Key Terms
ضمان الدرك (daman al-darak): A guarantee given by the seller (or a third party) that if the sold property is evicted due to a prior right, the buyer will be compensated the price.
تسليم (taslim): Acknowledgment or admission; here, acceptance of the seller's ownership by the surety.
ختم (khatm): Sealing of a document with a personal seal to prevent forgery; its presence or absence does not affect acknowledgment.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession; a legal acknowledgment that can be used as evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT considered an acknowledgment (taslim) of the seller's ownership?
A) A clause in the sale contract guaranteeing against eviction (daman al-darak).
B) Writing in the document 'sold while owning it' and signing as witness.
C) Testifying before a judge about the sale, even if judgment is not given.
D) Simply writing and sealing as a witness without any additional phrase.

Key Takeaway
Only actions that imply an assumption of risk (like a guarantee clause) or explicit wording of ownership constitute acknowledgment; mere witnessing or sealing do not.

Kafalah for Kharaj: Valid for Fixed Tax Only
Commentary specifying conditions for kafalah on kharaj and rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah for kharaj and rahn, and distinguish between fixed and proportional land tax based on the nature of the debt.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid only for debts in the dhimmah; proportional kharaj is not a debt.

In this passage, the commentator al-Shāriḥ explains the ruling that suretyship (kafalah) is valid for land tax (kharaj) and for a pledge (rahn). However, a crucial distinction is made between fixed and proportional kharaj, and a notable exception emerges for liability (darak). Understanding these nuances is key to applying the principle correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rulings in the Matn
Extract the four items for which kafalah is declared valid: kharaj, rahn, nawā'ib, qismah. Note that the commentary focuses on kharaj and rahn.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqā'iq.
2
Analyze the Basis for Kharaj
Why is kafalah for kharaj valid? Because it is a debt that is demanded. Contrast with zakat on apparent wealth, which is an act of worship, not a debt.
This establishes the condition: must be a financial debt in the dhimmah.
3
Distinguish Between Types of Kharaj
Kharaj includes fixed (muwazzaf) and proportional (muqāsama). The commentary states that some scholars restrict validity to fixed kharaj because it is a debt in the dhimmah; proportional is not a debt until assessed.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf.
4
Examine the Analogy to Rahn
Rahn is compared to kafalah via the commonality of securing (tawaththuq). Generally, rahn is valid wherever kafalah is valid, but there is an exception: darak (liability for defect) – kafalah is valid but rahn is not.
The analogy is valid but not perfect; note the exception.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that kafalah for kharaj muwazzaf is valid; for muqāsama it is not valid because it is not a debt in the dhimmah.
This is the position taken by the commentator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن ضمن عن آخر خراجه أو رهن به أو ضمن نوائبه أو قسمته صح) أما الخراج فلكونه ديناً مطالباً به قيد به للاحتراز عن الزكاة في الأموال الظاهرة فإنه لا يجوز الضمان بها عن صاحب المال لأنها مجرد فعل ولهذا لا تؤخذ من تركته إلا بوصيته. فشمل الخراج الموظف وخراج المقاسمة، وخصصه بعضهم بالموظف وهو ما يجب في الذمة ونفي صحة الضمان بخراج المقاسمة لأنه لم يكن ديناً في الذمة والرهن كالكفالة بجامع التوثق فيجوز في كل موضع تجوز الكفالة فيه؛ هكذا ذكر الشارح وهو منقوض بالدرك فإن الكفالة به جائزة دون الرهن
His statement: (Whoever stands surety for another's land tax, or for a pledge made by him, or for his impositions, or for his share of division, it is valid.) As for the land tax, because it is a debt that is demanded, it is restricted to that to avoid the zakat on apparent wealth, for suretyship for it on behalf of the owner is not permissible because it is a mere act of worship, and therefore it is not taken from his estate except by his will. Thus it includes both the fixed land tax (kharaj mawazzaf) and the proportional land tax (kharaj muqasamah), but some scholars specify it to the fixed tax, which is what is obligatory in the dhimmah, and they negate the validity of suretyship for proportional tax because it is not a debt in the dhimmah. And the pledge is like suretyship in the commonality of securing, so it is permissible in every place where suretyship is permissible; thus the commentator mentioned, but it is refuted by the liability (dark), for suretyship for it is valid but the pledge is not.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq stating the validity of kafalah (suretyship) for four things: kharaj (land tax), rahn (pledge), nawā'ib (impositions), and qismah (division). The author explains that kharaj is a debt demanded, so it qualifies for kafalah, unlike zakat on apparent wealth which is a pure act of worship and not a debt. The kharaj includes two types: kharaj mawazzaf (a fixed amount) and kharaj muqasamah (a share of the produce). Some scholars limit validity to the fixed tax because it is a debt in the dhimmah, while the proportional tax is a share of the crop and not a debt until assessed. The pledge (rahn) is analogized to kafalah because both serve as security (tawaththuq). However, the commentator notes an objection: for 'dark' (liability for defect in a sale), kafalah is valid but rahn is not, showing the analogy is imperfect. The passage then transitions to discuss nawā'ib in the next lesson.

Key Terms
خراج (kharāj): Land tax: a tax on agricultural land, either fixed (mawazzaf) or proportional (muqāsama).
خراج الموظف (kharāj al-muwazzaf): Fixed land tax, a definite amount due annually on land, considered a debt in the dhimmah.
خراج المقاسمة (kharāj al-muqāsama): Proportional land tax, a share of the produce, not a debt until assessed.
رهن (rahn): Pledge: giving an asset as security for a debt.
درك (darak): Liability for defect in a sale; the seller's guarantee against defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is kafalah for zakat on apparent wealth not valid?
A) Because it is a debt demanded by the state
B) Because it is a pure act of worship and not a debt
C) Because zakat is not a financial obligation
D) Because the owner does not consent

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for kharaj is valid only if the kharaj is a fixed debt in the dhimmah; proportional kharaj is not a debt, so kafalah is invalid.

Kafalah for Unjust Impositions: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah for unjust taxes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah for unjust impositions, identify the key definitional issue, and explain why the majority view (invalidity) is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Kafalah for unjust impositions is invalid because it affirms oppression, which must be eliminated.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: is kafalah (suretyship) valid for unjust taxes and extortions (jibāyāt)? Two great scholars of the school, the brothers Ṣadr al-Islām and Fakhr al-Islām al-Bazdawī, disagree. The dispute hinges on the very definition of kafalah: does it require a legal debt, or is a mere physical demand sufficient? We will trace the arguments and see why the later school settled on invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of nawā'ib
First, distinguish between lawful impositions (e.g., public works, ransom) and unlawful extortions (jibāyāt). Kafalah for lawful ones is valid by consensus; for unlawful ones, there is dispute.
2
State the position of Ṣadr al-Islām
He holds kafalah invalid for unjust impositions. His proof: kafalah is 'joining a liability to a liability in demand or debt' (ḍamm dhimmah ilā dhimmah fī al-muṭālaba aw al-dayn). Since unjust impositions have no legal demand or debt, the definition is not fulfilled.
Ṣadr al-Islām emphasizes the requirement of a shar‘ī (legal) basis.
3
State the position of Fakhr al-Islām
He holds kafalah valid. His proof: kafalah is based on demand (muṭālaba), and physical demand (ḥissiyya) suffices. Unjust impositions are physically demanded, so kafalah is valid. He compares it to other debts where demand is the key.
Fakhr al-Islām expands the definition to include any enforceable demand.
4
Examine the later tarjīḥ
Al-Khayr al-Ramlī and the majority of later mashāyikh (as recorded in al-Bazzāziyya and al-Khulāṣa) prefer the view of Ṣadr al-Islām. The reason: oppression must be eliminated (يجب إعدامه), and validating kafalah would affirm it. Fatwa is given on invalidity.
The tarjīḥ is based on the principle of sadd al-dharā’i‘ (blocking the means to evil).
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
If kafalah is valid, the guarantor who pays by order has recourse against the principal. If invalid, the payment is considered an oppressive act and there is no recourse.
This practical consequence shows the weight of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوائب فجمع نائبة وفي الصحاح : النائبة المصيبة واحدة نوائب الدهر اهـ. وفي اصطلاحهم قيل : أراد بها ما يكون بحق كأجرة الحراس وكري النهر المشترك والمال الموظف لتجهيز الجيش وفداء الأسرى وقيل : المراد بها ما ليس بحق كالجبايات التي في زماننا يأخذها الظلمة بغير حق. فإن كان مراده هو الأول جازت الكفالة بها اتفاقاً لأنه واجب مضمون، وإن كان مراده الثاني ففيه اختلاف المشايخ فقال بعضهم : لا تجوز الكفالة منهم صدر الإسلام البزدوي لأنها ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة أو الدين، وهنا لا مطالبة ولا دين شرعيان على الأصيل فلم يتحقق معناها. وقال بعضهم : تجوز منهم فخر الإسلام علي البزدوي أخو صدر الإسلام المتقدم لأنها في المطالبة مثل سائر الديون بل فوقها والعبرة للمطالبة لأنها شرعت لإلتزامها فالمطالبة الحسية كالمطالبة الشرعية ولذا قلنا : ومن قام بتوزيع هذه النوائب على المسلمين بالقسط أي بالعدل يؤجر وإن كان الآخذ بالأخذ ظالماً، وقلنا من قضی نائبه غیره بامزه رجع عليه وإن لم يشترط الرجوع وهو الصحيح كما في الخانية، كمن قضى دين غيره بأمره. وفي العناية قال شمس الأئمة : هذا إذا أمره به لا عن إكراه، أما إذا كان مكرهاً في الأمر فلا يعتبر أمره في الرجوع .اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : وينبغي أن كل من إنها ضم في الدين يمنع صحتها هنا، ومن قال في المطالبة يمكن أن يقول بصحتها ويمكن أن يمنعها بناء على أنها في المطالبة في الدين أو معناه أو مطلقاً .اهـ. وقوله بناء على أنها في لما قدمنا أنها لا تقتصر على المطالبة في الدين إذ لو كان كذلك لم يشمل الدين وحفظه فكان كالأجرة لإخراج مقاسمة لأنه غير واجب وقرينة إرادة الموظف قوله أو رهن به إذ الرهن بخراج المقاسمة غير صحيح بخلاف الموظف. اهـ ما في النهر. وقال بعض الفضلاء: والذي اعتمدوه جميعاً في التعليل بقولهم لأنه دين له مطالب من جهة العباد فصار كسائر الديون يدل على اختصاصه بالموظف، أما خراج المقاسمة فجزء من الخارج وهو عين غير مضمون حتى لو هلك لا يؤخذ شيء والكفالة بأعيان غير مضمونة لا تجوز كالزكاة في الأموال الظاهرة اهـ. قوله : (صدر الإسلام) هو أبو اليسر رملي قوله : (وهو الصحيح كما في (الخانية عبارة الخانية هكذا: وإن كفل عن كتاب الكفالة ٤٠١ مالاً التعريف الكفالة بالنفس لأنها ضم في المطالبة بالحضور. وفي قوله «أو مطلقاً» نظر لأنه إذا قال بأنها في المطالبة مطلقاً لا يمنعها هنا. وفي البزازية صادر الوالي رجلاً وطلب منه . وضمن رجل ذلك وبذل الحط ثم قال الضامن ليس لك علي شيء لأنه ليس للوالي عليه شيء قال شمس الإسلام والقاضي يملك المطالبة لأن المطالبة الشرعية كالمطالبة الحسية اهـ. ولو قال لأن المطالبة الحسية كالمطالبة الشرعية لكان أولى كما لا يخفى وظاهر كلامهم ترجيح الصحة ولذا قال في إيضاح الإصلاح والفتوى على الصحة فإنها كالديون الصحيحة حتى لو رجل بالجبايات اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنها تصح ويرجع على المكفول عنه إن كان بأمره، وكذا السلطان إذا صدر رجلاً فأمر الرجل غيره أن يؤدي عنه المال لكل ما هو مطالب به حساً جازت الكفالة به، فإن أمر غيره بذلك إن قال على أن ترجع علي بذلك كان له أن يرجع عليه وإلا اختلفوا فيه، والصحيح أنه يرجع . ذكر في السير المسألة إذا أسر في دار الحرب فاشتراه رجل منهم إن اشتراه بغير أمره يكون متطوعاً لا يرجع بذلك على الأسير ويخلي سبيله، وإن اشتراه بأمره في القياس لا يرجع المأمور على الآمر . وفي الاستحسان يرجع سواء أمره الأسير أن يرجع بذلك عليه أو لم يقل على أن ترجع بذلك عليه وهو كما لو قال الرجل لغيره أنفق من مالك على عيالي أو أنفق في بناء داري فأنفق المأمور كان له أن يرجع على الآمر بما أنفق، وكذا الأسير إذا أمر رجلاً ليدفع الفداء ويأخذ منهم فهو بمنزلة ما لو أمره بالشراء .اهـ لكن قاضيخان حالف ذلك في شرحه على الجامع الصغير حيث قال : وأما الجبايات التي يوظفها السلطان على الناس قال بعضهم تصح بها الكفالة لأنها مطالب بها حساً بمنزلة الدين الواجب، وعلى هذا قالوا من قضى نائبه غيره بإذنه وهو غير مكره في الأمر يرجع بها عليه وإن لم يشترط الضمان، والأصح أنه لا يصح الضمان بها، ولو أداه بأمره ولم يشترط الضمان لا يرجع لأنه ظالم في حق الآخذ والمأخوذ . منه فلا تصح به الكفالة اهـ. قوله : (وقوله بناء على أنها في المطالبة ممنوع الخ) قال الرملي : هذا الممنوع ممنوع إذ الكلام في الكفالة بالدين لا بالنفس. قوله : ( وفي قوله أو مطلقاً نظر الخ) أقول : مراد المحقق بيان وجه للصحة ووجه للمنع على القول بأنها الضم في المطالبة فقوله بناء على أنها الضم في المطالبة في الدين أو معناه وجه للمنع وقوله أو مطلقاً. للصحة وجه ففي كلامه لف ونشر غير مرتب قوله : (ولو قال لأن المطالبة الحسية الخ) قال الرملي الظاهر أنه من عكس التشبيه وهو يدل على الأبلغية فلا أولوية؛ كذا رأيت بخط بعضهم وفيه نظر إذ ليس المقام مقام الأبلغية وهذا الشارح لم ينف الجواز إنما ذكر الأولوية فتأمل. قوله : (وظاهر كلامهم ترجيح الصحة الخ رجح الخير الرملي في فتاويه عدمها مستنداً إلى ما في البزازية ضمان الجبايات على قول عامة المشايخ لا يصح فجعله قول العامة ومثله في الخلاصة وذكر أن ما قاله في إيضاح الإصلاح غير مسلم بلا برهان وأن ما قاله المؤلف هنا غير مسلم أيضاً لأن ظاهر كلامهم يخالفه لما صرح به في الخلاصة والبزازية أنه قول العامة والعلة له أن الظلم يجب إعدامه ويحرم تقريره وفي القول بصحته تقريره. وقال مؤيد زاده في مجموعة نقلاً عن العمادية : والأسير إذا قال لغيره خلصني فدفع المأمور مالاً وخلصه منه اختلف فيه ؛ قال السرخسي: يرجع في
As for nawā'ib, it is the plural of nā'iba. In al-Sihāh: al-nā'iba is a calamity, one of the vicissitudes of time. In their terminology, it is said: what is meant is that which is by right, like the wages of guards, digging of a shared canal, funds allocated to equip the army, and ransom of captives. And it is said: what is meant is that which is not by right, like the extortions (jibāyāt) that in our time oppressors take unjustly. If the intended meaning is the first, suretyship for it is valid by agreement because it is an obligatory guaranteed duty. If the intended meaning is the second, then there is a difference among the mashāyikh. Some said: suretyship is not valid among them – Ṣadr al-Islām al-Bazdawī – because it is the joining of a liability to a liability in demand or debt, and here there is no legal demand or debt on the principal, so its meaning is not realized. Some said: it is valid among them – Fakhr al-Islām ‘Alī al-Bazdawī, brother of the aforementioned Ṣadr al-Islām – because it is in demand like other debts, even stronger, and the consideration is the demand because it was legislated for its commitment, so the physical demand is like the legal demand. Therefore we said: whoever undertakes to distribute these impositions among the Muslims equitably will be rewarded, even if the taker is unjust in taking. And we said: whoever pays the nā'iba of another with his permission, he has recourse against him even if he did not stipulate recourse, and this is the correct view as in al-Khāniyya, like one who pays another's debt by his order. In al-‘Ināya, Shams al-A'imma said: This is if he ordered him without coercion; if he was coerced in the order, his order is not considered for recourse. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: It should be that whoever says it is joining in debt prohibits its validity here, and whoever says it is in demand can say it is valid, or can prohibit it based on whether it is in demand for debt, or its meaning, or absolutely. And his statement 'based on that it is' – since we have presented that it is not limited to demand in debt, because if that were the case, it would not include the like of wage for removal of [goods] from muqāsama because it is not obligatory, and the indication that the fixed [tax] is intended is his statement 'or a pledge by it' because the pledge for kharāj muqāsama is not valid unlike the fixed. This is what is in al-Nahr. And some virtuous scholars said: What they all relied upon in the reasoning, that it is a debt demanded by the people and thus becomes like other debts, indicates its specificity to the fixed tax. As for kharāj muqāsama, it is a part of the produce, a non-guaranteed asset such that if it perishes, nothing is taken, and suretyship for non-guaranteed assets is not valid, like zakat on apparent wealth. His statement: (Ṣadr al-Islām) is Abū al-Yusr al-Ramlī. His statement: (And it is the correct view as in al-Khāniyya: the wording of al-Khāniyya is: If he stood surety for (page break) wealth – the definition of suretyship for persons is joining in demand for presence. And in his statement 'or absolutely' there is consideration, because if he says that it is in demand absolutely, it does not prevent it here. In al-Bazzāziyya: A governor extorted a man and demanded from him, and a man stood surety for that and paid the protection money, then the guarantor said: you have no claim on me because the governor has no claim on him. Shams al-Islām and al-Qāḍī said: he possesses the right of demand because the legal demand is like the physical demand. If he had said 'the physical demand is like the legal demand' it would be better, as is not hidden. And the apparent meaning of their words is a preference for validity, and therefore it is said in Īḍāḥ al-Iṣlāḥ: The fatwa is on validity, for it is like valid debts. So even if a man [stands surety] for jibāyāt, they differed, and the correct view is that it is valid, and he has recourse against the principal if it was by his order. Likewise, if the sultan extorts a man and the man orders another to pay the money for him, suretyship for it is valid for everything that is demanded physically. If he ordered another to do that, and said 'on condition that you have recourse against me for it', he has recourse; otherwise, they differed, and the correct view is that he has recourse. Mentioned in al-Siyar: The issue of a captive in dar al-ḥarb: if a man buys him, if he buys him without his order, he is a volunteer and does not have recourse on the captive, and he sets him free. If he buys him by his order, by qiyas the agent does not have recourse on the principal; by istiḥsān he has recourse, whether the captive ordered him to have recourse or not. And it is like when a man says to another: spend from your wealth on my family or spend in building my house, and the agent spends, he has recourse on the principal for what he spent. Likewise, the captive when he orders a man to pay the ransom and take him from them, it is like ordering him to purchase. But Qāḍīkhān disagreed in his commentary on al-Jāmi‘ al-Ṣaghīr: As for the jibāyāt that the sultan imposes on people, some said suretyship is valid because they are demanded physically like an obligatory debt. On this basis, they said: whoever pays the nā'iba of another by his permission while not coerced in the order, he has recourse even if he did not stipulate the guarantee. The more correct view is that suretyship is not valid for it, and if he paid by his order without stipulating guarantee, he does not have recourse because he is an oppressor in right of the taker and the taken. Therefore suretyship for it is not valid. His statement: (And his statement 'based on that it is in demand' is prohibited, etc.) said al-Ramlī: This prohibition is prohibited, for the discussion is about suretyship for debt, not for the person. His statement: (And in his statement 'or absolutely' there is consideration, etc.) I say: The intent of the muḥaqqiq is to show a face for validity and a face for prohibition based on the saying that it is joining in demand. So his statement 'based on that it is joining in demand in debt or its meaning' is a face for prohibition, and his statement 'or absolutely' is a face for validity. In his speech there is an unordered laff wa nashr. His statement: (And if he said 'because the physical demand is like the legal demand' etc.) said al-Ramlī: It appears that this is an inversion of the simile, which indicates superiority, so no preference. I saw this in the handwriting of some, but there is consideration, for this is not a context of superiority, and this commentator did not deny permissibility, only mentioned preference. His statement: (And the apparent meaning of their words is preference for validity, etc.) al-Khayr al-Ramlī gave fatwa in his Fatāwā against it, relying on what is in al-Bazzāziyya: Suretyship for jibāyāt according to the generality of the mashāyikh is not valid, so he made it the general view. Likewise in al-Khulāṣa. He mentioned that what was said in Īḍāḥ al-Iṣlāḥ is not accepted without proof, and what the author said here is also not accepted because the apparent meaning of their words opposes it, as stated in al-Khulāṣa and al-Bazzāziyya that it is the general view, and the reason is that injustice must be eliminated and it is forbidden to affirm it, and in saying it is valid there is affirmation. And Mu’ayyad Zāda said in his collection quoting from al-‘Imādiyya: The captive, when he says to another 'free me' and the agent pays money and frees him, there is disagreement; al-Sarakhsī said: he has recourse in (al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq / 6 / 26).
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of kafalah (suretyship) for nawā'ib, specifically unjust impositions (jibāyāt) taken by oppressors. The author first defines nawā'ib: they can be lawful (e.g., guards' wages, canal digging, military funds, ransom) or unlawful extortions. For lawful ones, kafalah is valid by consensus because they are guaranteed obligations. For unlawful extortions, there is ikhtilāf: Ṣadr al-Islām al-Bazdawī holds kafalah invalid because it requires a legal debt or demand, which is absent; Fakhr al-Islām al-Bazdawī holds it valid because the physical demand (mutālaba ḥissiyya) suffices, as kafalah was legislated for commitment. The text then cites various authorities. Al-Khāniyah states the correct view is that the guarantor has recourse if he paid by order without coercion. Fatḥ al-Qadīr explains that if one defines kafalah as joining in debt, it prevents validity; if as joining in demand, validity depends on whether demand is for debt or absolute. Al-Nahr and others distinguish between fixed and proportional kharaj. On page 401, the discussion extends to jibāyāt, where some scholars like Shams al-Islām and al-Qāḍī allow kafalah based on physical demand. However, Qāḍīkhān and al-Khayr al-Ramlī argue it is invalid because paying unjust impositions affirms oppression. The captive case illustrates the rule: paying ransom by order gives recourse by istiḥsān. Ultimately, the muftā bihi is the view of the majority (general mashāyikh) that kafalah for jibāyāt is invalid.

Key Terms
النوائب (al-nawā’ib): Plural of nā’iba: impositions or exactions; can be lawful (like taxes for public good) or unlawful (oppressive extortions).
الجبايات (al-jibāyāt): Taxes or exactions collected by authorities, often implying unjust extortion in this context.
ضم ذمة إلى ذمة (ḍamm dhimmah ilā dhimmah): Joining of one liability to another; the technical definition of kafalah as adding the guarantor's liability to the principal's.
المطالبة (al-muṭālaba): Demand; the right to seek payment or performance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (as presented in the text), what is the ruling on kafalah for unjust impositions (jibāyāt)?
A) It is valid because the demand is physical.
B) It is not valid because it affirms oppression.
C) It is valid only if the principal is coerced.
D) It is valid if the guarantor stipulates recourse.

Key Takeaway
The majority of Hanafi scholars hold that kafalah for unjust jibāyāt is invalid because it affirms oppression; fatwa is on invalidity.

Kafalah — Surety
advanced
12 min
Validity of Kafala for Taxes: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafala for taxes and ransom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on kafala for oppressive taxes, identify the evidence and reasoning for each, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to determine the guarantor's recourse.

Core Idea
Kafala for oppressive taxes is invalid; the guarantor has no recourse, to avoid aiding injustice.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Ibn Nujaym delves into a dispute within the Hanafi school concerning the validity of suretyship (kafala) for taxes imposed by the ruler. While some earlier scholars considered such guarantees valid due to the physical demand of payment, later authorities, notably Sahib al-Muhit, declared them invalid to prevent strengthening oppression. The discussion extends to the analogous case of ransoming a captive, where istihsan allows recourse. Understanding this dispute clarifies when a guarantor can be compelled to pay and whether he can seek reimbursement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the two conflicting Hanafi views on kafala for taxes: one permits it and grants recourse, the other prohibits it and denies recourse.
The pro-validity view relies on physical demand; the anti-validity view emphasizes preventing injustice.
2
Analyse the evidence for validity
Examine the proofs: analogy to valid debts, the captive ransom case from al-Siyar, and the principle of istihsan overriding qiyas.
The captive case analogy shows that istihsan grants recourse even without explicit stipulation.
3
Analyse the evidence for invalidity
Examine Qadi Khan's critique and Sahib al-Muhit's opinion: taxes are oppressive, so guaranteeing them legitimizes oppression; the required conditions for valid kafala (order with 'ala or 'anni) are not met.
The mufta bihi is from al-Muhit: invalid and no recourse.
4
Evaluate the reconciliation
The author notes that both are sound opinions, but the fatwa is on invalidity. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): the guarantor cannot be forced to pay and if he pays voluntarily, he has no recourse.
This protects the guarantor from contributing to injustice.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a modern scenario where a ruler imposes an unjust tax and a person guarantees it, apply the mufta bihi ruling to determine the legal status and consequences.
The ruling is that the guarantee is invalid; the guarantor has no obligation and no recourse if he pays.

فقوله بناء على أنها الضم في المطالبة في الدين أو معناه وجه للمنع وقوله أو مطلقاً. للصحة وجه ففي كلامه لف ونشر غير مرتب قوله : (ولو قال لأن المطالبة الحسية الخ) قال الرملي الظاهر أنه من عكس التشبيه وهو
