عدمها مستنداً إلى ما في البزازية ضمان الجبايات على قول عامة المشايخ لا يصح فجعله قول العامة ومثله في الخلاصة وذكر أن ما قاله في إيضاح الإصلاح غير مسلم بلا برهان وأن ما قاله المؤلف هنا غير مسلم أيضاً لأن ظاهر كلامهم يخالفه لما صرح به في الخلاصة والبزازية أنه قول العامة والعلة له أن الظلم يجب إعدامه ويحرم تقريره وفي القول بصحته تقريره. وقال مؤيد زاده في مجموعة نقلاً عن العمادية : والأسير إذا قال لغيره خلصني فدفع المأمور مالاً وخلصه منه اختلف فيه ؛ قال السرخسي: يرجع في البحر الرائق / ٦ / ٢٦

ثم من أصحابنا من قال: الأفضل للإنسان أن يساوي أهل محلته في إعطاء النائبة قال شمس الأئمة هذا كان في ذلك الزمان لأنه إعانة على الحاجة والجهاد، وأما في زماننا فأكثر النوائب تؤخذ ظلماً، ومن تمكن دفع المظلمة عن نفسه فهو خير له، وإذا أراد الإعطاء فليعط من هو عاجز عن دفع الظلم عن نفسه لفقر ليستعين به الفقير على الظلم وينال المعطي الثواب؛ كذا في فتح القدير.

المسألتين. وقال صاحب المحيط : لا يرجع وهذا هو الأصح وعليه الفتوى فهو مدافع لما في الإصلاح وقول قاضيخان الصحيح الصحة لا يدفع قول صاحب المحيط هذا هو الأصح وعليه الفتوى اهـ .ملخصاً. أقول : غايته أنهما قولان مصححان وقالوا لا يعدل عن تصحيح قاضيخان كما نقله المؤلف لأنه فقيه النفس على أن لقائل أن يقول ليس في كلام المحيط تصحيح خلاف ما صححه قاضيخان لأن المنقول عن المحيط لم يستوف شرائطه صحة الكفالة إذ ليس فيه الأمر بالرجوع وهو بأن يشتمل على لفظة عني» أو «علي» فلهذا صحح عدم الرجوع. ثم رأيت في الخانية قال : وإن اشتراه بأمره في القياس لا يرجع المأمور على الآمر وفي الاستحسان يرجع سواء أمر الأسير أن يرجع بذلك عليه أو لم يقل على أن ترجع بذلك علي وهو كما لو قال الرجل لغيره أنفق على عيالي أو في بناء داري .اهـ. فعلم أن ما صححه في المحيط هو القياس ووجهه ما قلنا كما دل عليه كلام الخانية والاستحسان خلافه، وهذا غير مسألتنا كما لا يخفى لأن الكلام فيها عند استيفاء شرائط صحة الكفالة، ثم رأيت بخط بعض الأفاضل ما حاصله المراد من صحة الكفالة بالنوائب رجوع الكفيل على الأصيل لو كانت الكفالة بالأمر لا أنه يضمن لطالبها الظالم لأن الظلم يجب إعدامه ولا يجوز تقريره فلا تغتر بظاهر الكلام .اهـ. ولعمري أنه تنبيه حسن ولهذا لم يذكروا الرجوع على

من

مالك
Book of Suretyship. [Definition] Suretyship for the person because it is joining in the demand for presence. In his statement 'or absolutely' there is consideration because if he said it is in demand absolutely, it does not prevent it here. In al-Bazzaziyya: The governor oppressed a man and demanded from him. A man guaranteed that and paid the fine, then the guarantor said: 'You have no claim against me because the governor has no claim against him.' Shams al-Islam said: The judge has the right to demand because legal demand is like physical demand. If he said because physical demand is like legal demand, it would be more appropriate. The apparent meaning of their statements is a preference for validity, hence he said in Idah al-Islah: The fatwa is on validity, for it is like valid debts. So even regarding a man with taxes, they differed. The correct view is that it is valid and he has recourse against the guaranteed if by his order. Likewise, if the ruler issues a man, then the man orders another to pay the money on his behalf for everything that is demanded physically, the surety is valid. If he orders another and says 'on condition that you have recourse against me', he has recourse; otherwise, they differed, and the correct view is that he has recourse. Mentioned in al-Siyar: The case of a captive in dar al-harb whom a man buys: if he buys without his order, he is a volunteer, no recourse against the captive, and he releases him. If he buys by his order, by qiyas the agent has no recourse against the principal; by istihsan, he has recourse, whether the captive ordered him to have recourse or did not say 'on condition you have recourse against me'. It is like when a man says to another: 'Spend from your wealth on my family' or 'spend on building my house', and he spends, he has recourse against the one who commanded. Similarly, the captive if he orders a man to pay the ransom and take him, it is like ordering him to buy. But Qadi Khan opposed that in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir, saying: As for taxes imposed by the ruler on people, some say surety is valid because it is demanded physically, like a due debt. On this basis, they said: One who pays a tax for another by his permission while not coerced in the matter has recourse even if he did not stipulate guarantee. But the correct view is that guarantee is not valid, and if he pays by his order without stipulating guarantee, he has no recourse because he is unjust to the taker and the taken; therefore, surety is not valid. [Commentary on the matn] 'His statement: Based on it being in demand is prevented...' al-Ramli said: This prevented is prevented, for the discussion is about surety for debt, not for person. 'And in his statement: or absolutely, there is consideration...' I say: The investigator intended to show a face for validity and a face for prevention according to the view that it is joinder in demand. So his statement 'based on it being joinder in demand regarding debt' or its meaning is a face for prevention, and his statement 'or absolutely' is a face for validity. So his words have non-ordered wrapping and spreading. 'And if he said because physical demand like legal demand...' al-Ramli said: Apparently it is inversion of the simile, which indicates eloquence, so no priority. I saw in the handwriting of some: there is consideration, for this is not a place for eloquence, and this commentator did not deny permissibility, he only mentioned priority. 'And apparent meaning of their statements is preference for validity...' al-Khayr al-Ramli gave fatwa against it in his Fatawa, relying on al-Bazzaziyya that guarantee for taxes according to the general mashayikh is not valid, so he made it the general view, and similarly in al-Khulasa. He said that what was said in Idah al-Islah is not accepted without proof, and what the author said here is also not accepted because the apparent meaning of their statements contradicts it, as stated in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya that it is the general view, and the reason is that oppression must be removed and it is prohibited to affirm it, and affirming its validity is affirming it. Mu'ayyad Zada said in his collection, quoting from al-Imadiyya: The captive if he says to another 'rescue me' and he pays money and rescues him, there is dispute; al-Sarakhsi said: he has recourse. Then some of our scholars said: It is better for a person to treat his neighbors equally in giving the levy. Shams al-A'imma said: This was in that time because it is assistance for need and jihad. As for our time, most levies are taken unjustly, and whoever can repel injustice from himself, it is better for him. If he wants to give, let him give to one who is unable to repel injustice due to poverty, so that the poor person may use it against oppression and the giver gets reward. From Fath al-Qadir. 'The two issues.' Sahib al-Muhit said: He does not have recourse, and this is the most correct and on it is fatwa, so it contradicts what is in al-Islah and Qadi Khan's statement that the correct is validity. The statement of Sahib al-Muhit 'this is the most correct and on it is fatwa' overrides. I say: At most, they are two validated opinions. They said: Do not deviate from the correction of Qadi Khan as the author quoted because he was a jurist of high intelligence. However, one may say that the statement of al-Muhit does not correct contrary to what Qadi Khan corrected because the transmitted from al-Muhit did not fulfill the conditions of valid surety, for it does not contain the order for recourse, which requires the word 'anna' or 'ala'. Hence he corrected non-recourse. Then I saw in al-Khaniyya: If he buys by his order, by qiyas the agent has no recourse against the principal; by istihsan, he has recourse, whether the captive ordered him to have recourse or did not say 'on condition you have recourse against me'. It is like when a man says to another: 'Spend on my family' or 'on building my house'. So it is known that what al-Muhit corrected is qiyas, and its face is what we said, as indicated by al-Khaniyya, and istihsan opposes it. This is not our issue, because the discussion there is when the conditions of valid surety are fulfilled. Then I saw in the handwriting of some virtuous person that the gist is: The meaning of the validity of surety for levies is the recourse of the surety against the principal if the surety was by order, not that he guarantees for the unjust claimant, because oppression must be removed and it is not permissible to affirm it, so do not be deceived by the apparent wording. And by my life, it is a good reminder, and for this they did not mention recourse against the owner of the property.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of kafala (suretyship) for taxes (al-jibayat) imposed by the ruler and for the ransom of a captive. The author, Ibn Nujaym, presents a detailed discussion of the differing positions within the Hanafi school. The passage begins by defining kafala as joinder in demand (ضم في المطالبة), then moves to the issue of kafala for taxes. There are two main opinions: one considers it valid because taxes are demanded physically (مطالب بها حساً) and thus akin to a valid debt; the other considers it invalid because taxes are often oppressive (ظلم) and validating the guarantee would legitimize injustice. The author cites al-Siyar regarding the captive case, where istihsan allows recourse for the ransomer against the captive by analogy to spending on one's family or building. However, Qadi Khan opposes this for taxes, stating the correct view is that guarantee is not valid. The commentary then examines the wording of the matn, and the author concludes that the relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) is that of Sahib al-Muhit, who holds that the guarantor has no recourse, thus invalidating kafala for taxes. Nevertheless, the author notes that there are two sound opinions (قولان مصححان) and that the view of Qadi Khan (validity) is not to be dismissed, but the fatwa is on non-recourse. The passage ends with a practical note about paying oppressive taxes in later times, advising that it is better to avoid it if possible.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafala): Contract of suretyship: joining in demand for presence or payment
الجبايات (al-jibayat): Taxes or levies imposed by the ruler (often oppressive)
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases
النوائب (al-nawa'ib): Levies or incidental taxes; often synonymous with jibayat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in this passage, what is the ruling on kafala for oppressive taxes (al-jibayat)?
A) It is valid and the guarantor has recourse against the principal.
B) It is invalid and the guarantor has no recourse.
C) It is valid only if the guarantor stipulates recourse.
D) It is invalid but the guarantor can still demand payment from the principal.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that kafala for oppressive taxes is invalid, and the guarantor has no recourse, to avoid strengthening injustice.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Kafalah for Taxes and the Meaning of Al-Qismah
Commentary on kafalah for taxes and the meaning of al-qismah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of kafalah for taxes and explain the dispute over the meaning of 'al-qismah' in the source text.

Core Idea
Kafalah for taxes is valid but recourse depends on authorization; 'qismah' means a fixed share, not division.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the validity of suretyship (kafalah) for taxes (nawa'ib) and the correct interpretation of the word 'al-qismah' in the matn. The author reviews multiple Hanafi positions, including a Quranic proof for the meaning of 'qismah', and concludes with an intra-madhhab dispute regarding the surety's right of recourse. The lesson clarifies the mufta bihi view and provides ethical guidance on dealing with unjust taxes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling
Start with the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if a tax collector forcibly takes from a sharecropper (al-akkar), the sharecropper can have recourse against the landowner. This establishes that the surety (kafil) may recover from the principal if the tax was taken unjustly.
The base ruling assumes the tax was taken by force; it does not apply to voluntary payments.
2
Analyze the Dispute over 'Al-Qismah'
Scholars differed on whether 'al-qismah' refers to the taxes themselves, a fixed share, or the act of division. Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id rejected the term as a mistake, while others cited Quran 54:28 where 'qismah' means 'share'. This linguistic debate affects whether kafalah for 'qismah' is valid (if it is an action, it cannot be guaranteed).
The narration with 'aw' suggests it is a specific category of taxes.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Recourse
Two main views: Qadi Khan held that the surety can recover if the kafalah was by the principal's command. The author of al-Muhit held that there is no recourse unless specific conditions (like the word 'ani' or 'ala') are met. The latter is considered the most correct and is mufta bihi.
The dispute does not affect the validity of kafalah itself, only the right of recourse.
4
Apply the Mufta Bihi and Ethical Guidance
The relied-upon view is that the surety has no recourse. However, the passage ends with a strong ethical warning: one should not help the unjust tax collector; instead, if one must pay, it is better to give to the poor who cannot repel injustice, as an act of charity.
This balances legal validity with moral responsibility.

أخذت من ويرجع الأكار فله الرجوع على مالك الأرض .اهـ. وفي الخانية : الصحيح الصحة على المكفول عنه إن كان بأمره وأما القسمة فقد قيل هي النوائب بعينها أو حصة منها والرواية بأو . وقيل : هي النائبة الموظفة الراتبة والمراد بالنوائب ما ينوبه عن راتب؛ كذا في الهداية. والحاصل أن المشايخ اختلفوا في معناه فأبو بكر بن سعيد ادعى أن هذه الكلمة غلط لأن القسمة مصدر والمصدر فعل وهذا الفعل غير مضمون ورد بأن القسمة تجيء بمعنى النصيب قال الله تعالى ونبئهم أن الماء قسمة بينهم ﴾ [القمر: ۲۸] والمراد النصيب. والفقيه أبو جعفر الهندواني قال : معناها أن أحد الشريكين إذا طلب القسمة من صاحبه وامتنع الآخر ذلك ضمن إنساناً ليقوم مقامه في القسمة جاز لأن القسمة واجبة عليه. وقال بعضهم : عن معناها إذا اقتسما ثم منع أحد الشريكين قسم صاحبه فتكون الرواية على هذا قسمة بالضمير لا بالتاء، وقد علمت أن القسمة بالتاء تجيء بمعنى القسم بلا تاء وقيل : هي النوائب بعينها فالعطف للبيان والتفسير . وقيل : ما يخص الرجل منها ولكنه كان ينبغي أن يعطف بالواو لا بأو ليكون من عطف الخاص على العام . وقيل : هي النائبة الموظفة الديوانية كل شهر أو ثلاثة أشهر والنوائب غير الراتبه ؛ كذا في العناية . ثم من أصحابنا من قال : الأفضل للإنسان أن يساوي أهل محلته في إعطاء النائبة قال شمس الأئمة هذا كان في ذلك الزمان لأنه إعانة على الحاجة والجهاد، وأما في زماننا فأكثر النوائب تؤخذ ظلماً، ومن تمكن دفع المظلمة عن نفسه فهو خير له، وإذا أراد الإعطاء فليعط من هو عاجز عن دفع الظلم عن نفسه لفقر ليستعين به الفقير على الظلم وينال المعطي الثواب؛ كذا في فتح القدير .

Book of Suretyship. It was taken from and the plowman has recourse to the landowner. End. And in al-Khaniyah: the correct is validity against the principal if with his command. As for al-qismah, it was said: it is the taxes themselves or a portion thereof, and the narration is with 'aw'. And it was said: it is the fixed permanent tax, and the meaning of nawa'ib is what befalls him regarding salary; thus in al-Hidayah. The summary: the mashayikh differed in its meaning. Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id claimed that this word is a mistake because qismah is a verbal noun and the verbal noun is an action, and this action is not guaranteed. It was rejected by saying qismah comes in the sense of share. Allah said: 'And inform them that the water is a qismah between them' [Qamar:28], meaning share. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani said: its meaning is that if one partner demands division from his partner and the other refuses, then he makes a surety for someone to stand in his place in the division, it is valid because division is obligatory upon him. And some said: its meaning is if they divided then one of the partners prevents the partner's portion, so the narration with a damir and not with ta'. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding kafalah for taxes (nawa'ib). The author begins by quoting a statement: 'Even if it is taken from the plowman, he has recourse against the landowner.' This is the basic ruling: if a tax collector forcibly takes from the sharecropper (al-akkar), the sharecropper can recover from the principal (the landowner). The commentary then delves into the meaning of 'al-qismah' which appears in the matn. Several interpretations are given by earlier scholars: some say it refers to the taxes themselves, others say it is a fixed regular tax, others say it is the fee of the divider (qassam). The author mentions a dispute among Hanafi scholars regarding the validity of kafalah for such taxes. Some, like Qadi Khan, held that the suretyship is valid and the surety can recover if authorized. Others, like the author of al-Muhit, held that the surety has no recourse because the conditions for valid recourse are not met. The author notes that the latter is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). However, he also quotes later scholars who advise paying the tax only to help the poor oppressed, not to support tyranny. The passage concludes with a warning against being deceived by the outward meaning of texts that might be used to justify supporting injustice.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; the legal guarantee of a debt or obligation where the surety (kafil) is liable for the principal's debt or obligation.
القسمة (al-qismah): In this context, refers to the taxes or levies imposed by the state or unjust rulers; also interpreted as a share or portion.
النوائب (al-nawa'ib): Taxes or levies that befall a person; in this text, refers to unjust impositions or regular dues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the passage, what condition must be met for a surety to have recourse against the principal in kafalah for taxes?
A) The tax must be just.
B) The surety must have used specific wording like 'ani' or 'ala'.
C) The principal must be a landowner.
D) The tax must be paid in installments.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for taxes is valid, but the surety's right of recourse depends on explicit authorization and wording; the mufta bihi view denies automatic recourse to prevent aiding injustice.

Dispute over Term of Suretyship
Ruling on dispute over term in suretyship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts the surety's word in a dispute over the term of suretyship and distinguish this from an ordinary acknowledgment of debt.

Core Idea
In suretyship, the surety's word prevails because he has not admitted a debt—only a future claim.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. When a surety guarantees a debt with a deferred term and the creditor disputes the term, the surety's word is taken. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this rule, the critical distinction from ordinary debt acknowledgment, and the inter-madhhab difference with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The scenario: surety says he guaranteed 100 due in one month; creditor says it is due now. The ruling: the surety's word is accepted (القول للضامن).
This is the madhab's position.
2
Trace the reasoning: no admission of debt
The surety has not admitted to a present debt; his statement only acknowledges a future claim after the month. He has not admitted any debt upon himself.
The commentator says: لأنه لم يقر بالدين لأنه لا دين عليه في الصحيح.
3
Distinguish from ordinary debt acknowledgment
If a debtor admits a debt with a term and the creditor disputes, the debtor's word is not accepted because he admitted the debt and then claimed a benefit (delay). In suretyship, the surety did not admit the debt.
The passage provides two differences (فرقان).
4
Note the ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf
Al-Shafi'i holds that suretyship makes the debt immediately binding on the surety; Abu Yusuf opposes this. The author mentions they have explained the difference elsewhere.
This dispute affects whether the surety's word is taken.
5
Understand the hila (legal trick) for a debtor with deferred debt
A debtor with a deferred debt who is sued and fears lying or immediate liability can ask the plaintiff: 'Is it due now or deferred?' If the plaintiff says deferred, no claim now; if due now, the debtor truthfully denies.
This is a permissible device to avoid sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح ومن قال لآخر ضمنت لك عن فلان مائة إلى شهر فقال هي حالة فالقول للضامن
قوله : (ومن قال لآخر ضمنت لك عن فلان مائة إلى شهر فقال هي حالة فالقول للضامن لأنه لم يقر بالدين لأنه لا دين عليه في الصحيح إنما أقر بمجرد المطالبة بعد الشهر. قيد بالضمان لأنه لو أقر بمائة إلى شهر وقال المقر له هي حالة فالقول للمقر له لأن المقر أقر بالدين ثم ادعى حقاً لنفسه وهو تأخير المطالبة إلى أجل وهذا هو الفرق. وفرق آخر أن الأجل في الدين عارض حتى لا يثبت إلا بشرط فكان القول قول من أنكر الشرط كما في الخيار. وأما الكفالة فالأجل في فنوع حتى يثبت من غير شرط بأن كان مؤجلاً على الأصيل، والشافعي الحق الدين بالكفالة، وأبو يوسف عكسه، والفرق قد أوضحناه . وذكر الشارح : والحيلة فيها إذا كان عليه دين مؤجل وادعى عليه وخاف الكذب إن أنكر والمؤاخذة في الحال إن أقر أن يقول هذا للمدعي الذي تدعيه من المال حال أم مؤجل ؛ فإن قال مؤجلاً فلا دعوى عليه في الحال، وإن قال حال فينكره وهو صدوق فلا حرج عليه . وقيل من عليه الدين مؤجل إذا أنكر الدين وقال ليس له قبلي حق فلا بأس به إذا لم يرد به إتواء حقه ا هـ . .
Correct. And whoever said to another: 'I guarantee you against so-and-so for one hundred until a month' and the other said: 'It is due now', then the word is that of the surety. His statement: (And whoever said to another: 'I guarantee you against so-and-so for one hundred until a month' and the other said: 'It is due now', then the word is that of the surety because he has not admitted the debt, for there is no debt upon him in the correct view; he only admitted a mere claim after the month. It is qualified by suretyship because if he admitted one hundred until a month and the one in whose favor it is said it is due, then the word is that of the one in whose favor it is because the admitter admitted the debt then claimed a right for himself, which is delaying the claim to a term, and this is the difference. Another difference is that the term in debt is incidental, not established except by condition, so the word is that of the one who denies the condition, as in the option. As for suretyship, the term is of a type such that it is established without condition, as when it is deferred upon the principal. Al-Shafi'i made the debt binding through suretyship, but Abu Yusuf made it contrary, and the difference has been explained by us. The commentator mentioned: the trick in this, if one has a deferred debt and is sued, and fears lying if he denies, and punishment at present if he admits, he says to the plaintiff: 'What you claim, of money, is it due now or deferred?' If he says deferred, then there is no lawsuit against him at present. If he says due now, then he denies it, and he is truthful, so no harm upon him. And it was said: the one with a deferred debt, if he denies the debt and says 'there is no right of yours upon me', there is no harm in it if he does not intend to annul his right. End.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn: if a surety says 'I guarantee for you against so-and-so, one hundred until a month' and the creditor says it is due now (i.e., no term), the surety's word is accepted. The commentator explains the reasoning: the surety has not admitted to a debt; he only admitted that the creditor has a claim after the month. So his acknowledgment is conditional. The commentary distinguishes this from an ordinary acknowledgment of debt, where if a debtor admits a debt with a term and the creditor disputes the term, the creditor's word is accepted because the debtor admitted the debt and then claimed a benefit (delay). In suretyship, the debt is not admitted from the start. The author also mentions a difference between Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i regarding whether the debt is established by suretyship. The passage ends with a permissible trick (hila) for a debtor who has a deferred debt and is sued: he can ask the plaintiff whether the claim is due now or deferred, and then act accordingly.

Key Terms
الضامن (al-damin): The surety or guarantor who undertakes to pay the debt if the principal fails.
الأجل (al-ajal): The deferred term or period until which the debt is payable.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal device or trick to achieve a permissible outcome without violating the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In suretyship, the surety's word is taken in a dispute over the term because he has not admitted a debt—only a future claim.

Suretyship for Darak: When Can the Buyer Claim from the Surety?
Ruling on when the buyer can claim from surety for darak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the buyer's right to claim from the surety for darak in a sale of a slave-girl, and distinguish between a claim of ownership and adjudication of freedom.

Core Idea
The buyer cannot claim from the surety until the court orders the seller to return the price.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the conditions under which a buyer can seek recourse from a surety (kafalah bi al-darak). The ruling hinges on whether the sale remains valid despite a third-party claim. The author distinguishes mere claim from adjudication of freedom, and explains the underlying rationale. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly applying suretyship in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the suretyship
The buyer purchases a slave-girl, and a third party provides suretyship for the darak (guarantee against legal claims). Assume the slave-girl is later claimed by someone else.
Darak is a form of kafalah that obligates the surety to return the price if the sale is invalidated.
2
Understand the condition for claiming from the surety
According to the matn, the buyer cannot claim from the surety until the price is adjudged against the seller by a judge. Mere claim does not trigger the surety's obligation.
The sale remains valid under zahir al-riwayah until the judge orders the return of the price.
3
Examine the proof: why the claim does not invalidate the sale
The author states that the claim (istihqaq) does not invalidate the sale on the apparent narration. Therefore, the seller is not yet obligated to return the price, so the surety is not liable.
This is based on the principle that a sale is not voided by a mere third-party claim; only an adjudication of ownership or freedom voids it.
4
Contrast with adjudication of freedom
If the slave-girl is adjudged free, the sale is void ab initio because a free person is not a valid object of sale. In that case, the buyer can immediately claim from both the seller and the surety.
The distinction is crucial: freedom destroys the legal competence (mahall) of the sale.
5
Analyze the discussion about the claimant's ratification and the benefit of suretyship
The passage notes that the claimant can ratify the sale after the judge's ruling, and that suretyship in unjust cases actually removes greater harm (like imprisonment or usury) rather than endorsing injustice.
This addresses a potential objection about the surety supporting injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى أمة وكفل له رجل بالدرك فاستحقت لم يأخذ المشتري الكفيل حتى يقضى له بالثمن على البائع)
قوله : (ومن اشترى أمة وكفل له رجل بالدرك فاستحقت لم يأخذ المشتري الكفيل حتى يقضى له بالثمن على البائع) لأنه الاستحقاق لا ينتقض البيع على ظاهر الرواية ما لم يقض له بالثمن على البائع فلم يجب له على الأصيل رد الثمن فلا يجب على الكفيل بخلاف القضاء بالحرية لأن البيع يبطل بها لعدم المحلية ويرجع على البائع والكفيل، ولذا قيد بالاستحقاق أي لغير البائع. أشار المؤلف إلى أن البيع لا ينتقض بقضاء القاضي للمستحق بالعين حتى لو كان الثمن عبداً فأعتقه بائع الجارية بعد حكم القاضي للمستحق نفذ إعتاقه ؛ كذا في العناية. وصحح في فصول الاستروشني أن للمستحق أن يجيز بعد قضاء القاضي وبعد قبضه قبل أن يرجع المشتري على بائعه بالثمن، والرجوع بمجرد الكفيل، وكيف يسوغ القول برجوع المكفول له الظالم و به اندفع ما مر عن الرملي من قوله «والعلة له الخ» لأن ذاك مسلم لو قلنا برجوع الظالم على الكفيل، أما على ما قلنا فليس فيه تقرير الظلم بل فيه رفعه لأنه لولا الكفيل يحبس الظالم المكفول ويضربه ويبيع عليه ماله وعباره بثمن بخس أو يلجئه إلى بيعه أو الاستدانة بالمرابحة ونحو ذلك مما هو مشاهد، وبالكفالة يرتفع كل ذلك والله تعالى أعلم. فهذا ما ظهر للفهم القاصر فتدبره قوله : (وصحح في فصول الأستروشني أن للمستحق أن يجيز الخ) قال الرملي : هذا صريح في
And whoever buys a slave-girl and a man becomes surety for him for the darak, and she is found to be claimed, the buyer does not take the surety until the price is adjudged against the seller. His statement: (And whoever buys a slave-girl and a man becomes surety for him for the darak, and she is found to be claimed, the buyer does not take the surety until the price is adjudged against the seller) because the claim does not invalidate the sale according to the apparent narration as long as he has not been adjudged the price against the seller, so the return of the price is not obligatory upon the principal, so it is not obligatory upon the surety. In contrast to the adjudication of freedom, because the sale becomes invalid there for lack of legal competence, and he has recourse against the seller and the surety. For this reason it is qualified by 'claim', i.e., other than the seller. The author indicated that the sale is not invalidated by the judge's ruling for the claimant for the asset, even if the price was a slave and the seller of the slave-girl manumitted him after the judge's ruling for the claimant, his manumission is effective; thus in al-Inayah. And it was corrected in Fusul al-Ustruwshani that the claimant has the right to give consent after the judge's ruling and after his taking possession before the buyer has recourse against his seller for the price, and the recourse of the surety? And how can it be permissible to say that the unjust claimant has recourse? And thereby refutes what was mentioned from al-Ramli of his statement 'the cause for him etc.' because that is accepted if we say the unjust person has recourse against the surety. But according to what we say, there is no affirmation of injustice but rather its removal, because if not for the surety, the unjust person would imprison the principal, beat him, sell his property and goods for a paltry price, or compel him to sell or borrow with interest and the like, which is witnessed. By the suretyship all that is removed, and Allah knows best. This is what appeared to the deficient understanding, so contemplate it. His statement: (And it was corrected in Fusul al-Ustruwshani that the claimant has the right to give consent after the judge's ruling...) He said: al-Ramli said: this is explicit in...
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a surety (kafalah bi al-darak) in the sale of a slave-girl. According to the matn, if a buyer buys a slave-girl and someone provides suretyship for the darak (guarantee against legal claims), and the slave-girl is claimed by a third party (e.g., she is not the seller's property), the buyer cannot claim from the surety until the price is adjudged against the seller. The reason is that the mere claim (istihqaq) does not invalidate the sale per the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) until the judge orders the seller to return the price. Only then does the seller become obligated to return the price, and consequently the surety's obligation activates. The author distinguishes this from a case where the slave-girl is adjudged free, because that destroys the sale (since a free person cannot be sold), so the buyer has immediate recourse against both seller and surety. The passage also discusses a situation where the claimant can ratify the sale after the judge's ruling, and it addresses the objection that allowing recourse against the surety might support injustice. The commentator argues that kafalah in unjust situations actually removes greater harm, because without the surety the claimant might resort to violence or usury.

Key Terms
الدرك (al-darak): A guarantee against defects or legal claims; specifically in this context, a suretyship that protects the buyer if the item is claimed by a third party.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): The claim of ownership by a third party; the legal assertion that the sold item belongs to someone else.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known narrations transmitted in the six core books of Imam Muhammad (the Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately when the slave-girl is claimed by a third party.
B) Only after the judge adjudges the price against the seller.
C) After the seller admits the claim.
D) Only after the buyer pays the price.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's right to claim from the surety for darak is conditional on a judgment against the seller, because a mere claim does not invalidate the sale in Hanafi law.

Guarantor's Word on the Term of Debt: Distinguishing Debtor's Acknowledgment and Guarantee
Ruling on guarantor's word vs creditor's word regarding payment term in guarantee contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the guarantor's word regarding the term of a debt and distinguish it from the debtor's acknowledgment, while also explaining the positions of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The guarantor's word is taken on the term because he did not acknowledge a debt—a key distinction from debtor's acknowledgment.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with a common dispute: a guarantor claims the guaranteed debt was deferred, while the creditor claims it is immediate. The Hanafi school gives a nuanced ruling favoring the guarantor, drawing a critical distinction between a debtor's acknowledgment (iqrar) and a guarantor's undertaking. The passage also clarifies the nature of the term in debt versus guarantee, and touches on the views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, culminating in a legal stratagem for those facing unjust claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
Recognize the case: A guarantor undertakes to pay a debt from the principal (asil) to the creditor, stating a term (e.g., one month). The creditor insists the debt is immediate. Determine who is believed.
Focus on the distinction between the guarantor's statement and the debtor's acknowledgment.
2
Distinguish Debtor's Iqrar from Guarantor's Undertaking
Understand that when a debtor acknowledges a debt and later claims a term, his word is not taken because he admitted the entire debt and then asserted an additional right (delay). In contrast, the guarantor never acknowledged a debt on himself; he only conceded potential liability after the term.
This is the core distinction: the guarantor's iqrar is limited to a future demand, not a present debt.
3
Analyze the Nature of the Term in Debt vs. Guarantee
In a direct debt, the term is an incidental condition (ariyad) that is not established without explicit stipulation. In guarantee, the term is inherent (naw'), so it is established without condition if the original debt was deferred.
This explains why the guarantor's word is taken: he denies a condition that is not part of the guarantee's default nature.
4
Compare Three Positions: Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: term is inherent in guarantee, so it follows the original debt without stipulation. Abu Yusuf: guarantee is a new contract; term must be stipulated, so if disputed, immediate. Al-Shafi'i: guarantee is attached to the debt, so it picks up the original term.
The Hanafi mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Apply the Legal Stratagem (Hila)
If a debtor with a deferred debt is sued, he can ask the plaintiff: 'Is your claim immediate or deferred?' If plaintiff says deferred, no immediate liability. If immediate, the debtor can deny it truthfully.
This hila is based on the distinction between the debtor's and guarantor's positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح ومن قال لآخر ضمنت لك عن فلان مائة إلى شهر فقال هي حالة فالقول للضامن لأنه لم يقر بالدين لأنه لا دين عليه في الصحيح إنما أقر بمجرد المطالبة بعد الشهر. قيد بالضمان لأنه لو أقر بمائة إلى شهر وقال المقر له هي حالة فالقول للمقر له لأن المقر أقر بالدين ثم ادعى حقاً لنفسه وهو تأخير المطالبة إلى أجل وهذا هو الفرق. وفرق آخر أن الأجل في الدين عارض حتى لا يثبت إلا بشرط فكان القول قول من أنكر الشرط كما في الخيار. وأما الكفالة فالأجل فيها نوع حتى يثبت من غير شرط بأن كان مؤجلاً على الأصيل، والشافعي الحق الدين بالكفالة، وأبو يوسف عكسه، والفرق قد أوضحناه. وذكر الشارح: والحيلة فيها إذا كان عليه دين مؤجل وادعى عليه وخاف الكذب إن أنكر والمؤاخذة في الحال إن أقر أن يقول هذا للمدعي: الذي تدعيه من المال حال أم مؤجل؟ فإن قال مؤجلاً فلا دعوى عليه في الحال، وإن قال حال فينكره وهو صدوق فلا حرج عليه. وقيل من عليه الدين مؤجل إذا أنكر الدين وقال ليس له قبلي حق فلا بأس به إذا لم يرد به إتواء حقه ا هـ.
Correct: And whoever said to another, 'I guarantee for you from so-and-so a hundred until a month,' and he said, 'It is immediate,' then the word is of the guarantor, because he did not acknowledge a debt, since there is no debt upon him in the correct position; he only acknowledged mere demand after the month. It is restricted by guarantee, because if he acknowledged a hundred until a month and the acknowledged one said it is immediate, then the word is of the acknowledged one, because the acknowledger acknowledged the debt and then claimed a right for himself, which is postponement of demand until a term, and this is the difference. Another difference: The term in debt is incidental, not established except by condition, so the word is of the one who denies the condition, as in the option. As for guarantee, the term is an inherent type, so it is established without condition, if it was deferred on the principal. Al-Shafi'i attached the debt to the guarantee, and Abu Yusuf reversed it, and we have clarified the difference. And the commentator mentioned: The stratagem in it: if he has a deferred debt and he is sued, and he fears lying if he denies, and immediate liability if he acknowledges, he should say to the plaintiff: 'What you claim of the money, is it immediate or deferred?' If he says deferred, then there is no claim against him immediately. If he says immediate, then he denies it, and if he is truthful, there is no sin upon him. And it was said: The one upon whom is a deferred debt, if he denies the debt and says 'I have no right due to him,' there is no harm in it if he does not intend thereby to delay his due right. End.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on a case of guarantee with a time limit. The text states that if a guarantor says 'I guarantee a hundred dirhams from so-and-so due in a month,' and the creditor says it is due immediately, the word of the guarantor is taken. The reason: the guarantor has not acknowledged a debt on himself; he merely acknowledged that the creditor has a right to demand payment after the month. If he had acknowledged a debt himself (e.g., 'I owe you a hundred due in a month'), then the word would be of the creditor if they dispute the term, because the debtor admitted the debt and then claimed a right to delay, which is a claim against the apparent. Another distinction: in a debt, the term is an incidental condition, so the one who denies it is believed; in guarantee, the term is inherent to the guarantee if the original debt was deferred. The text then notes that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the guarantee attaches the same term as the original debt (so if the original debt was deferred, the guarantee is also deferred), while Imam Abu Yusuf reverses this (holding that guarantee is immediate unless stipulated). The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the term in guarantee is effective without stipulation if the original debt was deferred. A legal stratagem (hila) is given for a person who owes a deferred debt and is sued wrongfully: he can ask the plaintiff whether the claim is immediate or deferred. If the plaintiff says deferred, there is no immediate claim; if immediate, the debtor can deny it truthfully without sin. Some scholars say that if the debtor denies the debt entirely, it is permissible if not intended to delay payment.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or suretyship; a contract where one person undertakes to be liable for a debt or obligation of another.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right due to another; an admission that creates a legal obligation.
أجل (ajal): A fixed time or deadline for payment of a debt or performance of an obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor says 'I guarantee for you from Zayd a hundred dirhams due in a month,' and the creditor says it is immediately due, whose word is accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) The word of the creditor, because he is the one to whom the debt is owed.
B) The word of the guarantor, because he did not acknowledge a debt on himself.
C) The word of the original debtor (Zayd), because he is the principal.
D) The word of both, and the matter is settled by compromise.

Key Takeaway
In guarantee, the term is inherent and follows the original debt; the guarantor's word is taken because he did not acknowledge a debt on himself.
